# Chapter 1: SAT Writing Foundations — Test Architecture, Scoring, and Strategic Framework

---

## 1.1 The Digital SAT: A Paradigm Shift in Standardized Testing

The SAT underwent a monumental transformation in March 2024, transitioning from a paper-based, linear test to a fully digital, adaptive examination. This shift fundamentally altered not only the medium through which students interact with the test but also the very architecture of how questions are delivered, how difficulty is calibrated, and how scores are ultimately computed. To master the SAT Writing and Language section — now officially called the "Reading and Writing" section — a student must first understand the structural DNA of the exam itself. Without this foundational knowledge, even the most grammatically proficient test-taker can fall victim to pacing errors, misallocated effort, and strategic missteps that cost valuable points.

The digital SAT is administered through the College Board's proprietary **Bluebook** application, which runs on laptops, tablets, and school-managed Chromebooks. The test is no longer a monolithic, fixed-form exam where every student sees the same questions in the same order. Instead, it employs a **multistage adaptive design**, meaning the test responds to your performance in real time. This adaptive mechanism is the single most important structural feature to understand before diving into content review, because it dictates how you should allocate your time, how you should approach the first module, and why a strong performance on Module 1 is the gateway to achieving a top score.

---

## 1.2 The Adaptive Structure: How the Test Responds to You

### 1.2.1 Two-Module Architecture

The Reading and Writing section consists of **two modules**, each containing $27$ questions ($25$ operational questions plus $2$ unscored pretest questions that are indistinguishable from scored items). Each module is allotted **32 minutes**, for a total section time of **64 minutes** and a total of **$54$ questions** ($50$ scored, $4$ unscored).

The critical feature of this two-module structure is that **Module 2 is adaptive based on your performance in Module 1**. Here is how the adaptation works:

- **Module 1 (Routing Module):** Every student sees the same Module 1, which contains a mix of easy, medium, and hard questions. This module serves as a routing mechanism — it determines which version of Module 2 you will receive.

- **Module 2 (Adaptive Module):** Based on your performance in Module 1, you are routed to either an **easier** or **harder** version of Module 2. The harder Module 2 contains more challenging questions but also has a higher scoring ceiling. The easier Module 2 contains more accessible questions but caps your potential score at a lower maximum.

This means that **your performance on Module 1 directly determines the scoring range available to you on the entire Reading and Writing section**. If you are routed to the harder Module 2, you have the opportunity to score in the $700$–$800$ range. If you are routed to the easier Module 2, your maximum possible score is typically capped somewhere in the low-to-mid $600$s. This makes Module 1 disproportionately important — arguably the most consequential **32 minutes** of the entire exam.

### 1.2.2 Why the Harder Module Matters

The adaptive design creates a paradox that every SAT strategist must internalize: **to achieve a top score, you must first demonstrate mastery on Module 1 so that you are routed to the harder Module 2.** The harder module is not just "more difficult" — it is the only path to a score above approximately $650$. Students who are routed to the easier module, even if they answer every single question correctly, will hit a score ceiling that prevents them from reaching the $700+$ range.

This has profound implications for test-day strategy:

1.  **Accuracy on Module 1 is paramount.** Every question in Module 1 carries outsized weight because it determines your routing. A careless error on an easy question in Module 1 can be the difference between the hard and easy Module 2.

2.  **Do not rush through Module 1.** Many students, anxious to "bank time" for later, speed through Module 1 and make preventable errors. This is a catastrophic mistake. The time you "save" by rushing Module 1 is worthless if you are routed to the easier module where those extra minutes cannot compensate for the lower scoring ceiling.

3.  **The harder Module 2 is not impossible.** Students often fear the harder module, but it is designed to be challenging. You are not expected to get every question right. The scoring algorithm accounts for difficulty — a $650$ on the hard module may require fewer correct answers than a $650$ on the easy module would (though the easy module's ceiling prevents reaching that score anyway).

### 1.2.3 The Pretest Questions

Each module contains **$2$ unscored pretest questions** that are embedded among the $27$ total questions. These questions are being field-tested for future exams and do not contribute to your score. However, **you cannot identify which questions are pretest items.** They look, feel, and are positioned identically to scored questions. This means you must treat every question as if it counts — there is no way to "skip" a pretest question, and attempting to guess which questions are unscored is a waste of mental energy.

The presence of pretest questions also means that the content distribution within any given module may not perfectly match the published percentages. One module might have an extra grammar question and one fewer rhetoric question, for example. This is normal and should not cause alarm.

---

## 1.3 Content Domains: The Four Pillars of Reading and Writing

The digital SAT Reading and Writing section is organized into **four content domains**, each testing a distinct set of skills. Understanding these domains — their approximate weightings, the types of questions they generate, and the strategies they require — is essential for efficient preparation.

### 1.3.1 Domain 1: Craft and Structure ($28\%$ of section, $13$–$15$ questions)

**Skills Tested:**
- Vocabulary in context (words in context questions)
- Text structure and purpose (why the author organized the passage a certain way)
- Cross-text connections (comparing two passages on the same topic)
- Understanding the function of a sentence or paragraph within a passage

**What This Looks Like on Test Day:**
You will encounter short passages (typically $25$–$150$ words) followed by a single question. For vocabulary questions, you must determine the meaning of a word or phrase as used in the passage — not its dictionary definition, but its contextual meaning. For text structure questions, you might be asked why the author included a particular paragraph, or what role a specific sentence plays in the argument. For cross-text connections, you will read two short passages and identify points of agreement or disagreement between the authors.

**Strategic Implications:**
Craft and Structure questions reward close reading and the ability to think about *how* a passage works, not just *what* it says. These questions often require you to step back from the details and consider the author's purpose, the rhetorical strategy being employed, and the logical flow of ideas. Vocabulary questions in this domain test sophisticated, often secondary meanings of common words — for example, "temperate" meaning "moderate or restrained" rather than "related to temperature."

### 1.3.2 Domain 2: Information and Ideas ($26\%$ of section, $12$–$14$ questions)

**Skills Tested:**
- Central claims and main ideas
- Making inferences based on the text
- Command of evidence (selecting textual or data-based evidence that supports a claim)
- Interpreting data from charts, graphs, and tables

**What This Looks Like on Test Day:**
You will read a passage and answer questions about its main idea, draw conclusions that are implied but not explicitly stated, or select the piece of evidence from the passage that best supports a given claim. Some questions will present a chart or graph alongside the passage and ask you to interpret the data or connect it to the passage's claims.

**Strategic Implications:**
Information and Ideas questions demand precise, text-based reasoning. The correct answer must be directly supported by the passage — not by your outside knowledge, not by what "seems reasonable," and not by what the author "probably meant." The SAT is fanatical about textual evidence. For data interpretation questions, you must read the graph carefully, noting axes labels, units, and trends, and then connect the data to the specific claim being made in the passage.

### 1.3.3 Domain 3: Standard English Conventions ($26\%$ of section, $11$–$15$ questions)

**Skills Tested:**
- Subject-verb agreement
- Verb tense consistency
- Pronoun-antecedent agreement
- Modifier placement (dangling and misplaced modifiers)
- Parallel structure
- Sentence boundaries (fragments, run-ons, comma splices)
- Punctuation (commas, semicolons, colons, dashes, apostrophes)
- Essential vs. non-essential clauses
- Comparisons and correlative conjunctions

**What This Looks Like on Test Day:**
These are the classic "grammar" questions. A portion of a short passage will be underlined, and you must select the alternative that best expresses the idea according to the conventions of standard written English. The "NO CHANGE" option is available if the original is already correct.

**Strategic Implications:**
Standard English Conventions questions are the most rule-based and, for many students, the most improvable through focused study. Over $80\%$ of these questions test the same $12$–$15$ grammar rules repeatedly. Mastery of these rules — subject-verb agreement, comma usage, pronoun clarity, etc. — can yield dramatic score improvements in a relatively short time. These questions are also the most amenable to a systematic, process-driven approach: identify the rule being tested, eliminate answers that violate the rule, and select the correct option.

### 1.3.4 Domain 4: Expression of Ideas ($20\%$ of section, $8$–$12$ questions)

**Skills Tested:**
- Rhetorical synthesis (combining information from multiple sources to support a claim)
- Transitions (selecting the word or phrase that best connects ideas)
- Concision and clarity (choosing the most effective, concise wording)
- Sentence placement and logical flow
- Add/delete decisions (whether a sentence should be added or removed)

**What This Looks Like on Test Day:**
Transition questions present two sentences with a blank between them and ask you to select the transition word or phrase that best captures the logical relationship. Rhetorical synthesis questions provide bulleted notes or research findings and ask you to select the bullets that best support a given claim. Add/delete questions ask whether a particular sentence should be included in a passage and why.

**Strategic Implications:**
Expression of Ideas questions test your ability to think about writing as a *rhetorical act* — not just whether a sentence is grammatically correct, but whether it is effective, well-placed, and logically connected to its surroundings. These questions require you to read broadly around the question (often several sentences before and after) to understand the context. Transition questions, in particular, reward students who can identify logical relationships (contrast, cause-effect, addition, example, etc.) with precision.

### 1.3.5 Content Domain Summary Table

| Domain | Approximate % | Approximate Question Count | Primary Skills |
|--------|--------------|---------------------------|----------------|
| Craft & Structure | ~$28\%$ | ~$13$–$15$ | Vocabulary in context, text structure, purpose, cross-text connections |
| Information & Ideas | ~$26\%$ | ~$12$–$14$ | Main ideas, inferences, command of evidence, data interpretation |
| Standard English Conventions | ~$26\%$ | ~$11$–$15$ | Grammar, punctuation, sentence structure, modifiers, parallelism |
| Expression of Ideas | ~$20\%$ | ~$8$–$12$ | Transitions, rhetorical synthesis, concision, sentence placement |

**Important Note:** These percentages are approximate and can vary slightly from test to test. The exact distribution is not fixed, and the presence of unscored pretest questions means that any individual module may deviate from these targets. However, over the full section, the distribution will closely approximate these ranges.

---

## 1.4 Scoring Architecture: How Your Raw Performance Becomes a Scaled Score

### 1.4.1 Raw Score to Scaled Score Conversion

The SAT Reading and Writing section is scored on a **scale of $200$ to $800$**. This scaled score is combined with the Math section score (also $200$–$800$) to produce a **total score ranging from $400$ to $1600$**.

Your raw score (the number of questions you answer correctly) is converted to a scaled score through an **equating process** that accounts for the difficulty of the specific test form you took. This means that the same raw score can produce slightly different scaled scores on different test dates, depending on the difficulty of the questions. The equating process ensures that a given scaled score represents the same level of ability regardless of which form of the test you took.

### 1.4.2 The Adaptive Scoring Algorithm

Because the digital SAT is adaptive, the scoring algorithm is more complex than a simple raw-to-scaled conversion. The algorithm considers:

1.  **Which module you were routed to** (harder or easier Module 2)
2.  **How many questions you answered correctly in each module**
3.  **The difficulty level of the specific questions you answered correctly and incorrectly**

This means that **not all correct answers are worth the same number of points.** A correct answer on a hard question in the harder Module 2 is worth more than a correct answer on an easy question in the easier Module 2. Conversely, an incorrect answer on a hard question is penalized less than an incorrect answer on an easy question.

### 1.4.3 No Penalty for Guessing

The SAT has **no penalty for wrong answers.** Your raw score is simply the number of questions you answer correctly — there is no deduction for incorrect answers. This has a critical strategic implication: **you should never leave a question blank.** Even if you have no idea which answer is correct, you should always make your best guess. A random guess gives you a **$25\%$** chance of earning a point; a blank gives you a **$0\%$** chance.

### 1.4.4 Score Benchmarks and Percentiles

Understanding where your score falls relative to other test-takers can help you set realistic goals and measure progress. While exact percentiles shift slightly from year to year, the following benchmarks provide a general framework:

| Scaled Score (R&W) | Approximate Percentile | Interpretation |
|-------------------|----------------------|----------------|
| $200$–$400$ | Bottom $1$st–$5$th | Significant improvement needed |
| $450$–$500$ | $25$th–$40$th | Below average; foundational gaps |
| $550$–$600$ | $45$th–$60$th | Near average; solid foundation |
| $600$–$650$ | $60$th–$75$th | Above average; competitive for many colleges |
| $650$–$700$ | $75$th–$85$th | Strong; competitive for selective colleges |
| $700$–$750$ | $85$th–$95$th | Very strong; competitive for highly selective colleges |
| $750$–$800$ | $95$th–$99$th+ | Exceptional; top-tier performance |

**Note:** These percentiles are approximate and based on recent data. The College Board publishes updated percentile ranks annually.

### 1.4.5 The Importance of the Harder Module for Score Ceilings

As discussed earlier, the adaptive structure creates a **score ceiling** for students routed to the easier Module 2. While the College Board has not published the exact ceiling, analysis of score data suggests that students in the easier module are unlikely to score above approximately **$640$–$660$**, regardless of how many questions they answer correctly. This means that **the single most important factor in achieving a $700+$ Reading and Writing score is performing well enough on Module 1 to be routed to the harder Module 2.**

This has direct implications for how you should prepare:

- **Early preparation should focus heavily on Module 1 content.** Since Module 1 is the same for all students, it is entirely predictable in terms of content domains and question types. Mastering the rules and strategies that appear in Module 1 is the most reliable path to unlocking the harder Module 2.

- **Do not neglect Module 2 preparation, but prioritize Module 1.** Once you are consistently performing well on Module 1 practice sets, shift your focus to the harder Module 2 content, which includes more challenging vocabulary, more complex passages, and more nuanced rhetorical questions.

---

## 1.5 Passage Types and Sources

### 1.5.1 The Digital SAT's Short-Passage Format

Unlike the old paper SAT, which featured four long passages with $11$ questions each, the digital SAT uses a **one-question-per-short-passage** format. Each passage is between **$25$ and $150$ words**, with an average of approximately **$75$ words**. This means you will read and respond to approximately **$54$ short passages** across the two modules.

This format has several important implications:

1.  **Context is compressed.** With only $25$–$150$ words, there is limited room for context. Every word matters. You cannot skim or skip sentences without potentially missing critical information.

2.  **Transitions and logical flow are tested differently.** On the old test, you might be asked about the relationship between paragraphs within a long passage. On the digital test, transition questions often present two sentences and ask you to identify the logical relationship between them, or they present a short passage with a blank and ask you to select the best transition word.

3.  **Genre variety is built into the format.** Because each question has its own passage, the test can seamlessly alternate between literature, science, history, and social science passages. You might see a literature passage followed by a science passage followed by a history passage.

### 1.5.2 Passage Source Categories

The College Board draws passages from four broad categories:

1.  **Literature:** Excerpts from novels, short stories, plays, and poetry. These passages test your ability to interpret figurative language, understand character and tone, and analyze narrative technique. Literature passages are most common in the Craft & Structure domain.

2.  **History / Civics / The "Great Global Conversation":** Excerpts from historical documents, speeches, essays, and foundational texts. The "Great Global Conversation" refers to the ongoing dialogue about freedom, justice, and human dignity that spans centuries and cultures. Passages might include excerpts from the Federalist Papers, speeches by Frederick Douglass or Martin Luther King Jr., or essays by Mary Wollstonecraft or Rabindranath Tagore. These passages are most common in the Information & Ideas and Craft & Structure domains.

3.  **Natural Science:** Excerpts from scientific articles, research summaries, and explanatory texts. These passages cover topics in biology, chemistry, physics, earth science, and astronomy. They often include data in the form of charts or graphs and are most common in the Information & Ideas domain (particularly command of evidence and data interpretation questions).

4.  **Social Science:** Excerpts from psychology, sociology, economics, and anthropology. These passages present research findings, theoretical arguments, and empirical data. They are most common in the Information & Ideas and Craft & Structure domains.

### 1.5.3 Genre-Specific Reading Strategies

Each passage type demands a slightly different reading approach:

- **Literature passages:** Focus on tone, mood, and the author's use of figurative language. Pay attention to what is *not* said — subtext, irony, and implication are frequently tested.

- **History/Civics passages:** Identify the author's central argument and the rhetorical strategies used to support it. Pay attention to the historical context (if provided) and the relationship between the author's claims and the evidence presented.

- **Natural Science passages:** Focus on the research question, methodology, findings, and implications. When data is presented, read the axes, units, and trends carefully before answering the question.

- **Social Science passages:** Similar to natural science — identify the claim, the evidence, and the conclusion. Be alert for the author's assumptions and the limitations of the research.

---

## 1.6 Question Types: A Taxonomy

Understanding the specific question types you will encounter is essential for efficient preparation. Each question type has its own strategic approach, and recognizing the type instantly allows you to deploy the appropriate strategy.

### 1.6.1 Standard English Conventions Question Types

These questions test grammar, punctuation, and sentence structure. The underlined portion of the passage contains an error (or is already correct), and you must select the best alternative.

**Common SEC Question Types:**

| Question Type | What It Tests | Frequency |
|--------------|---------------|-----------|
| Subject-Verb Agreement | Whether the verb matches the subject in number | Very High |
| Comma Usage | Correct placement and omission of commas | Very High |
| Pronoun Agreement & Clarity | Whether pronouns match their antecedents and refer unambiguously | High |
| Verb Tense | Consistency and correctness of verb tense | High |
| Parallel Structure | Whether items in a list or comparison are grammatically parallel | High |
| Sentence Boundaries | Fragments, run-ons, comma splices | High |
| Modifier Placement | Dangling and misplaced modifiers | Moderate |
| Semicolon/Colon/Dash Usage | Correct use of advanced punctuation | Moderate |
| Essential vs. Non-Essential | Whether a clause is restrictive or nonrestrictive | Moderate |
| Apostrophe Usage | Possessives vs. contractions | Moderate |
| Comparisons | Logical and grammatical comparisons | Lower but tested |

### 1.6.2 Expression of Ideas Question Types

These questions test your ability to improve the rhetorical effectiveness of a passage.

**Common EOI Question Types:**

| Question Type | What It Tests | Frequency |
|--------------|---------------|-----------|
| Transitions | Selecting the word/phrase that best connects ideas | High |
| Rhetorical Synthesis | Using bulleted research to support a claim | High |
| Add/Delete | Whether a sentence should be added or removed | Moderate |
| Sentence Placement | Where a sentence should be placed in a passage | Moderate |
| Concision | Choosing the most concise correct option | Moderate |
| Sentence Combining | Merging two sentences effectively | Lower but tested |

### 1.6.3 Craft and Structure Question Types

These questions test your ability to understand and analyze how a passage works.

**Common C&S Question Types:**

| Question Type | What It Tests | Frequency |
|--------------|---------------|-----------|
| Words in Context | Meaning of a word/phrase as used in the passage | High |
| Text Structure & Purpose | Why the author organized the passage a certain way | Moderate |
| Cross-Text Connections | Comparing two passages on the same topic | Moderate |
| Function/Purpose | What a specific sentence or paragraph does | Moderate |

### 1.6.4 Information and Ideas Question Types

These questions test your ability to comprehend, analyze, and draw conclusions from passages and data.

**Common I&I Question Types:**

| Question Type | What It Tests | Frequency |
|--------------|---------------|-----------|
| Central Idea/Claim | Identifying the main idea of a passage | High |
| Command of Evidence (Textual) | Selecting textual evidence that supports a claim | High |
| Command of Evidence (Quantitative) | Selecting data from a chart/graph that supports a claim | High |
| Inferences | Drawing conclusions supported by the passage | Moderate |
| Data Interpretation | Reading and interpreting charts, graphs, and tables | Moderate |

---

## 1.7 Strategic Framework: The Predict → Prove Method

The single most powerful strategy for the SAT Reading and Writing section is the **Predict → Prove Method.** This method, when practiced consistently, transforms the test from a guessing game into a systematic process of elimination and confirmation.

### 1.7.1 The Four Steps of Predict → Prove

**Step 1: Read the Task**
Before looking at the passage or the answer choices, read the question carefully. For Standard English Conventions questions, there is no explicit question — the task is implied by the underlined portion. For Expression of Ideas questions, the task is often stated explicitly (e.g., "Which transition word best connects the two sentences?"). For Craft and Structure and Information and Ideas questions, the question itself tells you what to look for.

**Step 2: Predict What the Answer Should Be**
Before looking at the answer choices, formulate your own answer based on your reading of the passage and your knowledge of the relevant rule or concept. This is the most critical step. By predicting first, you avoid being seduced by trap answers that sound plausible but are incorrect.

- For grammar questions: Identify the rule being tested, then predict what the correct application of that rule would look like.
- For transition questions: Read the sentences before and after the blank, determine the logical relationship (contrast, addition, cause-effect, etc.), and predict a transition that fits.
- For vocabulary questions: Read the sentence, determine the contextual meaning of the target word, and predict a synonym.
- For evidence questions: Read the claim, then scan the passage for the specific evidence that directly supports it.

**Step 3: Prove Your Prediction**
Once you have a prediction, look at the answer choices and find the one that matches. If your prediction matches an answer choice, you can select it with confidence. If none of the choices match your prediction, re-evaluate — either your prediction was wrong, or you need to refine it.

**Step 4: Eliminate and Confirm**
If you cannot make a clear prediction, use elimination. Cross out answers that are clearly wrong based on the rule being tested. Then, from the remaining options, select the one that best fits the passage and the rule.

### 1.7.2 Why Predict → Prove Works

The SAT is designed with **trap answers** — incorrect options that are deliberately crafted to appeal to common mistakes, careless reading, or intuitive but incorrect reasoning. If you read the answer choices before forming your own prediction, you are far more likely to be drawn to a trap answer. The Predict → Prove method forces you to think independently before being influenced by the test's distractions.

Consider this example:

*The researcher's findings were \_\_\_\_\_\_ significant, suggesting that the new treatment could revolutionize patient care.*

A) profoundly
B) marginally
C) tentatively
D) superficially

If you read the answer choices first, all four words could "fit" grammatically. But if you predict first — noting that "revolutionize" implies a major, transformative impact — you would predict a word meaning "very" or "deeply." This leads you directly to **A) profoundly**, without being distracted by the other options.

### 1.7.3 When Predict → Prove Is Most Essential

The Predict → Prove method is most critical for:

- **Transition questions** (where trap answers exploit logical relationships that are close but not quite right)
- **Vocabulary in context questions** (where trap answers use common but contextually incorrect meanings)
- **Command of evidence questions** (where trap answers mention the topic but don't directly support the claim)
- **Grammar questions where multiple answers "sound right"** (where trap answers exploit the gap between conversational and formal English)

---

## 1.8 The Two-Pacing Strategy: First Pass and Second Pass

### 1.8.1 Why You Need a Pacing Strategy

Each module gives you **32 minutes for 27 questions**, which works out to approximately **$70$ seconds per question.** However, not all questions require the same amount of time. A straightforward subject-verb agreement question might take $20$ seconds, while a complex rhetorical synthesis question might take $90$ seconds. If you spend exactly $70$ seconds on every question, you will either rush through easy questions (making careless errors) or run out of time on hard questions (leaving points on the table).

The solution is a **two-pass strategy:**

### 1.8.2 First Pass: Bank the Easy Points

On your first pass through the module, cap each question at approximately **$45$ seconds.** If you can answer a question within that time, do so and move on. If a question is taking longer than $45$ seconds, **mark it and move on.** The goal of the first pass is to secure all the "easy" and "medium" points — the questions where you are confident in your answer — without getting bogged down by difficult or time-consuming items.

**Key principles for the first pass:**

- **Do not sacrifice accuracy for speed on Module 1.** Remember, Module 1 determines your routing. A careless error on an easy question is far more costly than taking an extra $15$ seconds to get it right.
- **Trust your instincts.** If you know the answer, select it and move on. Do not second-guess yourself on the first pass.
- **Mark questions you skip.** Use the Bluebook app's flagging tool to mark questions you want to return to.

### 1.8.3 Second Pass: Tackle the Hard Questions

After completing the first pass, return to the questions you marked. Now you can spend the remaining time on these harder questions without the anxiety of knowing you haven't answered the easier ones. On the second pass:

- **Re-read the question and passage more carefully.** Sometimes a second reading reveals a clue you missed the first time.
- **Use elimination aggressively.** If you can't determine the correct answer, eliminate as many wrong answers as possible and make an educated guess.
- **Never leave a question blank.** If time is running out, guess on any remaining unanswered questions.

### 1.8.4 Pacing Benchmarks

To stay on track, use these internal benchmarks:

| Time Elapsed | Questions Completed (Approximate) |
|-------------|----------------------------------|
| $8$ minutes | ~$6$–$7$ questions |
| $16$ minutes | ~$13$–$14$ questions |
| $24$ minutes | ~$20$–$21$ questions |
| $32$ minutes | $27$ questions (complete) |

If you are significantly behind these benchmarks at any point, you need to speed up — either by making quicker decisions on current questions or by flagging more aggressively and moving on.

---

## 1.9 The Error Log: Your Most Powerful Study Tool

### 1.9.1 Why You Need an Error Log

Improvement on the SAT is not just about doing more practice questions — it's about **learning from your mistakes.** An error log is a systematic record of every question you get wrong (or guess on), along with an analysis of why you got it wrong and what you need to do differently next time.

Without an error log, students tend to repeat the same mistakes over and over, falling into the same traps and misapplying the same rules. With an error log, you can identify patterns in your errors, target your weak areas, and track your progress over time.

### 1.9.2 What to Include in Your Error Log

For each missed or guessed question, record the following:

| Field | Description |
|-------|-------------|
| **Date** | When you took the practice test or set |
| **Section/Module** | Reading & Writing, Module 1 or 2 |
| **Question Type** | Subject-verb agreement, transition, vocabulary, etc. |
| **Source** | Bluebook practice test #, Khan Academy, etc. |
| **Your Answer** | The answer you selected |
| **Correct Answer** | The actual correct answer |
| **Error Type** | Why you got it wrong (see below) |
| **Takeaway** | What you learned / what you'll do differently |
| **Reviewed?** | Date you reviewed this error again |

### 1.9.3 Error Type Categories

Categorizing your errors helps you identify patterns. Common error categories include:

1.  **Content Gap:** You didn't know the grammar rule or concept being tested.
2.  **Misread the Question:** You answered a different question than what was asked.
3.  **Careless Error:** You knew the rule but made a silly mistake (e.g., selected the wrong answer despite knowing the correct one).
4.  **Trap Answer:** You were drawn to a deliberately misleading answer choice.
5.  **Time Pressure:** You rushed and made an error you wouldn't have made with more time.
6.  **Vocabulary Gap:** You didn't know the meaning of a word in the passage or answer choices.
7.  **Logical Misstep:** You misidentified the logical relationship between ideas (e.g., chose a contrast transition when the relationship was actually cause-effect).
8.  **Overthought:** You talked yourself out of the correct answer by overanalyzing.

### 1.9.4 How to Use Your Error Log

- **Review your error log weekly.** Look for patterns — are you consistently missing comma questions? Are you falling for trap answers on transition questions? Are you running out of time on Module 2?
- **Target your weak areas.** If your error log reveals that you miss $60\%$ of pronoun agreement questions, dedicate your next study session to pronoun agreement rules and practice.
- **Re-do missed questions.** After reviewing the rule or concept, re-do the missed question from scratch (without looking at the correct answer) to confirm you now understand it.
- **Track your progress.** Over time, you should see the number of errors in each category decrease. If a category isn't improving, you need to adjust your study approach.

---

## 1.10 Common Traps and How to Avoid Them

The SAT is not just a test of knowledge — it's a test of **how well you can avoid being tricked.** The College Board employs several recurring trap patterns that exploit common cognitive biases and careless reading habits. Understanding these traps is as important as understanding the content.

### 1.10.1 The "Sounds Right" Trap

**What it is:** An answer choice that sounds natural in conversational English but violates a formal grammar rule.

**Why it works:** Most students rely on their "ear" — they read the sentence and choose the option that "sounds right." But the SAT tests *formal written English*, which often differs from conversational English. For example, "between you and I" sounds natural to many people but is grammatically incorrect (it should be "between you and me" because "me" is the object of the preposition).

**How to avoid it:** Always identify the specific grammar rule being tested before selecting an answer. If you cannot articulate the rule, you are relying on your ear, which is unreliable.

### 1.10.2 The "Longer Answer" Trap

**What it is:** An answer choice that is longer and more elaborate than necessary, adding redundant or unnecessary information.

**Why it works:** Students often assume that a more detailed answer must be more correct. In reality, the SAT rewards concision. When multiple answers are grammatically correct and convey the same meaning, the shortest option is almost always correct.

**How to avoid it:** Apply the **Concision Test.** If two answer choices are grammatically correct and convey the same meaning, choose the shorter one. Eliminate redundant phrases like "due to the fact that" (use "because"), "in the event that" (use "if"), and "at this point in time" (use "now").

### 1.10.3 The "Near-Noun Agreement" Trap

**What it is:** A subject-verb agreement question where a plural noun appears near the verb, but the actual subject is singular.

**Example:** "The *number* of students who *are* participating in the program *have* increased." The verb "have" is next to the plural noun "program," but the actual subject is "number," which is singular. The correct verb is "has."

**Why it works:** The SAT deliberately places plural nouns near verbs when the true subject is singular, exploiting the tendency to match the verb with the nearest noun.

**How to avoid it:** Always isolate the true subject by crossing out prepositional phrases, relative clauses, and other modifiers between the subject and verb. Then match the verb to the actual subject, not the nearest noun.

### 1.10.4 The "Half-Right" Trap

**What it is:** An answer choice that begins correctly but adds an incorrect phrase, word, or clause at the end.

**Example:** A transition question where the first part of the answer correctly identifies the logical relationship but the second part introduces an unsupported claim.

**Why it works:** Students often read the first part of the answer, see that it's correct, and select it without reading the rest. The SAT exploits this by making the first part of the wrong answer appealing and the second part subtly wrong.

**How to avoid it:** Read the *entire* answer choice before selecting it. Even one word can make an otherwise correct answer wrong.

### 1.10.5 The "Extreme Language" Trap

**What it is:** An answer choice that uses absolute or extreme language ("always," "never," "all," "none," "completely") when the passage supports a more moderate claim.

**Why it works:** Extreme language is attention-grabbing and can seem more "definitive" and therefore more correct. But careful authors rarely make absolute claims, and the SAT rewards answers that reflect the nuanced, qualified language of the passage.

**How to avoid it:** Be skeptical of any answer choice that uses absolute language. Unless the passage explicitly supports an absolute claim, the correct answer will typically use more moderate language ("often," "many," "some," "tends to").

### 1.10.6 The "Scope Error" Trap

**What it is:** An answer choice that is factually true but addresses a broader or narrower scope than the passage discusses.

**Example:** A passage discusses the effects of a specific policy on urban air quality. An answer choice claims the policy "improved environmental conditions nationwide" — this is a scope error because the passage only discusses urban air quality, not all environmental conditions nationwide.

**Why it works:** The answer choice contains information that is plausible and related to the passage, but it overgeneralizes or misrepresents the passage's scope.

**How to avoid it:** Compare the scope of the answer choice to the scope of the passage. If the answer choice makes a broader claim than the passage supports, it is wrong.

### 1.10.7 The "Causal Claim Without Signals" Trap

**What it is:** An answer choice that implies a causal relationship between two events or ideas without the passage providing a causal signal (e.g., "because," "therefore," "as a result").

**Why it works:** Students often infer causation from correlation — if two things are mentioned together, they assume one caused the other. The SAT tests whether you can distinguish between correlation and causation.

**How to avoid it:** Look for explicit causal language in the passage. If the passage does not use words like "because," "therefore," "caused," or "led to," do not select an answer that implies causation. Prefer weaker relationships like "associated with" or "coincided with."

---

## 1.11 The Mental Game: Test-Day Psychology

### 1.11.1 Managing Anxiety

Test anxiety is one of the biggest obstacles to SAT performance. Anxiety impairs working memory, reduces focus, and leads to careless errors — exactly the kind of errors that are most costly on an adaptive test where every question in Module 1 matters.

**Strategies for managing anxiety:**

1.  **Simulate test conditions during practice.** Take full-length, timed practice tests in a quiet environment with the same tools (Bluebook app, calculator, pencils) you'll use on test day. The more familiar the testing experience feels, the less anxiety you'll feel on the actual day.

2.  **Develop a pre-test routine.** On test day, arrive early, eat a good breakfast, and engage in a brief relaxation exercise (deep breathing, visualization, or positive self-talk) before the test begins.

3.  **Focus on the process, not the outcome.** During the test, concentrate on executing your strategy — reading carefully, predicting answers, eliminating wrong choices — rather than worrying about your score. The score is a byproduct of good process.

4.  **Use the mark/return tool strategically.** If a question is causing anxiety, mark it and move on. Knowing you can return to it later reduces the pressure of having to get it right immediately.

### 1.11.2 Building Stamina

The digital SAT is approximately **$2$ hours and $14$ minutes** long (plus breaks). Maintaining focus and energy throughout the test requires mental stamina, which must be built through practice.

**Strategies for building stamina:**

1.  **Take full-length practice tests regularly.** At least once a week in the month before the test, take a complete, timed practice test under realistic conditions.

2.  **Practice active reading.** Passive reading — letting your eyes move over the words without engaging — leads to fatigue and missed information. Active reading involves highlighting transitions, writing brief notes, and constantly asking yourself "What is the author doing here?"

3.  **Stay hydrated and nourished.** Bring water and a small snack for the break between sections. Dehydration and low blood sugar impair cognitive function.

4.  **Use breaks effectively.** During the break, stand up, stretch, eat your snack, and take a few deep breaths. Do not discuss the test with other students — this often increases anxiety.

### 1.11.3 The Growth Mindset

Perhaps the most important psychological factor in SAT preparation is the **growth mindset** — the belief that your abilities can be developed through effort, strategy, and learning from mistakes. Students with a growth mindset view errors as opportunities to improve; students with a fixed mindset view errors as evidence of innate inability.

Research consistently shows that students with a growth mindset achieve higher scores, persist longer in the face of difficulty, and respond more constructively to feedback. Every error in your error log is not a failure — it is a data point that tells you exactly what to study next.

---

## 1.12 Recommended Resources for SAT Writing Preparation

### 1.12.1 Official College Board Resources

- **Bluebook Practice Tests:** The College Board offers free, full-length adaptive practice tests through the Bluebook app. These are the highest-fidelity practice materials available because they are created by the same organization that writes the actual test. Take all available practice tests under timed conditions.

- **Khan Academy SAT Practice:** Khan Academy has an official partnership with the College Board and offers free, personalized SAT practice. The platform adapts to your skill level and provides targeted practice in your weak areas.

### 1.12.2 Third-Party Resources

- **Erica Meltzer's "The Ultimate Guide to SAT Grammar":** Widely regarded as the most comprehensive and effective grammar prep book for the SAT. Meltzer breaks down every grammar rule tested on the SAT with clear explanations and targeted practice.

- **The College Panda SAT Writing:** Based on official exam questions, this book provides concise rule summaries and high-quality practice sets.

- **PrepScholar and Test Innovators:** Online platforms offering adaptive practice tests and detailed explanations.

### 1.12.3 How to Use Resources Effectively

1.  **Start with a diagnostic.** Take a full-length practice test to establish your baseline score and identify your weakest areas.

2.  **Study one rule at a time.** Don't try to learn everything at once. Focus on one grammar rule or question type per study session, and complete $10$–$15$ practice questions on that specific topic.

3.  **Review every mistake.** For every question you get wrong, write it in your error log, identify the error type, and review the relevant rule.

4.  **Gradually increase difficulty.** Once you are comfortable with individual rules, practice mixed sets that require you to identify which rule is being tested.

5.  **Take regular full-length tests.** Every $1$–$2$ weeks, take a full-length practice test to measure progress and adjust your study plan.

---

## 1.13 Chapter Summary: Key Takeaways

1.  **The digital SAT is adaptive.** Your performance on Module 1 determines whether you receive the harder or easier Module 2. The harder Module 2 is the only path to a score above ~$650$.

2.  **There are four content domains:** Craft & Structure (~$28\%$), Information & Ideas (~$26\%$), Standard English Conventions (~$26\%$), and Expression of Ideas (~$20\%$). Each domain tests distinct skills and requires different strategies.

3.  **Use the Predict → Prove method.** Formulate your own answer before looking at the choices to avoid trap answers.

4.  **Employ a two-pass pacing strategy.** Cap first-pass questions at ~$45$ seconds; flag and return to harder questions on the second pass.

5.  **Maintain an error log.** Track every mistake, categorize the error type, and review regularly to identify patterns and target weak areas.

6.  **Beware of common traps:** the "sounds right" trap, the "longer answer" trap, the "near-noun agreement" trap, the "half-right" trap, the "extreme language" trap, the "scope error" trap, and the "causal claim without signals" trap.

7.  **Manage test-day anxiety** through simulation, routine, and a growth mindset.

8.  **Use official College Board resources** (Bluebook, Khan Academy) as your primary practice materials, supplemented by high-quality third-party resources like Erica Meltzer's grammar guide.

---

*This chapter has established the foundational architecture of the SAT Reading and Writing section — its structure, scoring, content domains, question types, strategic frameworks, and common traps. With this foundation in place, we are now ready to dive deep into the specific grammar rules, rhetorical strategies, and reading techniques that will enable you to maximize your score. The following chapters will systematically address each content domain, beginning with the most rule-based and highest-yield domain: Standard English Conventions.*

---


# Chapter 2: Sentence Structure Deep Dive — Independent Clauses, Dependent Clauses, Fragments, and Run-Ons

---

## 2.1 Why Sentence Architecture Is the Foundation of Everything

The SAT Writing and Language section — and its digital successor, the Reading and Writing module — is, at its core, a test of whether you can recognize and produce structurally sound English sentences. Every grammar rule, every punctuation mark, every transition word, and every rhetorical decision ultimately traces back to one foundational question: **Is this sentence structurally complete and logically coherent?**

If you cannot identify an independent clause on sight, you will struggle with comma splices, semicolon placement, fragment correction, and modifier attachment. If you cannot distinguish a dependent clause from an independent one, you will misplace commas, misidentify subjects, and fall for trap answers that look correct but violate fundamental structural rules. If you cannot recognize a run-on sentence, you will waste time second-guessing punctuation choices that should be automatic.

This chapter is the architectural blueprint. Everything else — subject-verb agreement, pronoun reference, modifier placement, punctuation — is built on top of the concepts in this chapter. Master this, and the rest of the guide becomes dramatically easier.

---

## 2.2 The Anatomy of a Clause

### 2.2.1 Defining "Clause"

A **clause** is a group of words that contains both a **subject** and a **verb**. This is the single most important definition in all of SAT grammar. Every other concept — fragments, run-ons, comma splices, semicolon usage, modifier attachment — flows from this definition.

Let us break it down with absolute precision:

- **Subject:** The noun, pronoun, or noun phrase that performs the action of the verb or is described by the verb. The subject answers the question "Who or what is this sentence about?"
- **Verb:** The action word or state-of-being word that tells us what the subject does, what is done to the subject, or what the subject is.

If a group of words has both, it is a clause. If it is missing either one, it is **not** a clause — it is a phrase or a fragment.

### 2.2.2 The Two Fundamental Categories

Every clause in the English language falls into exactly one of two categories:

1. **Independent Clause (IC):** A clause that can stand alone as a complete sentence. It expresses a complete thought. It does not depend on anything else to make sense.

2. **Dependent Clause (DC):** A clause that **cannot** stand alone as a complete sentence. It begins with a subordinating conjunction or a relative pronoun, which makes it dependent on an independent clause to complete its meaning. Even though it has a subject and a verb, it leaves the reader hanging.

The SAT tests your ability to distinguish between these two types of clauses in virtually every grammar question. This is not an exaggeration. If you can look at any group of words and instantly classify it as independent or dependent, you will eliminate wrong answers at lightning speed.

### 2.2.3 The "Stand-Alone Test"

Here is the most reliable test for determining whether a clause is independent or dependent:

**Read the clause by itself. Does it sound like a complete sentence? Does it leave you waiting for more information?**

- "The researcher published her findings." → This sounds complete. You are not waiting for anything else. This is an **independent clause**.
- "Because the researcher published her findings" → This sounds incomplete. You are immediately asking, "What happened? What was the result?" This is a **dependent clause**.
- "Which was published in a prestigious journal" → This sounds incomplete. You are asking, "What was published? What are we talking about?" This is a **dependent clause**.

This intuitive test works, but it has a limitation: it relies on your ear, and your ear can be fooled by sophisticated SAT trap answers. That is why we also need the mechanical tests described below.

### 2.2.4 The "Subordinating Conjunction Test"

If a clause begins with a **subordinating conjunction**, it is automatically a dependent clause. The subordinating conjunction subordinates the clause — it makes it secondary, dependent, incomplete.

The most common subordinating conjunctions:

| Category | Subordinating Conjunctions |
|----------|---------------------------|
| Time | after, before, when, while, since, until, as soon as, whenever, as |
| Cause/Reason | because, since, as, so that, in order that |
| Condition | if, unless, provided that, even if, whether |
| Concession/Contrast | although, though, even though, whereas, while |
| Place | wherever, where |
| Manner | as if, as though |

**Rule:** If a clause begins with any of these words, it is a **dependent clause** and **cannot stand alone as a sentence**, no matter how long it is or how complete it sounds.

### 2.2.5 The "Relative Pronoun Test"

If a clause begins with a **relative pronoun**, it is a **dependent clause** (specifically, a relative clause, also called an adjective clause).

The relative pronouns: **who, whom, whose, which, that, where, when**

**Note:** Relative clauses are always dependent, but they serve a special function — they modify a noun. This is why they are sometimes called adjective clauses. We will explore this in detail in Section 2.5.

---

## 2.3 Independent Clauses: The Building Blocks of Sentences

### 2.3.1 What Makes a Clause Independent?

An independent clause meets three criteria:

1. **It has a subject** (a noun, pronoun, or noun phrase that the clause is about).
2. **It has a verb** (an action or state of being performed by or applied to the subject).
3. **It expresses a complete thought** (it does not leave the reader waiting for additional information).

If all three criteria are met, the clause is independent and can function as a sentence on its own.

### 2.3.2 Examples of Independent Clauses

Each of these can stand alone as a sentence. Period. Done. No additional words needed.

- "The committee approved the proposal."
- "Researchers have been studying this phenomenon for decades."
- "Climate change affects coastal communities disproportionately."
- "She submitted her application before the deadline."

### 2.3.3 The Minimum Viable Independent Clause

An independent clause can be as short as **two words** — a subject and a verb:

- "Birds fly."
- "She left."
- "Time passes."
- "Dogs bark."

These are complete sentences. They have a subject and a verb, and they express a complete thought. The SAT will sometimes test whether you recognize that even a very short clause is independent, especially in punctuation questions where you need to determine whether a semicolon or period is appropriate.

### 2.3.4 Independent Clauses with Compound Elements

An independent clause can be expanded with compound subjects, compound verbs, objects, modifiers, and prepositional phrases — and it remains independent as long as it still has at least one subject and one verb and expresses a complete thought.

- "The committee and the board **approved** the proposal unanimously." (Compound subject: "committee and board"; single verb: "approved")
- "The committee **reviewed** the proposal and **approved** it unanimously." (Single subject: "committee"; compound verb: "reviewed and approved")
- "The committee and the board **reviewed** the proposal and **approved** it unanimously." (Compound subject AND compound verb)

All three are independent clauses. The presence of "and" does not change the clause type — it simply adds elements within the clause.

### 2.3.5 The "One Main Verb" Principle

A single independent clause contains **one main verb** (or a compound verb joined by "and"). This is a critical concept for the SAT because it helps you identify where one clause ends and another begins.

**Example:** "The researcher **conducted** the experiment, and her assistant **published- Subject-verb pair 1: "researcher conducted" → first independent clause
- Subject-verb pair 2: "assistant published" → second independent clause

This sentence has two independent clauses joined by a comma + "and." This is a **compound sentence**, and it requires specific punctuation. The key takeaway: **count the subject-verb pairs to count the clauses.**

---

## 2.4 Dependent Clauses: The Supporting Actors

### 2.4.1 What Makes a Clause Dependent?

A dependent clause has a subject and a verb but **cannot stand alone** as a complete sentence. It is "dependent" on an independent clause to complete its meaning. It typically begins with a **subordinating conjunction** or a **relative pronoun**, which creates an expectation that more information is coming.

### 2.4.2 The Three Types of Dependent Clauses

Dependent clauses come in three varieties, each serving a different grammatical function:

#### Type 1: Adverbial Dependent Clauses (Subordinate Clauses)

These clauses function as **adverbs** — they modify the verb of the independent clause by telling us **when, where, why, how, under what condition, or to what extent** the action occurs.

They begin with **subordinating conjunctions**.

| Subordinating Conjunction | Function | Example |
|---|---|---|
| because, since, as | Cause/reason | "Because the data was inconclusive, the team repeated the experiment." |
| although, though, even though, while, whereas | Concession/contrast | "Although the results were promising, further testing was needed." |
| if, unless, provided that | Condition | "If the hypothesis is confirmed, the theory will be strengthened." |
| when, while, before, after, since, until, as soon as | Time | "When the temperature increased, the reaction accelerated." |
| wherever, where | Place | "Wherever the species was introduced, it thrived." |
| so that, in order that | Purpose | "The researchers calibrated the instruments so that the measurements would be precise." |
| as if, as though | Manner | "The compound behaved as if it were unstable." |

**Key structural point:** An adverbial dependent clause can come **before** or **after** the independent clause.

- Before: "**Because the data was inconclusive**, the team repeated the experiment."
- After: "The team repeated the experiment **because the data was inconclusive**."

When the dependent clause comes **first**, it is followed by a comma. When it comes **second**, no comma is needed (with rare exceptions for contrastive clauses with "although," "though," etc.).

#### Type 2: Adjectival Dependent Clauses (Relative Clauses)

These clauses function as **adjectives** — they modify a **noun** in the independent clause by providing additional information about it.

They begin with **relative pronouns**: who, whom, whose, which, that, where, when.

**Critical distinction for the SAT:** Relative clauses can be **essential** (restrictive) or **nonessential** (nonrestrictive), and this distinction determines whether commas are used.

- **Essential (no commas):** "The students **who completed the assignment** received credit." (Only the students who completed the assignment received credit. The clause identifies which students.)
- **Nonessential (commas):** "The students, **who completed the assignment**, received credit." (All the students completed the assignment and received credit. The clause adds extra information.)

The SAT strongly prefers **"that"** for essential clauses and **"which"** (with a comma) for nonessential clauses. Using "which" without a comma for an essential clause is a common error the SAT tests.

#### Type 3: Nominal Dependent Clauses (Noun Clauses)

These clauses function as **nouns** — they can serve as the subject of a sentence, the object of a verb, or the object of a preposition.

They begin with words like: **that, what, whatever, who, whoever, whomever, whether, how, why, when, where**

| Function | Example |
|---|---|
| Subject | "**What the researchers discovered** challenged existing assumptions." |
| Object of verb | "The study revealed **that the treatment was effective**." |
| Object of preposition | "The debate centered on **whether the results were reproducible**." |
| Subject complement | "The question is **whether the findings can be generalized**." |

Nominal clauses are less frequently tested on the SAT than adverbial and adjectival clauses, but they do appear, particularly in questions about sentence structure and logical flow.

### 2.4.3 The "Incomplete Thought" Test for Dependent Clauses

If you are ever unsure whether a clause is dependent, apply this test:

**Read the clause in isolation. Does it leave you hanging? Does it create an expectation that something else must follow?**

- "Because the experiment failed" → You immediately think, "So what happened?" → **Dependent**
- "Which was conducted over three years" → You think, "What was conducted?" → **Dependent**
- "That the hypothesis was correct" → You think, "What about it?" → **Dependent**
- "The experiment failed" → You think, "Okay, I understand." → **Independent**

This test is your first line of defense against fragment and run-on errors.

---

## 2.5 Relative Clauses: A Special Deep Dive

### 2.5.1 Why Relative Clauses Deserve Special Attention

Relative clauses are the most frequently tested type of dependent clause on the SAT. They appear in questions about:

- Comma usage (essential vs. nonessential)
- Pronoun choice (who vs. whom, that vs. which)
- Subject-verb agreement (identifying the true subject)
- Modifier placement
- Sentence boundaries

### 2.5.2 The Relative Pronoun as a "Door"

Think of a relative pronoun as a **door** that opens into a dependent clause. When you encounter "who," "whom," "whose," "which," or "that," you are entering a clause that modifies a noun and cannot stand alone.

The relative clause continues until you reach the verb of that clause and complete the thought. Everything from the relative pronoun to the end of the clause is "inside" the relative clause.

**Example:** "The architect, **who had designed several award-winning buildings**, was commissioned for the new museum."

- The relative clause is: "who had designed several award-winning buildings"
- It modifies: "The architect"
- It is nonessential (set off by commas) because it adds extra information rather than identifying which architect

### 2.5.3 "That" vs. "Which": The SAT's Favorite Distinction

This is one of the most tested grammar rules on the entire SAT.

**"That" — Used for Essential (Restrictive) Clauses**

An essential clause is necessary to identify the noun it modifies. Without it, the sentence would be ambiguous or would refer to a different thing. **No commas.**

**"Which" — Used for Nonessential (Nonrestrictive) Clauses**

A nonessential clause adds extra information that is not necessary to identify the noun. The sentence would still make sense and refer to the same thing without it. **Must be set off by commas.**

**The SAT's Position:** The College Board follows the traditional American usage rule: "that" for essential, "which" (with a comma) for nonessential. While some style guides (particularly British ones) allow "which" for essential clauses, the SAT does not. If you see "which" without a preceding comma on the SAT, it is almost certainly wrong.

### 2.5.4 "Who" vs. "Whom" in Relative Clauses

The distinction between "who" and "whom" is determined by the pronoun's function **within the relative clause**, not by the antecedent.

- **Who** = subject of the relative clause (performs the action)
- **Whom** = object of the relative clause (receives the action or follows a preposition)

**The He/Him Test:**

Replace "who" with "he" and "whom" with "him." If "he" works, use "who." If "him" works, use "whom."

**Important:** The presence of a comma before "who" or "whom" depends on whether the clause is essential or nonessential, not on the who/whom distinction itself.

---

## 2.6 Sentence Fragments: The Incomplete Thought

### 2.6.1 What Is a Fragment?

A **sentence fragment** is a group of words that is punctuated as a sentence (begins with a capital letter, ends with a period) but is **not actually a sentence** because it lacks one or more of the requirements for a complete sentence:

1. No subject
2. No verb
3. No complete thought (it is a dependent clause masquerading as a sentence)

The SAT tests fragments in two primary ways:
- As answer choices that are fragments (you must eliminate them)
- As portions of a passage that need to be connected to an independent clause

### 2.6.2 Common Fragment Types

**Type 1: Missing Subject**
A fragment may have a verb but no subject. Example: "**Conducted** the experiment over six months." (Who conducted it? No subject.)

**Type 2: Missing Verb**
A fragment may have a subject but no verb. Example: "The researcher **carefully**." (What did the researcher do? No verb.)

**Type 3: Dependent Clause as a Sentence (Most Common)**
A dependent clause — beginning with a subordinating conjunction or relative pronoun — is punctuated as if it were a complete sentence. Example: "**Because the data was inconclusive.**"

**Type 4: -ing Fragment**
A phrase beginning with an -ing word (a present participle) is not a complete sentence because -ing words are not finite verbs unless accompanied by a helping verb. Example: "**Having completed the experiment.**"

**Type 5: Prepositional Phrase Fragment**
A prepositional phrase has no subject and no verb. It cannot be a sentence. Example: "**In the middle of the night.**"

**Type 6: Appositive Fragment**
An appositive is a noun or noun phrase that renames another noun. It has no verb and cannot stand alone. Example: "**A renowned expert in molecular biology.**"

### 2.6.3 The Fragment Detection Protocol

When evaluating whether a group of words is a fragment, follow this step-by-step process:

1. **Find the verb.** Is there a finite verb (a verb with a subject and a tense)? If not, it is a fragment.
2. **Find the subject.** What is performing the action of the verb? If there is no subject, it is a fragment.
3. **Check for subordinating conjunctions.** Does the clause begin with "because," "although," "when," "if," "which," "that," "who," etc.? If so, it is a dependent clause and cannot stand alone.
4. **Check for -ing words.** Is the only verb form an -ing word without a helping verb? If so, it is a fragment.
5. **Read it aloud.** Does it sound complete, or are you waiting for more information?

If any of these checks reveal a problem, you have a fragment.

---

## 2.7 Run-On Sentences: When Clauses Collide

### 2.7.1 What Is a Run-On Sentence?

A **run-on sentence** occurs when two or more independent clauses are joined without appropriate punctuation or a coordinating conjunction. The clauses "run on" into each other without proper separation.

The SAT tests two types of run-on sentences:
1. **Fused sentences** (no punctuation at all between clauses)
2. **Comma splices** (only a comma between clauses)

### 2.7.2 Fused Sentences

A fused sentence is two independent clauses joined with **no punctuation whatsoever**.

- ✗ "The experiment was successful the results were published in a top journal."
- ✓ "The experiment was successful. The results were published in a top journal."
- ✓ "The experiment was successful; the results were published in a top journal."
- ✓ "The experiment was successful, and the results were published in a top journal."

The fused sentence is the most egregious run-on error. On the SAT, it typically appears as an answer choice where two independent clauses are jammed together without any punctuation.

### 2.7.3 Comma Splices

A comma splice is two independent clauses joined by **only a comma**. This is the most common run-on error tested on the SAT.

- ✗ "The experiment was successful, the results were published in a top journal."
- ✓ "The experiment was successful. The results were published in a top journal."
- ✓ "The experiment was successful; the results were published in a top journal."
- ✓ "The experiment was successful, and the results were published in a top journal."

**Why is a comma splice wrong?** A comma is not strong enough to separate two independent clauses. It creates a grammatical error because the reader cannot tell where one sentence ends and the next begins. The comma is a "soft" punctuation mark; separating two complete sentences requires "hard" punctuation (period or semicolon) or a comma + coordinating conjunction.

### 2.7.4 The Three Valid Ways to Join Two Independent Clauses

This is one of the most important rules in all of SAT grammar. There are exactly **three** correct ways to join two independent clauses:

| Method | Punctuation | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Period between sentences | "The experiment was successful. The results were published." |
| **Semicolon** | Semicolon between clauses | "The experiment was successful; the results were published." |
| **Comma + FANBOYS** | Comma + coordinating conjunction | "The experiment was successful, and the results were published." |

**FANBOYS** = For, And, Nor, But, Or, Yet, So

These are the **only** coordinating conjunctions that can join two independent clauses with a comma. Any other word — including "however," "therefore," "moreover," "furthermore," "consequently," "thus," "hence," "also," "then" — **cannot** join two independent clauses with just a comma.

### 2.7.5 The "However" Trap

The word **"however"** is the single most tested word in SAT grammar when it comes to run-on sentences. "However" is a **conjunctive adverb**, not a coordinating conjunction. It **cannot** join two independent clauses with a comma.

- ✗ "The experiment was successful, however, the results were published." (Comma splice — two ICs joined by only a comma)
- ✓ "The experiment was successful. However, the results were published." (Period + "However" at start of new sentence)
- ✓ "The experiment was successful; however, the results were published." (Semicolon + "however" + comma)
- ✓ "The experiment was successful. The results, however, were published." (Period + "however" embedded in second sentence)

**The same rule applies to:** therefore, moreover, furthermore, consequently, thus, hence, accordingly, also, then, meanwhile, otherwise, nevertheless, nonetheless, similarly, likewise.

### 2.7.6 The Run-On Detection Protocol

When evaluating whether a sentence is a run-on, follow this process:

1. **Count the independent clauses.** Identify each subject-verb pair. If there are two or more independent clauses, the sentence needs proper joining punctuation.
2. **Check the punctuation between clauses.** Is there a period? Semicolon? Comma + FANBOYS? If not, it is a run-on.
3. **Check for conjunctive adverbs.** If you see "however," "therefore," "moreover," etc., verify that they are preceded by a semicolon or period, not a comma.
4. **Check for comma splices.** If two independent clauses are separated by only a comma, it is a comma splice — always wrong.

---

## 2.8 The Four Sentence Types (By Structure)

Understanding the four sentence types will help you analyze any sentence on the SAT and identify structural errors.

### 2.8.1 Simple Sentence (One Independent Clause)

A simple sentence has exactly **one independent clause** and no dependent clauses.

- "The committee approved the proposal."
- "Researchers have been studying climate change for decades."
- "She submitted her application."

A simple sentence can be very long and still be simple, as long as it has only one subject-verb pair (or a compound subject/verb within a single clause).

- "The committee, after extensive deliberation and consultation with external experts, approved the proposal unanimously." → Still a simple sentence. The prepositional phrases and modifiers do not create additional clauses.

### 2.8.2 Compound Sentence (Two or More Independent Clauses)

A compound sentence has **two or more independent clauses** joined by:
- A semicolon
- A comma + FANBOYS
- A conjunctive adverb preceded by a semicolon

- "The committee approved the proposal, and the board ratified it."
- "The committee approved the proposal; the board ratified it."
- "The committee approved the proposal; however, the board requested revisions."

### 2.8.3 Complex Sentence (One Independent + One or More Dependent Clauses)

A complex sentence has **one independent clause** and **one or more dependent clauses**.

- "Because the data was inconclusive, the team repeated the experiment." (DC + IC)
- "The researcher who discovered the compound was awarded a Nobel Prize." (IC with embedded DC)
- "The report, which was published last month, revealed significant findings." (IC with embedded DC)

### 2.8.4 Compound-Complex Sentence (Two or More Independent + One or More Dependent)

A compound-complex sentence has **two or more independent clauses** and **one or more dependent clauses**.

- "Because the data was inconclusive, the team repeated the experiment, and they obtained more reliable results." (DC + IC + IC)
- "The researcher who led the study published her findings, but the results were disputed by several colleagues." (IC with DC + IC)

The SAT does not ask you to label sentence types, but understanding them helps you identify structural errors. A compound sentence without proper joining punctuation is a run-on. A complex sentence where the dependent clause stands alone is a fragment.

---

## 2.9 Advanced Clause Identification Strategies

### 2.9.1 The "Cross-Out" Method for Finding the True Subject

When a sentence has multiple clauses, prepositional phrases, and modifiers, finding the true subject of the main verb can be challenging. The SAT exploits this by placing plural nouns near verbs when the true subject is singular (or vice versa).

**The Strategy:** Cross out everything that is NOT the main clause. This includes:

1. **Prepositional phrases** (in the lab, of the experiment, with great care, by the researcher)
2. **Relative clauses** (who discovered the compound, which was published last year)
3. **Participial phrases** (having completed the study, published in a top journal)
4. **Appositive phrases** (a renowned expert, the lead author of the study)
5. **Introductory dependent clauses** (Because the data was inconclusive,)

After crossing out these elements, what remains should be the core of the independent clause: subject + verb (+ object/complement).

**Example:** "The results of the experiment, which was conducted over a period of three years by a team of twelve researchers, **were** published in a prestigious journal."

- Cross out: "of the experiment" (prepositional phrase)
- Cross out: ", which was conducted over a period of three years by a team of twelve researchers," (relative clause)
- What remains: "The results... were published in a prestigious journal."
- Subject: "The results" (plural) → Verb: "were" (plural) ✓

### 2.9.2 The "Clause Counting" Method

To determine how many clauses a sentence contains, identify every subject-verb pair. Each pair represents one clause.

**Example:** "The researcher who designed the experiment published her findings, and her colleague replicated the study."

- Subject-verb pair 1: "who designed" → relative clause (dependent)
- Subject-verb pair 2: "researcher published" → main clause (independent)
- Subject-verb pair 3: "colleague replicated" → second main clause (independent)

This sentence has three clauses: one dependent, two independent. It is a compound-complex sentence.

### 2.9.3 Identifying Clause Boundaries

Knowing where one clause ends and another begins is essential for correct punctuation. Here are the signals:

| Signal | What It Indicates |
|---|---|
| Period (.) | End of a sentence; separates independent clauses |
| Semicolon (;) | Separates two closely related independent clauses |
| Comma + FANBOYS | Separates two independent clauses |
| Subordinating conjunction | Beginning of a dependent clause |
| Relative pronoun | Beginning of a relative (dependent) clause |
| Conjunctive adverb (however, therefore, etc.) | Beginning of a new independent clause (needs semicolon or period before it) |
| Colon (:) | Introduces a list, explanation, or elaboration after an independent clause |
| Dash (—) | Sets off information or introduces an elaboration |

### 2.9.4 The "Inversion" Exception

Normally, the subject comes However, in two situations, the verb comes before the subject:

1. **Sentences beginning with "There" or "There is/are":**
   - "There **are** three reasons for this phenomenon." (Subject: "three reasons"; Verb: "are")
   - "There **is** a significant correlation." (Subject: "a significant correlation"; Verb: "is")

2. **Questions:**
   - "Have the results been verified?" (Subject: "the results"; Verb: "have been verified")

This matters for subject-verb agreement. The SAT frequently places a singular noun immediately after "there is/are" when the true subject is plural, or vice versa.

---

## 2.10 Common Structural Errors: A Comprehensive Catalog

### 2.10.1 Error: Dependent Clause as a Sentence

**What it looks like:** A sentence that begins with "Because," "Although," "When," "If," "Which," "That," "Who," etc., and ends with a period, but the thought is incomplete.

**Why it's wrong:** Dependent clauses cannot stand alone. They must be attached to an independent clause.

**How to fix it:**
- Connect the dependent clause to an independent clause: "Because the data was inconclusive, the team repeated the experiment."
- Remove the subordinating conjunction: "The data was inconclusive." (Now it's an independent clause.)

### 2.10.2 Error: Two Independent Clauses with No Punctuation (Fused Sentence)

**What it looks like:** "The experiment was successful the results were published."

**Why it's wrong:** Two complete thoughts are jammed together with no separation.

**How to fix it:**
- Add a period: "The experiment was successful. The results were published."
- Add a semicolon: "The experiment was successful; the results were published."
- Add a comma + FANBOYS: "The experiment was successful, and the results were published."

### 2.10.3 Error: Two Independent Clauses with Only a Comma (Comma Splice)

**What it looks like:** "The experiment was successful, the results were published."

**Why it's wrong:** A comma alone cannot separate two independent clauses.

**How to fix it:** Same as above — period, semicolon, or comma + FANBOYS.

### 2.10.4 Error: "However" or "Therefore" Between Two Independent Clauses with Only a Comma

**What it looks like:** "The experiment was successful, however, the results were published."

**Why it's wrong:** "However" is a conjunctive adverb, not a coordinating conjunction. It cannot join two independent clauses with a comma.

**How to fix it:**
- "The experiment was successful. However, the results were published."
- "The experiment was successful; however, the results were published."
- "The experiment was successful; the results, however, were published."

### 2.10.5 Error: Semicolon After a Dependent Clause

**What it looks like:** "Because the data was inconclusive; the team repeated the experiment."

**Why it's wrong:** A semicolon must separate two independent clauses. "Because the data was inconclusive" is a dependent clause.

**How to fix it:** Use a comma instead: "Because the data was inconclusive, the team repeated the experiment."

### 2.10.6 Error: Colon After an Incomplete Sentence

**What it looks like:** "The team included: a biologist, a chemist, and a physicist."

**Why it's wrong:** The words before the colon ("The team included") do not form a complete independent clause.

**How to fix it:**
- Remove the colon: "The team included a biologist, a chemist, and a physicist."
- Complete the clause before the colon: "The team included three specialists: a biologist, a chemist, and a physicist."

### 2.10.7 Error: Fragment Created by Incorrect Sentence Break

**What it looks like:** A passage where a period is placed in the middle of what should be a single sentence, creating a fragment.

**Example:** "The researcher published her findings. Which had taken years to compile."

**Why it's wrong:** "Which had taken years to compile" is a relative clause — it cannot stand alone.

**How to fix it:** Replace the period with a comma: "The researcher published her findings, which had taken years to compile."

---

## 2.11 The "Useless" Clauses: Non-Essential Elements You Can Cross Out

### 2.11.1 The Concept of "Useless" (Non-Essential) Clauses

One of the most powerful strategies on the SAT is the ability to identify clauses and phrases that can be mentally "crossed out" without affecting the grammatical completeness of the sentence. These are non-essential elements — they add information, but the sentence would still be grammatically complete without them.

There are three types of "useless" (non-essential) clauses/phrases:

1. **Relative clauses** (who, whom, whose, which, that)
2. **Comma clauses** (set off by commas — introductory, ending, and middle/appositive)
3. **Prepositional phrases** (in, on, at, by, for, with, etc.)

### 2.11.2 Why "Crossing Out" Matters

When you cross out non-essential elements, you isolate the core of the sentence: the subject and verb of the main clause. This allows you to:

- Check subject-verb agreement without being distracted by intervening words
