- Identify the true subject of the whether a sentence is complete or a fragment
- Spot run-on sentences by counting independent clauses

### 2.11.3 The Crossing-Out Protocol

**Step 1:** Identify and cross out all prepositional phrases.
**Step 2:** Identify and cross out all relative clauses.
**Step 3:** Identify and cross out all comma clauses (introductory, ending, and middle).
**Step 4:** What remains is the core independent clause. Check it for completeness and correctness.

**Example:** "The results of the experiment, which was conducted over a period of three years by a team of twelve researchers in a state-of-the-art facility, were, as the lead author noted, groundbreaking."

- Cross out prepositional phrases: "of the experiment," "over a period," "of three years," "by a team," "of twelve researchers," "in a state-of-the-art facility"
- Cross out relative clause: "which was conducted..."
- Cross out comma clause: "as the lead author noted"
- Core: "The results were groundbreaking." → Complete sentence, subject-verb agreement correct.

### 2.11.4 Important Caveats

- **The subject can NEVER be found within a prepositional phrase, relative clause, or comma clause.** This is an absolute rule. If you are looking for the subject, ignore these elements entirely.
- **A sentence may contain zero, one, two, or all three types of "useless" clauses.** The absence of these elements does not make a sentence incomplete.
- **Crossing out is a mental tool, not a physical one.** You are not deleting words from the sentence; you are temporarily ignoring them to see the underlying structure.

---

## 2.12 Subordination and Coordination: How Clauses Relate

### 2.12.1 Coordination: Equal Clauses

**Coordination** joins two clauses of **equal grammatical rank** — two independent clauses. The relationship between the clauses is one of equality; neither is subordinate to the other.

Coordinating conjunctions (FANBOYS) and semicolons create coordination:

- "The experiment was successful, **and** the results were published." (Two ICs of equal rank)
- "The experiment was successful; the results were published." (Two ICs of equal rank)
- "The experiment was successful, **but** the results were disputed." (Two ICs of equal rank)

### 2.12.2 Subordination: Unequal Clauses

**Subordination** joins a **dependent clause** to an **independent clause**. The dependent clause is "subordinate" — it is lower on the independent clause for its full meaning.

Subordinating conjunctions create subordination:

- "**Because** the experiment was successful, the results were published." (DC + IC)
- "The results were published **because** the experiment was successful." (IC + DC)
- "**Although** the results were disputed, the experiment was successful." (DC + IC)

### 2.12.3 Why Subordination vs. Coordination Matters on the SAT

The SAT tests your ability to choose the correct relationship between ideas. Sometimes the logical relationship requires coordination (equal ideas), and sometimes it requires subordination (one idea is more important than the other).

The SAT may ask you to choose between a coordinating conjunction and a subordinating conjunction based on the logical relationship between the ideas.

---

## 2.13 Sentence Structure and the Digital SAT: What Has Changed

### 2.13.1 The Shift to Short Passages

The digital SAT presents one question per short passage (25–150 words). This means you are not reading long passages with multiple questions per passage. Instead, you are evaluating individual sentences or short texts for structural correctness.

This format places even more emphasis on your ability to quickly identify:
- Whether a sentence is complete or a fragment
- Whether two clauses are properly joined
- Whether modifiers are correctly attached
- Whether punctuation is used correctly

### 2.13.2 The Adaptive Nature of the Test

The digital SAT is adaptive: your performance on Module 1 determines the difficulty of Module 2. A harder Module 2 is required for top scores. This means that if you are performing well, you will see more complex sentence structures, more subtle errors, and more sophisticated vocabulary in Module 2.

Mastering sentence structure is essential for performing well enough on Module 1 to unlock the harder Module 2 — and then for handling the harder Module 2 when you get there.

### 2.13.3 Question Types That Test Sentence Structure

On the digital SAT, sentence structure is tested through:

- **Standard English Conventions questions** (~26% of the section): Direct grammar and punctuation questions
- **Expression of Ideas questions** (~20% of the section): Transitions, sentence placement, add/delete, rhetorical synthesis
- **Craft & Structure questions** (~28% of the section): Text structure, purpose, cross-text connections
- **Information & Ideas questions** (~26% of the section): Main ideas, inferences, command of evidence

Sentence structure knowledge is directly relevant to the first two categories and indirectly relevant to all four.

---

## 2.14 The Complete Sentence Structure Decision Tree

When you encounter any group of words on the SAT, use this decision tree to classify it:

```
START: Is there a subject?
├── NO → FRAGMENT (missing subject)
└── YES → Is there a verb?
    ├── NO → FRAGMENT (missing verb)
    └── YES → Is the clause introduced by a subordinating conjunction or relative pronoun?
        ├── YES → DEPENDENT CLAUSE
        └── NO → INDEPENDENT CLAUSE
            └── Does it express a complete thought?
                ├── YES → It is a complete sentence (or part of one).
```

When there are **two or more** independent clauses:

```
Are there two or more ICs?
├── NO → Simple or complex sentence (check for DC attachment)
└── YES → How are they joined?
    ├── Period → ✓ Correct (two separate sentences)
    ├── Semicolon → ✓ Correct
    ├── Comma + FANBOYS → ✓ Correct
    ├── Semicolon + conjunctive adverb + comma → ✓ Correct
    ├── Comma only →  COMMA SPLICE
    ├── Nothing → ✗ FUSED SENTENCE
    └── Comma + conjunctive adverb → ✗ COMMA SPLICE
```

---

## 2.15 Nuanced Scenarios and Edge Cases

### 2.15.1 The "Imperative Sentence" Exception

In imperative sentences (commands), the subject "you" is implied and not stated.

- "Close the door." → Subject: [You] (implied); Verb: "Close." This is a complete sentence.
- "Please submit your application by the deadline." → Subject: [You] (implied); Verb: "submit." Complete sentence.

The SAT rarely tests this directly, but it is important to recognize that not every sentence has an explicitly stated subject.

### 2.15.2 The "Elliptical Clause" Exception

In some constructions, words are omitted (ellipted) but understood from context.

- "The first experiment was successful, the second not." → "not" implies "was not successful." This is acceptable in formal English.
- "She runs faster than I [run]." → The verb is omitted but understood.

The SAT does not typically test elliptical constructions as errors, but you should be aware that not every clause has every word explicitly stated.

### 2.15.3 The "Expletive Construction" (There is/are, It is)

Sentences beginning with "There" or "It" as expletives (placeholder subjects) have the real subject after the verb.

- "There are three main causes of the phenomenon." → Real subject: "three main causes"
- "It is important to note that the results are preliminary." → Real subject: "to note that the results are preliminary" (delayed subject)

These constructions are common on the SAT and are frequently used to test subject-verb agreement.

### 2.15.4 The "Appositive Trap"

An appositive is a noun or noun phrase that renames another noun. It is set off by commas (or dashes/parentheses) and is non-essential.

The SAT sometimes places a plural noun in an appositive near a verb to trick you into using a plural verb when the subject is singular.

**Key rule:** Phrases like "along with," "as well as," "in addition to," "together with," "accompanied by" do NOT create compound subjects. The subject before these phrases determines verb number.

### 2.15.5 Correlative Conjunctions and Clause Structure

Correlative conjunctions (either...or, neither...nor, not only...but also, both...and) join parallel elements. These elements can be words, phrases, or clauses.

When correlative conjunctions join two independent clauses, the same punctuation rules apply as for any two independent clauses.

### 2.15.6 The "Parenthetical Element" and Clause Boundaries

Parenthetical elements — set off by commas, dashes, or parentheses — can create confusion about clause boundaries.

**SAT Trap:** An answer choice might incorrectly punctuate the parenthetical element, mixing commas with dashes or parentheses. The rule is: **never mix punctuation types.** If you open with a comma, close with a comma. If you open with a dash, close with a dash.

---

## 2.16 The Relationship Between Sentence Structure and Other Grammar Topics

### 2.16.1 Sentence Structure and Subject-Verb Agreement

You cannot check subject-verb agreement until you have correctly identified the subject and the verb. And you cannot correctly identify the subject and verb until you have identified the clause boundaries and crossed out non-verbals, prepositional phrases, and dependent clauses.

**The chain of dependency:**
Sentence structure → Clause identification → Subject isolation → Verb identification → Agreement check

If any link in this chain breaks, the agreement check fails.

### 2.16.2 Sentence Structure and Pronoun Reference

A pronoun must have a clear antecedent — a specific noun it refers to. If the sentence structure is unclear (e.g., a dependent clause is incorrectly attached, or a modifier is misplaced), the pronoun reference may become ambiguous.

### 2.16.3 Sentence Structure and Modifiers

A modifier must be placed next to the word it modifies. If the sentence structure is unclear — if clauses are improperly joined or fragments are present — the modifier may end up modifying the wrong word.

### 2.16.4 Sentence Structure and Punctuation

Every punctuation mark on the SAT is governed by clause structure:
- **Periods** separate independent clauses into separate sentences.
- **Semicolons** join two independent clauses.
- **Commas** separate dependent clauses from independent clauses, set off non-essential elements, and join independent clauses with FANBOYS.
- **Colons** follow independent clauses to introduce lists or explanations.
- **Dashes** set off non-essential information or introduce elaborations.

If you cannot identify clause types, you cannot punctuate correctly.

---

## 2.17 Summary: The Sentence Structure Master Framework

### The Five Pillars of Sentence Structure

1. **Every clause has a subject and a verb.** If either is missing, it is not a clause — it is a phrase or fragment.

2. **An independent clause can stand alone.** It has a subject, a verb, and expresses a complete thought. It does not begin with a subordinating conjunction or relative pronoun.

3. **A dependent clause cannot stand alone.** It has a subject and a verb but begins with a subordinating conjunction or relative pronoun, making it incomplete.

4. **Two independent clauses must be joined by a period, semicolon, or comma + FANBOYS.** Any other joining method creates a run-on sentence (fused sentence or comma splice).

5. **Non-essential elements (prepositional phrases, relative clauses, comma clauses) can be crossed out** to reveal the core of the sentence. The subject is never found within these elements.

### The Three Questions to Ask About Any Sentence

1. **Is it complete?** Does it have a subject, a verb, and a complete thought? If not, it is a fragment.

2. **Are the clauses properly joined?** If there are two or more independent clauses, are they separated by a period, semicolon, or comma + FANBOYS? If not, it is a run-on.

3. **Is the punctuation correct?** Does every punctuation mark follow the rules for clause structure? Commas, semicolons, colons, and dashes each have specific structural requirements.

### The One Strategy That Solves Everything

**Cross out the non-essential elements. Find the core. Check the core. Then check the connections.**

This single strategy — stripping away the noise to reveal the underlying structure — will help you solve the vast majority of SAT grammar questions. It is the foundation upon which all other grammar skills are built.

---

## 2.18 Looking Ahead

With sentence structure mastered, you are now equipped to tackle the remaining grammar topics with confidence. The next chapters will build on this foundation:

- **Subject-Verb Agreement** requires you to identify the true subject by crossing out non-essential elements — exactly as we have practiced in this chapter.
- **Pronoun Agreement** requires you to identify the antecedent, which means understanding which noun a pronoun refers to within the clause structure.
- **Modifiers** must be attached to the correct word, which means understanding clause boundaries and the logical relationship between clauses.
- **Punctuation** is entirely governed by clause structure — commas, semicolons, colons, and dashes each have specific rules based on the types of clauses they connect or separate.

Every topic in this guide traces back to the concepts in this chapter. Return to these principles whenever you encounter a grammar question that seems confusing. Strip away the complexity, find the core, and apply the rules. The SAT rewards precision, and precision begins with understanding structure.

---

---


# Chapter 3: Subject-Verb Agreement Mastery — Isolation Techniques, Special Cases, and Tricky Interrupters

---

## 3.0 Why Subject-Verb Agreement Is the Most Tested Grammar Rule on the SAT

Subject-verb agreement (SVA) is, without question, the single most frequently tested grammatical rule on the SAT Writing and Language section. Across every iteration of the test — from the old paper-based SAT to the current digital adaptive format — SVA questions consistently appear with a frequency that dwarfs most other grammar topics. The reason is simple: SVA errors are easy to disguise, easy to rush past, and extraordinarily effective at separating students who understand English grammar at a structural level from those who simply rely on what "sounds right."

The fundamental rule is deceptively simple: **singular subjects take singular verbs; plural subjects take plural verbs.** But the SAT does not test this rule in isolation. Instead, the test wraps the rule in layers of misdirection — prepositional phrases, relative clauses, appositives, inverted sentence structures, and collective nouns — all designed to obscure the true subject and lead you to match the verb to the wrong noun.

This chapter will systematically dismantle every technique the SAT uses to obscure subject-verb relationships. By the end, you will have a repeatable, mechanical process for isolating any subject in any sentence, no matter how complex or deliberately confusing the construction.

---

## 3.1 The Foundational Pattern: The "S" Rule

Before diving into complex isolation techniques, you must internalize the most fundamental pattern in English subject-verb agreement:

> **Singular subjects typically do NOT end in "S." Singular verbs typically DO end in "S."**
>
> **Plural subjects typically DO end in "S." Plural verbs typically DO NOT end in "S."**

This is the single most important pattern in all of SVA. Let's examine it with crystal clarity:

| Subject | Verb | Explanation |
|---|---|---|
| The dog **runs** | Singular subject (dog) + singular verb (runs) | Both patterns hold |
| The dogs **run** | Plural subject (dogs) + plural verb (run) | Both patterns hold |
| The child **plays** | Singular subject (child) + singular verb (plays) | Both patterns hold |
| The children **play** | Plural subject (children) + plural verb (play) | Both patterns hold |

**Critical nuance:** This pattern is a *general guideline*, not an absolute law. English is full of exceptions — irregular plurals (children, people, mice), Latin/Greek borrowings (criteria, phenomena, data), and nouns that look plural but are singular (news, mathematics, measles). The "S" rule is your starting point, not your ending point. You must always confirm the actual number of the subject through the isolation techniques described in the sections that follow.

### Why This Pattern Exists

In English, the third-person singular present tense verb form adds an "-s" or "-es" suffix (he walks, she runs, it goes). Plural subjects, by contrast, use the base verb form (they walk, we run, go). This is a relic of older English conjugation patterns that have been simplified over centuries. The key insight is that the "-s" on a verb is a *singularity marker* — it signals "this verb is conjugated for a singular subject in the third person."

### When the "S" Rule Fails

Be aware of these common situations where the "S" rule can mislead you:

1. **Irregular plurals without -s:** children, men, women, mice, geese, people, teeth, feet. These are plural but do not end in "s," so they take plural verbs (no -s on the verb).

2. **Nouns ending in -s that are singular:** news, mathematics, physics, economics, measles, mumps, billiards, athletics. These take singular verbs.

3. **Nouns with the same singular and plural form:** sheep, deer, fish (sometimes), species, series, aircraft. Context determines whether they are singular or plural.

4. **Latin/Greek plural forms:** criterion → criteria, phenomenon → phenomena, datum → data, medium → media, bacterium → bacteria, curriculum → curricula, analysis → analyses, thesis → theses, crisis → crises, appendix → appendices. The SAT occasionally tests these, particularly "data" (plural) and "criteria" (plural).

---

## 3.2 The Isolation Method: A Step-by-Step Mechanical Process

This is the core technique of the entire chapter. When you encounter any SVA question on the SAT, you must follow this process mechanically. Do not skip steps. Do not rely on intuition. Execute the process.

### The Five-Step Isolation Method

**Step 1: Identify the verb in question.**
Locate the verb whose number (singular or plural) is being tested. The SAT will typically present two answer choices that differ only in the verb's number (e.g., "is" vs. "are," "has" vs. "have," "was" vs. "were").

**Step 2: Draw a line through the verb and ask, "What is doing this action?"**
The answer to this question is the subject. But you must not simply grab the nearest noun — that is the SAT's most common trap.

**Step 3: Eliminate everything that is NOT the subject.**
This is where the isolation happens. You must cross out (mentally or on paper) every phrase, clause, and modifier that sits between the subject and the verb. The three categories of "useless" material are:

- **Prepositional phrases** (see Section 3.3)
- **Relative clauses** (see Section 3.4)
- **Comma clauses / nonessential modifiers** (see Section 3.5)

**Step 4: Identify the true subject.**
What remains after elimination is the subject. Determine whether it is singular or plural.

**Step 5: Match the verb to the subject.**
Apply the foundational pattern. Singular subject → singular verb. Plural subject → plural verb.

### Visual Representation of the Process

Consider this sentence:

> *The collection of rare manuscripts, along with several first-edition novels, **is/are** housed in the university library.*

**Step 1:** The verb in question is "is/are."

**Step 2:** What is housed in the library? The collection.

**Step 3:** Eliminate everything between the subject and verb:
- "of rare manuscripts" → prepositional phrase → CROSS OUT
- ", along with several first-edition novels," → comma clause / prepositional phrase → CROSS OUT

**Step 4:** What remains? "The collection" → singular.

**Step 5:** Singular subject → singular verb → **"is."**

The correct answer is: *"The collection of rare manuscripts, along with several first-edition novels, **is** housed in the university library."*

---

## 3.3 Prepositional Phrases: The Most Common Interrupter

### The Rule

**The subject of a sentence can NEVER be found inside a prepositional phrase.**

This is one of the most important rules in all of SAT grammar, and it is tested with extraordinary frequency. A prepositional phrase is a group of words that begins with a preposition and ends with a noun or pronoun (called the object of the preposition). The object of the preposition is NOT the subject of the sentence, no matter how close it appears to the verb.

### The Preposition List

You must be able to instantly recognize prepositions. Here is a comprehensive list:

**Simple Prepositions:**
about, above, across, after, against, along, amid, among, around, at, before, behind, below, beneath, beside, between, beyond, but (when meaning "except"), by, concerning, considering, despite, down, during, except, for, from, in, inside, into, like, near, of, off, on, onto, out, over, past, regarding, since, through, throughout, to, toward, under, underneath, until, up, upon, with, within, without

**Compound/Phrasal Prepositions:**
according to, along with, as well as, because of, by means of, in addition to, in front of, in place of, in spite of, instead of, on account of, on behalf of, on top of, out of, prior to, with regard to, with respect to

### How Prepositional Phrases Obscure the Subject

The SAT places prepositional phrases between the subject and the verb to create distance and confusion. The object of the preposition — the noun at the end of the phrase — often *sounds* like it could be the subject, especially if it differs in number from the true subject.

**Example 1:**
> *The box of chocolates **is/are** on the table.*

- Prepositional phrase: "of chocolates"
- Object of preposition: "chocolates" (plural)
- True subject: "box" (singular)
- Correct verb: **"is"**

**Example 2:**
> *The boxes of chocolate **is/are** on the table.*

- Prepositional phrase: "of chocolate"
- Object of preposition: "chocolate" (singular, uncountable)
- True subject: "boxes" (plural)
- Correct verb: **"are"**

**Example 3 (Multiple prepositional phrases):**
> *The impact of the new regulations on employee productivity **has/have** been significant.*

- Prepositional phrase 1: "of the new regulations"
- Prepositional phrase 2: "on employee productivity"
- Both are eliminated
- True subject: "impact" (singular)
- Correct verb: **"has"**

**Example 4 (Long, complex prepositional phrase):**
> *The analysis of the data collected from the experiments conducted in three separate laboratories **reveals/reveal** a surprising pattern.*

- Prepositional phrases: "of the data," "from the experiments," "in three separate laboratories"
- All eliminated
- True subject: "analysis" (singular)
- Correct verb: **"reveals"**

### The "Along With" Trap

The SAT loves to use "along with," "as well as," "in addition to," "together with," "accompanied by," and "coupled with" as interrupters. These phrases are NOT the same as "and." They do NOT create compound subjects. The subject before the phrase determines the verb number.

> *The CEO, along with the board of directors, **is** attending the conference.* (singular — "CEO" is the subject)

> *The students, as well as the teacher, **are** going on the field trip.* (plural — "students" is the subject)

> *The novel, in addition to its sequels, **has** won numerous awards.* (singular — "novel" is the subject)

This distinction — "and" creates a compound (plural) subject, while "along with" and similar phrases do NOT — is one of the most frequently tested concepts in all of SAT grammar.

---

## 3.4 Relative Clauses: The Second Major Interrupter

### The Rule

**The subject of a sentence can NEVER be found inside a relative clause.**

A relative clause is a dependent clause that begins with a relative pronoun (who, whom, whose, which, that) and modifies a noun. Relative clauses are essential to the meaning of the noun they modify, but they are NOT part of the main clause's subject-verb relationship.

### Relative Pronouns

| Relative Pronoun | Function | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **who** | Subject of relative clause (people) | "The student **who studies** hard will succeed" |
| **whom** | Object of relative clause (people) | "The student **whom I tutored** improved" |
| **whose** | Possessive (people and things) | "The author **whose book** won is famous" |
| **which** | Subject/object of relative clause (things, nonessential) | "The book, **which is long**, is brilliant" |
| **that** | Subject/object of relative clause (things, essential) | "The book **that won** is brilliant" |

### How Relative Clauses Obscure the Subject

**Example 1:**
> *The results of the experiment, which **was/were** conducted over a period of six months, **has/have** been published.*

- Relative clause: "which was/were conducted over a period of six months"
- Inside the relative clause, "which" refers to "results" (plural), so the verb inside the relative clause should be "were"
- Main clause subject: "results" (plural)
- Main clause verb: **"have"**

**Example 2:**
> *The professor who teaches the advanced calculus courses **is/are** retiring this year.*

- Relative clause: "who teaches the advanced calculus courses"
- Inside the relative clause, "who" refers to "professor" (singular), so the verb inside the relative clause is "teaches"
- Main clause subject: "professor" (singular)
- Main clause verb: **"is"**

**Example 3 (Multiple relative clauses):**
> *The company that manufactures the components which power the new devices **has/have** announced a merger.*

- Relative clause 1: "that manufactures the components" (modifies "company")
- Relative clause 2: "which power the new devices" (modifies "components")
- Main clause subject: "company" (singular)
- Main clause verb: **"has"**

### "That" vs. "Which" — A Brief but Important Note

While the SAT primarily tests the distinction between "that" (essential/restrictive, no comma) and "which" (nonessential/nonrestrictive, with comma) in the context of punctuation and clause types, it is worth noting that both create relative clauses that must be eliminated when isolating the subject. The presence of "that" or "which" is a signal that you are entering a relative clause — everything from the relative pronoun to the verb inside that clause is part of the dependent clause and must be set aside.

---

## 3.5 Comma Clauses and Nonessential Modifiers: The Third Major Interrupter

### The Rule

**Nonessential (nonrestrictive) modifiers — set off by commas, dashes, or parentheses — can be removed from the sentence without affecting the main clause's subject-verb relationship.**

These interrupting elements come in three forms:

1. **Introductory Comma Clauses (ICCs):** Begin with subordinating conjunctions, prepositions, or -ing adverbs; appear at the beginning of a sentence; must be followed by a comma.

2. **Ending Comma Clauses (ECCs):** Appear at the end of a sentence; often begin with -ing adverbs; preceded by a comma.

3. **Middle Comma Clauses (MCCs) / Appositives:** Interrupt the sentence between the subject and verb; set off by commas, dashes, or parentheses on both sides.

### How Comma Clauses Obscure the Subject

**Example 1 (Appositive):**
> *The Amazon River, the second-longest river in the world, **flows/flow** through Brazil and Peru.*

- Appositive: "the second-longest river in the world"
- Eliminated
- True subject: "The Amazon River" (singular)
- Correct verb: **"flows"**

**Example 2 (Nonessential clause with commas):**
> *The research team, which includes scientists from five countries, **has/have** published its findings.*

- Nonessential clause: "which includes scientists from five countries"
- Eliminated
- True subject: "The research team" (singular — collective noun)
- Correct verb: **"has"**

**Example 3 (Dash-separated interrupter):**
> *The new policy—one that has been debated for months—**takes/take** effect on January 1st.*

- Interrupter: "one that has been debated for months"
- Eliminated
- True subject: "The new policy" (singular)
- Correct verb: **"takes"**

**Example 4 (Parenthetical interrupter):**
> *The museum's collection (which spans over 5,000 years of history) **is/are** truly remarkable.*

- Parenthetical: "which spans over 5,000 years of history"
- Eliminated
- True subject: "The museum's collection" (singular)
- Correct verb: **"is"**

### The Possessive Trap Within Interrupters

The SAT frequently places possessive nouns within interrupting phrases. Remember: **possessives can never be subjects.** If you see "Kailyn's," "the company's," "the student's," or any other possessive form, it cannot be the subject of the main verb.

> *The company's decision to expand into international markets **has/have** surprised analysts.*

- "Company's" is possessive — it modifies "decision"
- True subject: "decision" (singular)
- Correct verb: **"has"**

---

## 3.6 The "There Is/Are" Construction: Inverted Subject-Verb Order

### The Rule

**When a sentence begins with "There" (or "Here"), the subject comes AFTER the verb.**

This is one of the most important exceptions to the general rule that the subject comes before the verb. In "there is/are" constructions, the verb appears first, and the true subject follows. This inversion is a common source of SVA errors because students instinctively use a singular verb without checking the subject that follows.

### How to Handle "There" Constructions

**Step 1:** Identify the verb (immediately after "There").

**Step 2:** Look PAST the verb to find the true subject.

**Step 3:** Determine if the true subject is singular or plural.

**Step 4:** Match the verb to the subject.

**Example 1:**
> *There **is/are** three reasons for the delay.*

- True subject: "three reasons" (plural)
- Correct verb: **"are"**

**Example 2:**
> *There **has/have** been numerous complaints about the new policy.*

- True subject: "numerous complaints" (plural)
- Correct verb: **"have"**

**Example 3:**
> *There **remains/remain** a significant amount of work to be done.*

- True subject: "a significant amount" (singular — "amount" is the head noun)
- Correct verb: **"remains"**

**Example 4:**
> *There **seems/seem** to be several problems with the analysis.*

- True subject: "several problems" (plural)
- Correct verb: **"seem"**

### "There" vs. "It"

Be careful not to confuse "There" (used in existential constructions) with "It" (used as a dummy subject). "It" is always singular and always takes a singular verb:

> *It **is** important to note the limitations of the study.* (correct)
> *It **are** important to note the limitations of the study.* (incorrect)

### "Here" Constructions

The same inversion rule applies to "Here":

> *Here **is** the report you requested.* (singular subject: "report")
> *Here **are** the reports you requested.* (plural subject: "reports")

---

## 3.7 Questions: The Other Inverted Structure

### The Rule

**In questions, the subject often appears in the middle of the verb phrase or after part of the verb.**

This inversion in questions can make it harder to identify the subject, especially when auxiliary verbs are involved.

**Example 1:**
> *What **is/are** the main causes of the conflict?*

- Subject: "the main causes" (plural)
- Correct verb: **"are"**

**Example 2:**
> *How many students **has/have** completed the assignment?*

- Subject: "many students" (plural)
- Correct verb: **"have"**

**Example 3:**
> *Which of the proposals **does/do** the committee support?*

- Subject: "Which" — but "which" refers to "proposals" (plural)
- Correct verb: **"do"**

### Strategy for Questions

Convert the question to a statement. The subject will become clear:

- "What are the main causes of the conflict?" → "The main causes of the conflict **are**..."
- "How many students have completed the assignment?" → "Many students **have** completed..."

---

## 3.8 Collective Nouns: Singular by Default

### The Rule

**Collective nouns — nouns that refer to a group of people or things — are typically treated as SINGULAR when the group acts as a unit.**

### Common Collective Nouns

| Category | Examples |
|---|---|
| **Groups of people** | team, committee, jury, family, group, class, audience, crowd, staff, faculty, band, orchestra, government, council, board, panel, crew, squad, army, flock, herd, swarm |
| **Collections** | collection, set, bunch, pile, stack, bundle, batch, pack |
| **Organizations** | company, corporation, organization, institution, agency, department, school, university, hospital |
| **Abstract groups** | majority, minority, number (see Section 3.10), variety, range, total |

### Examples

> *The team **is** playing well this season.* (The team acts as a unit → singular)
> *The committee **has** reached a decision.* (The committee acts as a unit → singular)
> *The jury **was** deliberating for three days.* (The jury acts as a unit → singular)
> *The family **is** moving to a new city.* (The family acts as a unit → singular)
> *The audience **was** captivated by the performance.* (The audience acts as a unit → singular)

### When Collective Nouns Are Plural

A collective noun is treated as plural when the members of the group are acting individually, not as a unit. This is usually indicated by context — for example, if the sentence emphasizes disagreement or individual actions within the group.

> *The team **are** arguing among themselves about the strategy.* (Individual members disagreeing → plural)
> *The family **were** divided on the decision.* (Individual members disagreeing → plural)

**However, on the SAT, collective nouns are almost always treated as singular.** The test rarely, if ever, presents a situation where a collective noun should be treated as plural. If you see a collective noun as the subject, your default choice should be a singular verb.

---

## 3.9 Indefinite Pronouns: The Always-Singular Group

### The Rule

**Certain indefinite pronouns are ALWAYS singular and always take singular verbs, regardless of any modifying phrases that follow them.**

### The Always-Singular Indefinite Pronouns

| Pronoun | Example | Correct Verb |
|---|---|---|
| **each** | Each of the students **has** a textbook. | has |
| **every** | Every candidate **is** qualified. | is |
| **everyone** | Everyone **has** arrived. | has |
| **everybody** | Everybody **is** welcome. | is |
| **someone** | Someone **is** waiting outside. | is |
| **somebody** | Somebody **has** taken my seat. | has |
| **anyone** | Anyone **is** eligible to apply. | is |
| **anybody** | Anybody **is** capable of learning. | is |
| **no one** | No one **has** volunteered. | has |
| **nobody** | Nobody **is** perfect. | is |
| **either** | Either option **is** acceptable. | is |
| **neither** | Neither answer **is** correct. | is |
| **another** | Another opportunity **exists**. | exists |
| **one** | One of the students **is** absent. | is |

### The "Each/Every + of the + Plural Noun" Trap

This is one of the SAT's most reliable traps. The phrase "each of the students" or "every one of the candidates" contains a plural noun ("students," "candidates") inside a prepositional phrase ("of the students," "of the candidates"). Students who don't isolate the subject will match the verb to the plural noun in the prepositional phrase. This is always wrong.

> *Each of the students **has/have** completed the assignment.*
- "of the students" → prepositional phrase → eliminate
- True subject: "Each" (singular)
- Correct verb: **"has"**

> *Every one of the participants **was/were** given a certificate.*
- "of the participants" → prepositional phrase → eliminate
- True subject: "Every one" (singular)
- Correct verb: **"was"**

> *Neither of the proposals **addresses/address** the core issue.*
- "of the proposals" → prepositional phrase → eliminate
- True subject: "Neither" (singular)
- Correct verb: **"addresses"**

### "Everyone" vs. "Every one"

Note the distinction:
- **Everyone** (one word) = everybody (always singular)
- **Every one** (two words) = each individual one (always singular, but can be followed by "of the + plural noun")

Both take singular verbs.

---

## 3.10 The "Number" Distinction: "A Number of" vs. "The Number of"

### The Rule

**"A number of" = plural. "The number of" = singular.**

This is a fixed distinction that the SAT tests with high reliability.

| Expression | Meaning | Verb Number | Example |
|---|---|---|---|
| **A number of** | Several, many | **Plural** | "A number of students **have** protested." |
| **The number of** | The quantity, the count | **Singular** | "The number of students **is** increasing." |

### Why This Distinction Exists

"A number of" functions as a synonym for "several" or "many" — it refers to the individual members of a group, which is plural. "The number of" refers to the count or quantity itself, which is a single numerical value, and therefore singular.

### Examples

> *A number of employees **have** expressed concerns about the new policy.* (plural — referring to the employees)
> *The number of employees **has** increased significantly this year.* (singular — referring to the count)
> *A number of factors **contribute** to climate change.* (plural — referring to the factors)
> *The number of factors **is** overwhelming.* (singular — referring to the count)

---

## 3.11 "Neither/Nor" and "Either/Or": The Proximity Rule

### The Rule

**When two subjects are joined by "neither...nor" or "either...or," the verb agrees with the subject CLOSEST to it.**

This is called the **proximity rule** (or the rule of nearest attraction). It is different from "and" (always plural) and from "along with" (subject before the phrase determines the verb).

### Examples

> *Neither the teacher nor the students **were** present.*
- Subjects: "teacher" (singular), "students" (plural)
- Closest to verb: "students" (plural)
- Correct verb: **"were"**

> *Neither the students nor the teacher **was** present.*
- Subjects: "students" (plural), "teacher" (singular)
- Closest to verb: "teacher" (singular)
- Correct verb: **"was"**

> *Either the manager or the employees **are** responsible.*
- Closest to verb: "employees" (plural)
- Correct verb: **"are"**

> *Either the employees or the manager **is** responsible.*
- Closest to verb: "manager" (singular)
- Correct verb: **"is"**

### Contrast with "Neither...nor" as Indefinite Pronouns

When "neither" and "either" are used as indefinite pronouns (not joining two nouns), they are always singular:

> *Neither **is** correct.* (singular)
> *Either **is** acceptable.* (singular)

---

## 3.12 Compound Subjects with "And"

### The Rule

**Subjects joined by "and" are almost always PLURAL and take a plural verb.**

> *The cat and the dog **are** playing in the yard.*
> *Reading, writing, and arithmetic **are** taught in elementary school.*
> *Salt and pepper **are** on the table.*

### The Exception: Compound Subjects Referring to a Single Entity

When two nouns joined by "and" refer to a single person, thing, or concept, the subject is singular.

> *The founder and CEO of the company **is** stepping down.* (One person holds both roles → singular)
> *Macaroni and cheese **is** my favorite dish.* (One dish → singular)
> *Bread and butter **is** served with every meal.* (One concept → singular)
> *Law and order **is** essential for society.* (One concept → singular)
> *The long and short of it **is** that we failed.* (One concept → singular)

**How to determine if a compound subject is singular:** Ask yourself whether the two nouns refer to the same person/thing or to a single unified concept. If yes, singular. If they refer to separate entities, plural.

> *The founder and the CEO **are** two different people.* (Two separate people → plural)
> *The founder and CEO **is** the same person.* (One person → singular)

---

## 3.13 Titles, Amounts, and Distances as Singular Subjects

### The Rule

**Titles of works, amounts of money, distances, periods of time, and quantities treated as a single unit are SINGULAR, even if they contain plural words.**

### Examples

> *"The Canterbury Tales" **is** a classic of English literature.* (One work → singular)
> *Fifty dollars **is** too much to pay for a sandwich.* (One amount → singular)
> *Three miles **is** a long distance to walk.* (One distance → singular)
> *Two weeks **is** a standard vacation period.* (One period → singular)
> *Ten kilograms **is** the maximum weight allowed.* (One quantity → singular)
> *The United States **is** a large country.* (One country → singular)
> *Mathematics **is** required for all engineering students.* (One subject → singular)
> *The news **is** broadcast at 6 PM.* (One broadcast → singular)
> *Physics **is** the study of matter and energy.* (One field → singular)

### When Amounts Are Plural

If the emphasis is on the individual units rather than the total amount, the subject can be plural:

> *The fifty dollars **were** scattered across the floor.* (Emphasizing individual bills → plural)

However, this is rare on the SAT. In virtually all SAT contexts, amounts, distances, and titles are singular.

---

## 3.14 Latin and Greek Plurals

### The Rule

**Nouns borrowed from Latin and Greek often have irregular plural forms. The SAT occasionally tests these, particularly "data," "criteria," and "phenomena."**

### Common Latin/Greek Plurals

| Singular | Plural | Meaning |
|---|---|---|
| criterion | criteria | standards for judgment |
| phenomenon | phenomena | observable events |
| datum | data | individual facts/pieces of information |
| medium | media | means of communication/expression |
| bacterium | bacteria | microorganisms |
| curriculum | curricula | courses of study |
| memorandum | memoranda | written messages |
| stratum | strata | layers |
| analysis | analyses | examinations |
| thesis | theses | arguments/essays |
| crisis | crises | emergencies |
| hypothesis | hypotheses | proposed explanations |
| appendix | appendices | supplementary material |
| index | indices | (mathematical) indicators |
| vertex | vertices | (mathematical) points |

### Examples

> *The data **support/supports** the hypothesis.* (Data = plural → **support**)
> *The criteria **are/is** clearly stated in the rubric.* (Criteria = plural → **are**)
> *The phenomena **are/is** difficult to explain.* (Phenomena = plural → **are**)
> *The media **has/have** covered the event extensively.* (Media = plural → **have**)

**Note on "data":** In formal academic writing and on the SAT, "data" is treated as plural. In everyday speech, many people treat it as singular ("the data shows"), but the SAT follows the formal convention: "data" is plural.

---

## 3.15 Nouns with Identical Singular and Plural Forms

### The Rule

**Some nouns have the same form in both singular and plural. Context determines the number.**

### Common Unchanged Nouns

| Noun | Example (Singular) | Example (Plural) |
|---|---|---|
| **sheep** | The sheep **is** grazing. | The sheep **are** grazing. |
| **deer** | A deer **was** spotted. | Three deer **were** spotted. |
| **fish** | One fish **is** in the tank. | Five fish **are** in the tank. |
| **species** | This species **is** endangered. | Many species **are** endangered. |
| **series** | The series **is** popular. | Two series **are** airing. |
| **aircraft** | The aircraft **is** ready. | All aircraft **are** grounded. |
| **offspring** | The offspring **is** healthy. | The offspring **are** healthy. |
| **means** | One means **is** sufficient. | All means **are** acceptable. |

### Strategy

When you encounter one of these nouns, look for context clues — articles ("a," "the"), numbers ("one," "three," "many"), and quantifiers ("each," "every") — to determine whether the noun is singular or plural.

---

## 3.16 Gerunds and Infinitive Phrases as Subjects

### The Rule

**When a gerund (-ing form used as a noun) or an infinitive phrase serves as the subject, it is ALWAYS singular and takes a singular verb.**

### Gerund Subjects

> *Swimming **is** an excellent form of exercise.* (singular)
> *Reading classic novels **improves** vocabulary.* (singular)
> *Running marathons **requires** extensive training.* (singular)
> *Learning a second language **opens** many doors.* (singular)

### Infinitive Phrase Subjects

> *To err **is** human; to forgive, divine.* (singular)
> *To master a musical instrument **takes** years of practice.* (singular)
> *To travel the world **is** her greatest dream.* (singular)

### Compound Gerund Subjects

When two gerunds are joined by "and," the subject becomes plural (because it refers to two activities):

> *Swimming and running **are** both excellent cardiovascular exercises.* (plural — two activities)

But when two gerunds form a single concept, the subject remains singular:

> *Slow and steady **wins** the race.* (One concept → singular — though this is more idiomatic)

---

## 3.17 Relative Pronouns as Subjects: "Who," "Which," "That"

### The Rule

**When "who," "which," or "that" is the subject of a relative clause, the verb inside the clause agrees with the antecedent (the noun the pronoun refers to).**

This is different from the main clause SVA. Inside a relative clause, the relative pronoun takes its number from the noun it modifies.

### Examples

> *The student who **is** studying hard will succeed.* ("who" = "student" → singular → "is")
> *The students who **are** studying hard will succeed.* ("who" = "students" → plural → "are")
> *The book, which **is** on the table, is mine.* ("which" = "book" → singular → "is")
> *The books, which **are** on the table, are mine.* ("which" = "books" → plural → "are")
> *The car that **was** parked outside has been towed.* ("that" = "car" → singular → "was")
> *The cars that **were** parked outside have been towed.* ("that" = "cars" → plural → "were")

### The SAT Trap

The SAT may test SVA within a relative clause, and the trap is the same: a plural noun between the relative pronoun and the verb.

> *The report that addresses the issues of cost, efficiency, and sustainability **has/have** been published.*

- Inside the relative clause: "that addresses the issues of cost, efficiency, and sustainability"
- "that" = "report" (singular)
- "of cost, efficiency, and sustainability" → prepositional phrase → eliminate
- Verb inside relative clause: **"addresses"** (singular — correct as written)

---

## 3.18 "One of the + Plural Noun + Who/That/Which" Construction

### The Rule

**In the construction "one of the + plural noun + who/that/which," the verb inside the relative clause is usually PLURAL because "who/that/which" refers to the plural noun, not "one."**

### Examples

> *She is one of the students who **have** perfect attendance.*
- "who" refers to "students" (plural) → plural verb "have"
- Meaning: Among the students who have perfect attendance, she is one.

> *It is one of the books that **were** published last year.*
- "that" refers to "books" (plural) → plural verb "were"

> *He is one of the few people who **are** qualified for this position.*
- "who" refers to "people" (plural) → plural verb "are"

### The Exception: "The Only One of the + Plural Noun"

When "one" is preceded by "the only," the relative pronoun refers to "one" (singular), not the plural noun:

> *She is the only one of the students who **has** perfect attendance.*
- "who" refers to "the only one" (singular) → singular verb "has"
- Meaning: Among all the students, she alone has perfect attendance.

This distinction — "one of the" (plural verb in relative clause) vs. "the only one of the" (singular verb in relative clause) — is tested on the SAT and is a reliable differentiator between high-scoring and low-scoring students.

---

## 3.19 "More Than One" and "Many a" — Illogical Singular

### The Rule

**Despite their logical meaning (which implies plurality), the phrases "more than one" and "many a" take SINGULAR verbs.**

This is a quirk of English grammar where form overrides logic.

### Examples

> *More than one student **has** complained about the policy.* (singular verb)
> *More than one factor **contributes** to the problem.* (singular verb)
> *Many a student **has** struggled with this concept.* (singular verb)
> *Many a time **has** this strategy proven effective.* (singular verb)

**Note:** "More than one" takes a singular noun after it ("more than one student," not "more than one students"), and a singular verb. "Many a" also takes a singular noun and a singular verb.

---

## 3.20 Fractions and Percentages: It Depends on the "Of" Phrase

### The Rule

**When a fraction or percentage is the subject, the verb agrees with the noun in the "of" phrase that follows it.**

### Examples

> *Two-thirds of the students **are** present.* ("students" = plural → plural verb)
> *Two-thirds of the cake **is** gone.* ("cake" = singular → singular verb)
> *Fifty percent of the budget **has** been allocated.* ("budget" = singular → singular verb)
> *Fifty percent of the employees **have** agreed to the terms.* ("employees" = plural → plural verb)
> *One-third of the building **was** destroyed.* ("building" = singular → singular verb)
> *One-third of the buildings **were** destroyed.* ("buildings" = plural → plural verb)

### "The Rest of" and "Some of"

The same rule applies:

> *The rest of the cake **is** in the fridge.* (singular)
> *The rest of the cookies **are** in the jar.* (plural)
> *Some of the water **has** evaporated.* (singular — uncountable)
> *Some of the guests **have** arrived.* (plural)

---

## 3.21 "All," "None," "Some," "Any," "Most" — The Indefinite Pronoun Chameleons

### The Rule

**These indefinite pronouns can be singular or plural depending on the noun they refer to (usually found in the "of" phrase).**

### Examples

> *All of the information **is** confidential.* ("information" = uncountable/singular → singular verb)
> *All of the students **are** present.* ("students" = plural → plural verb)
> *None of the cake **was** eaten.* ("cake" = singular → singular verb)
> *None of the candidates **were** qualified.* ("candidates" = plural → plural verb)
> *Some of the evidence **is** compelling.* ("evidence" = uncountable/singular → singular verb)
> *Some of the reports **are** incomplete.* ("reports" = plural → plural verb)
> *Most of the damage **was** repaired.* ("damage" = uncountable/singular → singular verb)
> *Most of the houses **were** damaged.* ("houses" = plural → plural verb)

### "None": A Special Case

Traditionally, "none" was taught as always singular ("none of the students is..."). In modern usage and on the SAT, "none" can be either singular or plural depending on context. If the noun in the "of" phrase is plural, "none" is typically treated as plural. If the noun is singular or uncountable, "none" is singular. The SAT will not test the singular/plural ambiguity of "none" in a way that creates confusion — the context will make the correct answer clear.

---

## 3.22 "Every" and "Each" with Compound Subjects

### The Rule

**When "every" or "each" precedes a compound subject (two nouns joined by "and"), the subject is SINGULAR.**

### Examples

> *Every boy and girl **is** welcome to participate.* (singular — "every" distributes individually to each member)
> *Each book and magazine **has** been catalogued.* (singular — "each" distributes individually)
> *Every man and woman **deserves** equal treatment.* (singular)

The logic is that "every" and "each" emphasize individual members of the group, so the subject is conceptually singular even though it contains "and."

---

## 3.23 SVA in Subordinate Clauses: Don't Get Confused

### The Rule

**Subject-verb agreement applies independently within each clause. The verb in the main clause agrees with the subject of the main clause. The verb in the subordinate clause agrees with the subject of the subordinate clause.**

### Examples

> *The results suggest that the hypothesis **is** supported by the data.*
- Main clause: "The results suggest" (plural subject "results" → plural verb "suggest")
- Subordinate clause: "that the hypothesis is supported" (singular subject "hypothesis" → singular verb "is")

> *The professor who teaches the courses **is** retiring.*
- Main clause: "The professor...is retiring" (singular subject "professor" → singular verb "is")
- Relative clause: "who teaches the courses" (singular antecedent "professor" → singular verb "teaches")

> *The students who **are** enrolled in the advanced course **have** a strong background in mathematics.*
- Main clause: "The students...have" (plural subject "students" → plural verb "have")
- Relative clause: "who are enrolled" (plural antecedent "students" → plural verb "are")

---

## 3.24 The Complete SVA Decision Tree

When you encounter an SVA question on the SAT, follow this decision tree:

```
START: Identify the verb being tested
│
├─ Does the sentence begin with "There" or "Here"?
│   ├─ YES → The subject comes AFTER the verb. Find it and match.
│   └─ NO → Continue.
│
├─ Is the sentence a question?
│   ├─ YES → The subject may be in the middle of the verb phrase or after it.
│   │         Convert to a statement to find the subject.
│   └─ NO → Continue.
│
├─ Is there a "who," "which," or "that" between the subject and verb?
│   ├─ YES → You are inside a relative clause. Eliminate the relative clause.
│   │         The subject is in the main clause.
│   └─ NO → Continue.
│
├─ Are there commas, dashes, or parentheses setting off a phrase?
│   ├─ YES → Eliminate the nonessential clause. The subject is outside it.
│   └─ NO → Continue.
│
├─ Are there prepositional phrases between the subject and verb?
│   ├─ YES → Eliminate all prepositional phrases. The subject is outside them.
│   └─ NO → Continue.
│
├─ Is the subject a collective noun?
│   ├─ YES → Usually SINGULAR (unless members act individually).
│   └─ NO → Continue.
│
├─ Is the subject an indefinite pronoun (each, every, everyone, etc.)?
│   ├─ YES → SINGULAR.
│   └─ NO → Continue.
│
├─ Is the subject joined by "and"?
│   ├─ YES → Usually PLURAL (unless it refers to a single entity).
│   └─ NO → Continue.
│
├─ Is the subject joined by "or" or "nor"?
│   ├─ YES → Apply the PROXIMITY RULE (match the nearest subject).
│   └─ NO → Continue.
│
├─ Is the subject a fraction, percentage, or "all/none/some/most"?
│   ├─ YES → Match the noun in the "of" phrase.
│   └─ NO → Continue.
│
└─ Identify the subject. Is it singular or plural?
    ├─ SINGULAR → Choose the verb ending in "S" (or "was," "has," "is").
    └─ PLURAL → Choose the verb NOT ending in "S" (or "were," "have," "are").
```

---

## 3.25 The Most Common SAT SVA Traps: A Summary

| Trap | Example | Wrong Answer | Correct Answer | Why |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| **Prepositional phrase interrupter** | The box of chocolates ___ on the table. | are (matches "chocolates") | is (matches "box") | Subject is never in a prepositional phrase |
| **"Along with" trap** | The CEO, along with the board, ___ attending. | are (matches "board") | is (matches "CEO") | "Along with" ≠ "and" |
| **Relative clause trap** | The results that ___ significant were published. | is (matches "that" as singular) | are (matches "results") | "That" takes number from antecedent |
| **"There is/are" inversion** | There ___ three reasons for the delay. | is (default singular) | are (matches "reasons") | Subject follows verb in "there" constructions |
| **Indefinite pronoun trap** | Each of the students ___ a textbook. | have (matches "students") | has (matches "Each") | "Each" is always singular |
| **"A number of" vs. "The number of"** | A number of students ___ protested. | has (treating it as singular) | have ("a number of" = plural) | Fixed distinction |
| **Collective noun trap** | The team ___ playing well. | are (treating it as plural) | is (collective = singular) | Collective nouns are singular on the SAT |
| **Latin/Greek plural** | The data ___ the hypothesis. | supports (treating as singular) | support ("data" = plural) | Latin plurals take plural verbs |
| **"One of the + who" trap** | She is one of the students who ___ perfect attendance. | has (matches "one") | have (matches "students") | "Who" refers to the plural noun |
| **"The only one of the + who" trap** | She is the only one of the students who ___ perfect attendance. | have (matches "students") | has (matches "the only one") | "The only" changes the reference |
| **Compound subject = single entity** | The founder and CEO ___ stepping down. | are (two roles) | is (one person) | Same person = singular |
| **Fraction/percentage** | Two-thirds of the cake ___ gone. | are (matches "two-thirds") | is (matches "cake") | Match the "of" noun |
| **Gerund subject** | Swimming marathons ___ endurance. | require (treating as plural) | requires (gerund = singular) | Gerunds are always singular |
| **"More than one"** | More than one student ___ complained. | have (logical plural) | has (grammatical singular) | Form overrides logic |
| **"Every/each" + compound** | Every boy and girl ___ welcome. | are (compound = plural) | is (every distributes individually) | "Every" makes it singular |

---

## 3.26 Advanced SVA: Sentences with Multiple Layers of Interruption

The SAT's most challenging SVA questions combine multiple types of interrupters between the subject and verb. The key is to eliminate ALL interrupters systematically, one layer at a time.

### Example 1: Multiple Layers

> *The analysis of the trends in consumer behavior, which was conducted by the research team at the university, **has/have** revealed significant shifts in purchasing patterns.*

**Layer-by-layer elimination:**
1. "of the trends" → prepositional phrase → eliminate
2. "in consumer behavior" → prepositional phrase → eliminate
3. ", which was conducted by the research team at the university," → relative clause → eliminate
4. What remains: "The analysis...has revealed"
5. Subject: "analysis" (singular) → Verb: **"has"**

### Example 2: Multiple Layers with Collective Noun

> *The committee of executives from the various departments, along with the external consultants who were hired specifically for this project, **is/are** expected to deliver its report by Friday.*

**Layer-by-layer elimination:**
1. "of executives" → prepositional phrase → eliminate
2. "from the various departments" → prepositional phrase → eliminate
3. ", along with the external consultants" → comma clause / "along with" phrase → eliminate
4. "who were hired specifically for this project" → relative clause → eliminate
5. What remains: "The committee...is expected"
6. Subject: "committee" (collective noun, singular) → Verb: **"is"**

### Example 3: Inversion + Interrupter

> *There **remains/remain** a number of unresolved issues between the two parties, including disputes over territory, trade agreements, and the status of refugees.*

**Layer-by-layer elimination:**
1. "There" → inversion signal → subject comes after verb
2. "a number of unresolved issues" → subject = "a number of" → plural
3. "between the two parties" → prepositional phrase → eliminate
4. ", including disputes over territory, trade agreements, and the status of refugees" → comma clause → eliminate
5. Subject: "a number of unresolved issues" → plural → Verb: **"remain"**

---

## 3.27 SVA with "Do/Does" and "Don't/Doesn't"

### The Rule

When SVA is tested with the auxiliary verbs "do/does" or their negative forms "don't/doesn't," the same rules apply. "Do" and "don't" are plural (and first/second person singular). "Does" and "doesn't" are third-person singular.

### Examples

> *The students **don't** understand the material.* (plural subject → "don't")
> *The student **doesn't** understand the material.* (singular subject → "doesn't")
> ***Does** the data support the hypothesis?* (plural subject "data" → "do" — but wait: "data" is plural, so it should be "**Do** the data support the hypothesis?")
> ***Does** the analysis confirm the theory?* (singular subject "analysis" → "does")
> *The results **do** not **support** the conclusion.* (plural subject → "do not support")
> *The result **does** not **support** the conclusion.* (singular subject → "does not support")

### "So/Neither/Nor" Inversion

When "so," "neither," or "nor" is used to express agreement, the subject-verb order is inverted, and the auxiliary verb must agree with the subject:

> *The data support the hypothesis, and so **do** the observations.* (plural "observations" → "do")
> *The analysis doesn't confirm the theory, and neither **does** the simulation.* (singular "simulation" → "does")
> *The students were present, and so **was** the teacher.* (singular "teacher" → "was")

---

## 3.28 SVA in Conditional and Subjunctive Sentences

### The Rule

SVA applies in conditional and subjunctive sentences just as it does in any other sentence. The subjunctive mood (used for hypothetical or contrary-to-fact statements) has its own verb forms, but the subject-verb agreement principle remains the same.

### Subjunctive Examples

> *If I **were** you, I would reconsider.* (subjunctive "were" for all subjects in hypothetical "if" clauses)
> *If he **were** eligible, he would apply.* (subjunctive "were" — not "was")
> *I wish she **were** here.* (subjunctive "were")
> *The committee recommended that the policy **be** revised.* (subjunctive "be" — not "is")
> *It is essential that every student **submit** the assignment on time.* (subjunctive "submit" — not "submits")

**Note:** In the subjunctive mood, the verb form is the same for all subjects (e.g., "be," "were," "submit," "have"). This means SVA is not tested in the traditional sense within subjunctive constructions — the verb form doesn't change based on the subject. However, you should still recognize subjunctive constructions so you don't misidentify the subject.

---

## 3.29 Quick-Reference SVA Checklist

When you encounter an SVA question, run through this checklist:

- [ ] **Have I identified the verb being tested?**
- [ ] **Have I found the TRUE subject (not just the nearest noun)?**
- [ ] **Have I eliminated all prepositional phrases between the subject and verb?**
- [ ] **Have I eliminated all relative clauses (who, which, that)?**
- [ ] **Have I eliminated all comma clauses (appositives, nonessential modifiers)?**
- [ ] **Have I checked for "There/Here" inversion?**
- [ ] **Have I checked for question inversion?**
- [ ] **Have I checked for collective nouns (singular by default)?**
- [ ] **Have I checked for indefinite pronouns (each, every, everyone = singular)?**
- [ ] **Have I checked for "a number of" (plural) vs. "the number of" (singular)?**
- [ ] **Have I checked for "neither/nor" and "either/or" (proximity rule)?**
- [ ] **Have I checked for compound subjects with "and" (usually plural)?**
- [ ] **Have I checked for Latin/Greek plurals (data, criteria, phenomena)?**
- [ ] **Have I checked for fractions/percentages (match the "of" noun)?**
- [ ] **Have I checked for gerund/infinitive subjects (always singular)?**
- [ ] **Have I checked for "one of the + who" (plural) vs. "the only one of the + who" (singular)?**
- [ ] **Have I checked for "more than one" (grammatically singular)?**
- [ ] **Have I matched the verb to the TRUE subject, not to any interrupter?**

---

## 3.30 Common Student Mistakes and How to Avoid Them

### Mistake 1: Matching the verb to the nearest noun instead of the true subject.

**How to avoid:** Always eliminate prepositional phrases, relative clauses, and comma clauses before matching the verb to the subject. The nearest noun is almost never the subject on difficult SAT questions.

### Mistake 2: Treating "along with," "as well as," "in addition to," "together with," and "accompanied by" as equivalent to "and."

**How to avoid:** Memorize this: only "and" creates compound (plural) subjects. All other connecting phrases leave the subject unchanged in number.

### Mistake 3: Using a singular verb after "there" without checking the subject.

**How to avoid:** In "there is/are" constructions, always look past the verb to find the true subject. If the true subject is plural, use "are," "were," "have," etc.

### Mistake 4: Treating collective nouns as plural.

**How to avoid:** On the SAT, collective nouns (team, committee, family, group, etc.) are almost always singular. Use a singular verb unless the sentence clearly indicates that the members are acting individually (which is rare on the SAT).

### Mistake 5: Forgetting that "each" and "every" are always singular, even when followed by "of the + plural noun."

**How to avoid:** Memorize the list of always-singular indefinite pronouns. When you see "each" or "every," the verb is singular, period.

### Mistake 6: Confusing "a number of" (plural) with "the number of" (singular).

**How to avoid:** This is a fixed distinction. "A number of" = several = plural. "The number of" = the count = singular.

### Mistake 7: Applying the proximity rule to "and" instead of "or/nor."

**How to avoid:** "And" always creates a plural subject (with the single-entity exception). The proximity rule applies ONLY to "neither...nor" and "either...or."

### Mistake 8: Treating "data," "criteria," and "phenomena" as singular.

**How to avoid:** These are plural forms. "Data" takes a plural verb. "Criteria" takes a plural verb. "Phenomena" takes a plural verb. Their singular forms are "datum," "criterion," and "phenomenon."

### Mistake 9: Overlooking SVA errors in relative clauses.

**How to avoid:** Remember that SVA applies within relative clauses too. The verb inside a relative clause must agree with the antecedent of the relative pronoun.

### Mistake 10: Assuming that a noun ending in "s" is always plural.

**How to avoid:** Many singular nouns end in "s" (news, mathematics, physics, economics, measles, billiards, athletics). Conversely, many plural nouns do NOT end in "s" (children, people, men, women, mice, teeth, feet, data, criteria, phenomena). Always determine the actual number of the subject, not just the spelling.

---

## 3.31 The Psychology of SVA Traps: Why the SAT Designs Questions This Way

Understanding *why* the SAT designs SVA questions the way it does will help you anticipate traps and avoid them. The College Board's test writers follow specific principles when constructing SVA questions:

### Principle 1: Distance
The farther the subject is from the verb, the more likely you are to make an error. The SAT maximizes this distance by inserting long phrases between the subject and verb.

### Principle 2: Proximity of a Distractor Noun
The SAT places a noun with the *opposite* number from the true subject as close to the verb as possible. If the true subject is singular, the nearest noun to the verb will be plural, and vice versa.

### Principle 3: Multiple Interrupters
The most difficult SVA questions use two or three types of interrupters simultaneously (e.g., a prepositional phrase + a relative clause + a comma clause).

### Principle 4: Familiar Words in Unfamiliar Roles
The SAT uses words that students know in everyday contexts but may not recognize in grammatical contexts. For example, students know "data" as a casual singular ("the data shows") but need to recognize it as formal plural ("the data show").

### Principle 5: The "Sounds Right" Trap
Many SVA errors sound natural in spoken English. The SAT exploits the gap between conversational English and formal written English. If you are relying on what "sounds right," you will fall for traps.

### How to Counter These Principles
- **Always use the isolation method.** Do not rely on intuition.
- **Always eliminate interrupters before matching subject and verb.**
- **Always double-check the number of the true subject, not the nearest noun.**
- **Always apply formal written English rules, not conversational patterns.**

---

## 3.32 Summary Table: All SVA Rules at a Glance

| Rule | Singular | Plural | Example |
|---|---|---|---|
| Basic pattern | Subject without -s → verb with -s | Subject with -s → verb without -s | "The dog runs" / "The dogs run" |
| Prepositional phrases | Eliminate — subject is never inside | Eliminate — subject is never inside | "The box **is**..." (not "chocolates are") |
| "And" compound subjects | Single entity | Separate entities | "Macaroni and cheese **is**..." / "The cat and dog **are**..." |
| "Or/Nor" (proximity rule) | Match nearest subject | Match nearest subject | "Neither the teacher nor the students **were**..." |
| "Along with," etc. | Subject before phrase determines number | Subject before phrase determines number | "The CEO, along with the board, **is**..." |
| Collective nouns | Usually singular | Rarely plural | "The team **is**..." |
| Indefinite pronouns | each, every, everyone, someone, etc. | — | "Everyone **has**..." |
| "A number of" | — | Plural | "A number of students **have**..." |
| "The number of" | Singular | — | "The number of students **is**..." |
| "There/Here" inversion | Subject after verb | Subject after verb | "There **are** three reasons..." |
| Questions | Subject may be mid-verb | Subject may be mid-verb | "What **are** the reasons?" |
| Gerund/infinitive subjects | Always singular | — | "Swimming **is**..." |
| Fractions/percentages | Match "of" noun | Match "of" noun | "Two-thirds of the cake **is**..." |
| Latin/Greek plurals | datum, criterion, phenomenon | data, criteria, phenomena | "The data **support**..." |
| "One of the + who" | — | Plural verb in relative clause | "one of the students who **have**..." |
| "The only one of the + who" | Singular verb in relative clause | — | "the only one of the students who **has**..." |
| "More than one" | Grammatically singular | — | "More than one student **has**..." |
| "Every/each" + compound | Singular | — | "Every boy and girl **is**..." |
| Titles, amounts, distances | Singular (as a unit) | — | "Fifty dollars **is**..." |

---

This chapter has provided the most comprehensive treatment of subject-verb agreement available for SAT preparation. Every rule, every exception, every trap, and every strategy has been covered in exhaustive detail. Master this chapter, and you will never miss an SVA question on the SAT again. The key is not memorization alone — it is the disciplined, mechanical application of the isolation method on every single SVA question you encounter, regardless of how simple or complex the sentence appears.

---
