# Chapter 4: Reference Document Section 1 (Continued from Chapter 3)

---

## 1. Sentence Structure & Completeness — Expanded Theory

### 1.1 Requirements for a Complete Sentence — An Exhaustive Breakdown

A complete sentence — also called an **independent clause (IC)** — is the foundational unit of all written English. On the SAT Writing and Language section, the College Board tests your ability to recognize, construct, and repair independent clauses with relentless precision. Before we can even begin discussing punctuation, transitions, or rhetorical strategy, you must internalize what constitutes a complete sentence down to the most granular level.

#### The Two Non-Negotiable Components

Every independent clause must contain, at a bare minimum:

**1. A Subject** — The subject is the noun, pronoun, or noun phrase that performs the action of the verb, or about which something is stated. The subject answers the question "Who or what is this sentence about?"

- **Simple subject:** The core noun or pronoun (e.g., "The **scientist** published her findings.")
- **Complete subject:** The simple subject plus all its modifiers (e.g., "The **scientist who had spent decades researching coral reef degradation** published her findings.")
- **Compound subject:** Two or more subjects joined by "and" or other coordinating elements (e.g., "The scientist **and** her assistant published their findings.")

**2. A Verb** — The verb expresses the action, occurrence, or state of being of the subject. Without a verb, a group of words is merely a collection of words — it cannot function as a sentence.

- **Action verb:** Expresses a visible or invisible action (e.g., "The scientist **published** her findings.")
- **Linking verb:** Connects the subject to a subject complement; does not express action (e.g., "The results **were** groundbreaking." / "The data **seems** conclusive.")
- **Helping (auxiliary) verb:** Works with a main verb to create a verb phrase (e.g., "The team **has been studying** the phenomenon for years.")

#### The "Complete Thought" Requirement

Beyond having a subject and a verb, an independent clause must also express a **complete thought**. This is a subtle but critical distinction. Consider:

- "The scientist published." — Has a subject ("The scientist") and a verb ("published"), but in many contexts, it feels incomplete because the reader wants to know *what* was published. However, grammatically, this IS a complete sentence because the verb does not require an object.
- "Because the scientist published her findings." — Has a subject ("the scientist") and a verb ("published"), but the subordinating conjunction "because" makes it dependent. It does not express a complete thought; it leaves the reader hanging, waiting for the main clause.

The SAT will frequently test whether you can distinguish between a dependent clause and an independent clause. Remember: a dependent clause will begin with a subordinating conjunction (because, although, when, while, if, since, after, before, unless, until, whereas, whenever, wherever, even though, provided that, as long as, in order that, so that) or a relative pronoun (who, whom, whose, which, that, where, when, whoever, whomever, whatever, whichever). Any clause that begins with one of these words AND contains a subject and verb is automatically a dependent clause — it cannot stand alone as a sentence.

### 1.2 The Three "Useless" (Non-Essential) Clauses — Mastering the Art of Elimination

One of the most powerful strategies for SAT Writing is the ability to identify and mentally **cross out** portions of a sentence that are irrelevant to the grammatical structure at hand. There are three types of clauses that can be removed while the sentence remains grammatically complete. Let us examine each with extraordinary depth.

#### Type A: Relative Clauses

A relative clause is a dependent clause that modifies a noun and begins with a **relative pronoun**: who, whom, whose, which, or that.

**Critical SAT Distinction:**

- **"That" introduces a restrictive (essential) clause** — NO commas are used before or after "that."
  - Example: "The book **that won the Pulitzer Prize** is on the bestseller list."
  - Here, "that won the Pulitzer Prize" identifies WHICH book. It is essential. No commas.

- **"Which" introduces a nonrestrictive (nonessential) clause on the SAT** — it is preceded by a comma.
  - Example: "The novel, **which won the Pulitzer Prize last year**, is already on the bestseller list."
  - Here, "which won the Pulitzer Prize last year" adds extra information about a novel that has already been identified. It is nonessential. Use commas.

**Important Nuance:** In everyday English, "which" is sometimes used for restrictive clauses. On the SAT, however, the test-makers are strict: "that" = restrictive (no commas), "which" = nonrestrictive (comma required). This is one of the most frequently tested distinctions on the exam. If you see "which" without a comma on the SAT, it is almost certainly incorrect.

**Test Strategy:** When you encounter a relative clause, you can cross it out mentally to isolate the core subject-verb structure. Example:

> "The architect, **who had previously designed several award-winning museums in Europe**, presented her plans to the city committee."

Cross out the relative clause: "The architect presented her plans to the city committee." Now you can easily verify subject-verb agreement (singular "architect" → singular "presented").

#### Type B: Comma Clause (Three Subtypes)

A comma clause is any clause or phrase that is set off from the rest of the sentence by commas. These are also called **parenthetical elements** because they could theoretically be placed in parentheses. There are three positions where comma clauses appear:

**Sub-Type B1: Introductory Comma Clause (ICC)**

- **Position:** At the beginning of the sentence, before the main clause.
- **Structure:** Begins with a subordinating conjunction (although, because, when, if, since, after, before, while, unless, etc.), a preposition (in, on, at, for, with, by, during, through, etc.), or an -ing word functioning as an adverb.
- **Punctuation Rule:** MUST be followed by a comma.
- **Modifier Rule:** The subject of the main clause MUST logically perform the action described in the ICC (this connects directly to dangling modifier rules).

Examples:
- "**Although the experiment yielded unexpected results**, the team remained optimistic." (subordinating conjunction)
- "**In the early hours of the morning**, the researchers collected their samples." (preposition)
- "**Walking along the shoreline**, the marine biologist discovered a new species." (-ing adverb)

**Sub-Type B2: Ending Comma Clause (ECC)**

- **Position:** At the end of the sentence, after the main clause.
- **Structure:** Often begins with an -ing word functioning as an adverb, providing additional information about the action in the main clause.
- **Punctuation Rule:** Usually preceded by a comma (though sometimes the comma is omitted if the clause is tightly bound to the main action — the SAT tests both scenarios).

Examples:
- "The team published their findings in a prestigious journal, **attracting widespread attention from the scientific community**."
- "The ancient manuscript was carefully restored, **revealing previously illegible text**."

**Sub-Type B3: Middle Comma Clause (MCC) / Appositive**

- **Position:** Interrupts the sentence, typically between the subject and the verb.
- **Structure:** Provides nonessential information — it can be removed without destroying the grammatical integrity of the sentence.
- **Punctuation Rule:** Must be set off by **matching punctuation** on BOTH sides. The three acceptable pairs are:
  - Two commas: "Tom, **usually very uncreative**, wrote a beautiful song."
  - Two dashes: "Tom — **usually very uncreative** — wrote a beautiful song."
  - Two parentheses: "Tom (**usually very uncreative**) wrote a beautiful song."
- **CRITICAL RULE:** NEVER mix punctuation types. You cannot open with a comma and close with a dash, or open with parentheses and close with a comma. The SAT WILL test this as an error.

**Special Note on Appositives (a subset of MCC):**
An appositive is a noun or noun phrase that renames or identifies another noun immediately beside it.

- Nonessential appositive (use commas): "Dr. Katherine Johnson, **a mathematician at NASA**, calculated the trajectory for the Apollo 11 mission." (We know who she is; the apposition adds information.)
- Essential appositive (NO commas): "The novelist **Toni Morrison** won the Nobel Prize in Literature." (Essential to identify WHICH novelist — there are many novelists, and we need the name to specify.)

### 1.3 Prepositional Phrases — The Subject's Never-Never Land

A prepositional phrase always begins with a preposition and ends with a noun (called the object of the preposition):

$$[\text{Preposition}] + [\text{Modifiers}] + [\text{Object of the Preposition}]$$

Common prepositions include: about, above, across, after, against, along, among, around, at, before, behind, below, beneath, beside, between, beyond, by, down, during, except, for, from, in, inside, into, like, near, of, off, on, onto, out, outside, over, past, since, through, throughout, to, toward, under, underneath, until, up, upon, with, within, without.

**THE GOLDEN RULE:** The subject of a sentence can **NEVER** be inside a prepositional phrase. This is perhaps the single most important subject-verb agreement rule on the entire SAT.

Example:
> "The basket **of apples** is on the table."

Subject: "The basket" (NOT "apples") → Singular verb: "is"

The word "of apples" is a prepositional phrase. Cross it out. The subject is "The basket" — singular — so the verb must be singular ("is"), even though the plural noun "apples" sits right next to it.

More Complex Example:
> "The recommendations **of the committee members appointed by the governor** **are** expected to be controversial."

1. Find the verb: "are expected"
2. Cross out the prepositional phrase: "of the committee members"
3. But wait — "appointed by the governor" looks like it might be the subject. No. "appointed" begins a participial phrase (a relative clause variant). "By the governor" is another prepositional phrase. Cross those out too.
4. True subject: "The recommendations" → Plural
5. Correct verb: "are"

This strategy of elimination — crossing out prepositional phrases, relative clauses, and comma clauses — is the backbone of every subject-verb agreement question on the SAT.

---

## 2. Subject-Verb Agreement (SVA) — Expanded Theory

### 2.1 The Fundamental Agreement Principle

Subject-verb agreement is governed by one principle: **the verb must match the subject in number.** If the subject is singular, the verb must be singular. If the subject is plural, the verb must be plural. Sounds simple, but the SAT makes it deceptively difficult by placing long, distracting phrases between the subject and verb.

### 2.2 The "S" Pattern — A Critical Observation

The English language has an almost perverse inversion when it comes to adding "s":

| | Singular | Plural |
|---|---|---|
| **Subject** | Usually does NOT end in "s" (cat, child, team) | Usually DOES end in "s" (cats, children, teams) |
| **Verb** | Usually DOES end in "s" (runs, eats, is) | Usually does NOT end in "s" (run, eat, are) |

Think of it this way: **Singular subjects take verbs with "s"; plural subjects take verbs without "s."** This is the opposite of how nouns work, and it is a source of constant confusion.

Examples:
- "The dog **runs**." (singular subject, singular verb with "s")
- "The dogs **run**." (plural subject with "s", plural verb without "s")
- "The child **is** happy." (singular subject, singular verb)
- "The children **are** happy." (plural subject, plural verb)

### 2.3 The "There Is / There Are" Construction

When a sentence begins with "There" (called an **existential there**), the subject comes AFTER the verb. This is one of the few situations in English where the subject follows the verb.

- "There **is** a book on the table." → Subject: "a book" (singular) → Verb: "is" (singular)
- "There **are** three books on the table." → Subject: "three books" (plural) → Verb: "are" (plural)
- "There **seems** to be a problem." → Subject: "a problem" (singular) → Verb: "seems" (singular)
- "There **seem** to be several problems." → Subject: "several problems" (plural) → Verb: "seem" (plural)

**Strategy:** When you see "There" at the beginning of a sentence, cross out "There" and everything up to the verb. Then identify the subject that follows the verb and match accordingly.

### 2.4 Indefinite Pronouns — The Singular Traps

Certain pronouns look like they might refer to multiple people or things, but they are grammatically **singular** and require singular verbs:

| Singular Indefinite Pronouns |
|---|
| each, every, either, neither |
| someone, somebody, something |
| anyone, anybody, anything |
| no one, nobody, nothing |
| everyone, everybody, everything |
| one, another, much |

Examples:
- "Each of the students **has** completed the assignment." (NOT "have")
- "Everyone in the room **was** silent." (NOT "were")
- "Neither of the options **is** acceptable." (NOT "are")

**The "Each/Every" Rule:** Even when "each" or "every" appears after a plural noun, it still takes a singular verb:
- "The students each **have** completed the assignment." → Here, "each" is an appositive, not the subject. The subject is "The students" (plural) → "have" (plural).
- "Each of the students **has** completed the assignment." → Here, "Each" IS the subject → "has" (singular).

The difference is whether "each" is the subject itself or an appositive modifying a plural subject.

### 2.5 Compound Subjects — When "And" Creates Plurality

When two or more subjects are joined by "and," the resulting compound subject is **almost always plural**:

- "The teacher **and** the student **are** in the room." (plural)
- "Bread **and** butter **is** my favorite snack." (EXCEPTION: when the two items form a single unit or concept, the subject is treated as singular)

**The "Neither...Nor" and "Either...Or" Proximity Rule:**

When subjects are joined by "neither...nor" or "either...or," the verb agrees with the subject **closest** to it:

- "Neither the teacher nor the students **were** present." (students = plural → were)
- "Neither the students nor the teacher **was** present." (teacher = singular → was)
- "Either the manager or the employees **are** responsible." (employees = plural → are)
- "Either the employees or the manager **is** responsible." (manager = singular → is)

### 2.6 Collective Nouns — Singular by Default

Collective nouns refer to a group of individuals acting as a single unit. On the SAT, these are treated as **singular** unless the context clearly indicates that the members of the group are acting individually:

| Collective Nouns |
|---|
| team, group, committee, jury, family, flock, herd, audience, class, government, staff, band, orchestra, council, panel, board, majority, minority, public, army, navy, fleet |

Examples:
- "The team **is** winning the championship." (acting as one unit → singular)
- "The committee **has** reached its decision." (acting as one unit → singular)
- "The jury **are** divided in their opinions." (rare — only when members act individually; the SAT almost always treats these as singular)

### 2.7 Amounts, Distances, Titles, and Units

When an amount, distance, time period, or title is treated as a **single unit**, it takes a **singular verb**:

- "Three miles **is** a long way to walk." (one distance)
- "Ten dollars **is** too much to pay." (one amount of money)
- "Five years **is** the maximum sentence." (one time period)
- "The Canterbury Tales **is** a classic of English literature." (one book)
- "Physics **is** my favorite subject." (one field of study)
- "The New York Times **is** a respected newspaper." (one publication)

### 2.8 Plural-Form Singular Nouns

Some nouns end in "s" but are singular in meaning:

| Noun | Number |
|---|---|
| news | singular |
| mathematics | singular |
| economics | singular |
| politics | singular (when referring to the field) |
| measles | singular |
| mumps | singular |
| billiards | singular |
| summons | singular |

Examples:
- "The news **is** alarming."
- "Mathematics **is** required for all engineering majors."

### 2.9 Data, Criteria, Phenomena, and Other Greek/Latin Plurals

The SAT occasionally tests words borrowed from Greek and Latin that have irregular plural forms:

| Singular | Plural |
|---|---|
| criterion | criteria |
| phenomenon | phenomena |
| datum | data |
| medium | media |
| bacterium | bacteria |
| curriculum | curricula |
| memorandum | memoranda |
| stratum | strata |
| analysis | analyses |
| hypothesis | hypotheses |
| thesis | theses |
| crisis | crises |
| appendix | appendices |
| index | indices (or indexes) |

On the SAT, "data" is treated as **plural** (though in everyday usage, it is increasingly accepted as singular). The SAT prefers the traditional plural treatment: "The data **support** the hypothesis" (NOT "supports").

### 2.10 The "A Number of" vs. "The Number of" Distinction

This is a classic SAT trap:

- **"A number of"** = "many" → **Plural verb**
  - "A number of students **have** already registered."
  - "A number of issues **remain** unresolved."

- **"The number of"** = a specific quantity → **Singular verb**
  - "The number of students **has** increased this year."
  - "The number of issues **is** staggering."

### 2.11 The "Along With" / "As Well As" / "In Addition To" Rule

When a subject is followed by phrases like "along with," "as well as," "in addition to," "together with," "accompanied by," or "including," these phrases do NOT create compound subjects. The verb agrees with the subject BEFORE these phrases:

- "The CEO, **along with** all the board members, **is** attending the conference." (CEO = singular → is)
- "The students, **as well as** their teacher, **are** going on the field trip." (students = plural → are)
- "The painting, **in addition to** the sculptures, **was** donated to the museum." (painting = singular → was)

These phrases function similarly to prepositional phrases — they add information but do not change the number of the subject.

### 2.12 The Complete SVA Process — Step by Step

When you encounter a subject-verb agreement question on the SAT, follow this exact process:

**Step 1:** Identify the verb in question. What is the verb that the answer choices are changing?

**Step 2:** Find the subject of that verb. Ask: "Who or what is performing this action?"

**Step 3:** Eliminate everything that is NOT the subject:
- Cross out all prepositional phrases
- Cross out all relative clauses
- Cross out all comma clauses (introductory, middle, and ending)
- Cross out any other modifiers

**Step 4:** Determine if the remaining subject is singular or plural.

**Step 5:** Match the verb to the subject's number.

**Step 6:** Check for special cases (indefinite pronouns, collective nouns, "there is/are," proximity rule, etc.).

---

## 3. Modifiers — Expanded Theory

### 3.1 Dangling Modifiers — The Complete Analysis

A **dangling modifier** occurs when a modifying phrase (usually at the beginning of a sentence) does not logically connect to the noun it is supposed to modify. The modifier "dangles" because the word it should describe is either missing from the sentence or is not the grammatical subject of the main clause.

#### The Rule in Full

When a sentence begins with an **introductory modifying phrase** (which may begin with a subordinating conjunction, a preposition, or an -ing word), the **subject of the main clause** — the noun immediately following the comma — MUST be the logical performer of the action described in the introductory phrase.

#### Why This Matters

The introductory phrase creates an expectation. The reader assumes that the noun right after the comma is the one performing the action. If that noun cannot logically perform the action, the modifier "dangles."

#### Classic Examples of Dangling Modifiers

**Example 1:**
> ✗ "Walking through the park, the trees were beautiful."

Who is walking through the park? The trees? Trees cannot walk. The modifier "Walking through the park" dangles because the subject of the main clause ("the trees") cannot logically perform the action of walking.

> ✓ "Walking through the park, **I** noticed the beautiful trees."
> ✓ "Walking through the park, **the visitor** admired the beautiful trees."

**Example 2:**
> ✗ "Having finished the assignment, the TV was turned on."

Who finished the assignment? The TV? A TV cannot finish an assignment. The modifier dangles.

> ✓ "Having finished the assignment, **Jenny** turned on the TV."

**Example 3:**
> ✗ "After studying for six hours, the exam seemed easier."

Who studied for six hours? The exam? An exam cannot study. The modifier dangles.

> ✓ "After studying for six hours, **Maria** found the exam easier."

**Example 4:**
> ✗ "To improve his score, the textbook was studied carefully."

Who is trying to improve his score? The textbook? A textbook cannot improve a score. The modifier dangles.

> ✓ "To improve his score, **David** studied the textbook carefully."

#### The Subordinating Conjunction Trap

When an introductory phrase begins with a subordinating conjunction (because, although, when, while, if, since, after, before, unless, etc.), the same rule applies. The subject after the comma must be able to perform the action:

> ✗ "Although exhausted from the journey, the presentation was delivered flawlessly."

Who was exhausted? The presentation? A presentation cannot be exhausted. The modifier dangles.

> ✓ "Although exhausted from the journey, **the speaker** delivered the presentation flawlessly."

#### The Prepositional Phrase Trap

> ✗ "With great enthusiasm, the speech was delivered to the crowd."

Who has great enthusiasm? The speech? A speech cannot have enthusiasm. The modifier dangles.

> ✓ "With great enthusiasm, **the orator** delivered the speech to the crowd."

#### The -ing Word Trap (Most Common on the SAT)

> ✗ "Running through the forest, the sunset was breathtaking."

Who is running? The sunset? A sunset cannot run. The modifier dangles.

> ✓ "Running through the forest, **the hiker** was struck by the breathtaking sunset."

### 3.2 Misplaced Modifiers — The Complete Analysis

A **misplaced modifier** occurs when a modifying word, phrase, or clause is separated from the word it is supposed to modify, creating confusion or an unintended (often humorous) meaning.

#### The Rule

A modifier should be placed **as close as possible** to the word it modifies. When a modifier is too far away, the reader may accidentally associate it with the wrong word.

#### Classic Examples of Misplaced Modifiers

**Example 1:**
> ✗ "The dog chased the ball wearing a red collar."

What is wearing a red collar? The ball? The modifier "wearing a red collar" is too far from "the dog."

> ✓ "The dog wearing a red collar chased the ball."
> ✓ "Wearing a red collar, the dog chased the ball."

**Example 2:**
> ✗ "She served sandwiches to the children on paper plates."

Were the children on paper plates? The modifier "on paper plates" is misplaced.

> ✓ "She served the children sandwiches on paper plates."
> ✓ "She served sandwiches on paper plates to the children."

**Example 3:**
> ✗ "The professor explained the theory to the students using complex equations."

Who is using complex equations? The students? Or the professor? The modifier is ambiguous.

> ✓ "Using complex equations, the professor explained the theory to the students."
> ✓ "The professor explained the theory, using complex equations, to the students."

#### The "Only," "Almost," "Nearly," "Just," "Even," "Hardly," "Merely" Trap

These **limiting modifiers** must be placed immediately before the word they modify. Their placement can completely change the meaning of a sentence:

- "**Only** John said he loved Mary." (Nobody else said it — only John.)
- "John **only** said he loved Mary." (He said it but didn't do anything else.)
- "John said **only** he loved Mary." (Nobody else loves Mary — only John.)
- "John said he **only** loved Mary." (He loves nobody else — only Mary.)
- "John said he loved **only** Mary." (Same as above — he loves only Mary.)

On the SAT, if a limiting modifier is placed in the wrong position, the answer is incorrect.

### 3.3 Correcting Modifier Errors — The Four Strategies

When you identify a modifier error on the SAT, there are four possible correction strategies:

**Strategy 1: Change the subject of the main clause.**
This is the most common fix for dangling modifiers. Replace the illogical subject with a noun that can logically perform the action.

> ✗ "After reading the novel, the movie was disappointing."
> ✓ "After reading the novel, **I** found the movie disappointing."

**Strategy 2: Reposition the modifier.**
Move the modifying phrase closer to the word it should describe.

> ✗ "The student found the book in the library that was overdue."
> ✓ "The student found the book that was overdue in the library."

**Strategy 3: Add a logical subject to the modifying phrase.**
Sometimes the logical performer of the action isn't in the sentence at all. In this case, you may need to add a subject and convert the phrase into a full clause.

> ✗ "While walking to school, the rain began to fall."
> ✓ "While **I was** walking to school, the rain began to fall."

**Strategy 4: Change the voice or structure of the sentence.**
Sometimes the entire sentence needs to be restructured to eliminate the modifier error.

> ✗ "To win the election, votes must be earned."
> ✓ "To win the election, **a candidate** must earn votes."

### 3.4 The Special Case: End-of-Sentence Participial Phrases

A sentence like "The magician walked across the stage, dazzling the crowd with card tricks" is CORRECT — "dazzling" modifies "the magician" even though it's placed near "the stage." Don't overanalyze when the describing phrase is NOT at the start of the sentence.

This is a crucial distinction. The dangling modifier rule applies **only** to introductory modifying phrases — those that appear at the beginning of the sentence, before the main clause. When a participial phrase appears at the END of a sentence, it is generally understood to modify the subject of the main clause, even if it's placed near another noun.

Examples of CORRECT end-of-sentence participial phrases:
- "The scientist examined the specimen, **using a high-powered microscope**." (The scientist is using the microscope.)
- "The children ran through the park, **laughing and shouting with joy**." (The children are laughing and shouting.)
- "The author signed copies of her book, **hoping to connect with her readers**." (The author is hoping.)

However, even end-of-sentence participial phrases can be problematic if they create genuine ambiguity:
- ✗ "The car hit the tree driving at high speed." (Who/what is driving at high speed? The car? The tree? This is ambiguous and should be revised.)
- ✓ "Driving at high speed, the car hit the tree."
- ✓ "The car, driving at high speed, hit the tree."

### 3.5 Modifier Questions on the SAT — What to Expect

The SAT tests modifier errors in several ways:

1. **Dangling modifier at the beginning of a sentence** — The most common type. The introductory phrase doesn't logically connect to the subject after the comma.

2. **Misplaced modifier** — A word or phrase is too far from what it modifies, creating confusion.

3. **Limiting modifier placement** — "Only," "almost," "nearly," etc. are in the wrong position.

4. **Ambiguous modifier** — It's unclear which word the modifier is supposed to describe.

5. **Correct modifier placement** — Sometimes the NO CHANGE answer is correct, and the sentence is fine as written. Don't assume every modifier question has an error.

---

## 4. Punctuation — Expanded Theory

### 4.1 Commas — The Eight Essential Rules (Deep Dive)

The comma is the most frequently tested punctuation mark on the SAT. Mastery of comma rules is non-negotiable for a high score. Let us examine each rule with exhaustive detail.

#### Rule 1: Before a FANBOYS Conjunction Joining Two Independent Clauses

**FANBOYS** = For, And, Nor, But, Or, Yet, So

When a coordinating conjunction joins two **independent clauses**, a comma must precede the conjunction:

> "The team practiced diligently all season, **and** they won the championship."

Both "The team practiced diligently all season" and "they won the championship" are independent clauses. The comma before "and" is required.

**CRITICAL DISTINCTION:** When the FANBOYS conjunction joins two elements that are NOT both independent clauses (e.g., two verbs with the same subject, two objects, two adjectives), NO comma is used:

> "The team practiced diligently all season **and** won the championship."

Here, "practiced" and "won" share the same subject ("The team"). This is a compound predicate, not two independent clauses. No comma before "and."

**Test:** Can each side of the FANBOYS conjunction stand alone as a complete sentence? If yes → comma required. If no → no comma.

#### Rule 2: After Introductory Elements

Any dependent clause, prepositional phrase, or transitional expression that precedes the main clause must be followed by a comma:

- "**Although the weather was terrible**, the event was well attended." (subordinating conjunction)
- "**In the middle of the night**, the phone rang." (prepositional phrase)
- "**Having completed the project**, the team celebrated." (-ing participial phrase)
- "**However**, the results were inconclusive." (transitional expression)
- "**To improve her score**, she hired a tutor." (infinitive phrase functioning as an adverb)

**Note:** Short prepositional phrases (typically fewer than 4-5 words) at the beginning of a sentence sometimes omit the comma in informal writing, but the SAT generally expects the comma to be present.

#### Rule 3: Around Non-Essential (Nonrestrictive) Clauses and Phrases

Information that can be removed without changing the core meaning of the sentence must be set off by commas:

> "The novel, **which was published in 1925**, remains a classic of American literature."

The clause "which was published in 1925" is non-essential — we already know which novel is being discussed (or the title was mentioned earlier). The commas signal that this is extra information.

Compare with:
> "The novel **that was published in 1925** remains a classic."

Here, "that was published in 1925" is essential — it identifies WHICH novel. No commas.

#### Rule 4: The Oxford Comma (Serial Comma)

In a list of three or more items, the SAT requires a comma before the final "and" or "or":

> "She bought apples, oranges, **and** bananas."

The comma after "oranges" (before "and") is the Oxford comma. The SAT consistently uses the Oxford comma in its correct answer choices. If an answer choice omits the Oxford comma, it is incorrect on the SAT.

**Why the Oxford comma matters:**
> "I admire my parents, Batman, and Wonder Woman."

Without the Oxford comma, this could be read as "I admire my parents, who ARE Batman and Wonder Woman." The Oxford comma eliminates this ambiguity.

#### Rule 5: Between Coordinate Adjectives

When two or more adjectives independently modify the same noun, they are called **coordinate adjectives** and must be separated by a comma:

> "She is a **talented, dedicated** teacher."

Test for coordinate adjectives:
1. Can you insert "and" between them? "talented and dedicated teacher" → Yes.
2. Can you reverse their order? "dedicated, talented teacher" → Yes.
If both tests pass, the adjectives are coordinate → use a comma.

**Contrast with cumulative adjectives** (which do NOT take a comma):
> "She wore a **bright red** dress."

Test:
1. "bright and red dress" → Awkward.
2. "red bright dress" → Incorrect.
These are cumulative adjectives → NO comma.

#### Rule 6: To Set Off Names, Titles, and Direct Address

When a name or title is used as a non-essential appositive, it is set off by commas:

> "My sister, **Emily**, is a doctor." (I have only one sister; her name is extra information.)
> "The author **Toni Morrison** won the Nobel Prize." (Essential — identifies which author.)

When directly addressing someone, use commas:
> "**John**, could you please close the door?"
> "I believe, **Senator**, that you are mistaken."

#### Rule 7: To Separate Elements in Dates and Geographic Addresses

- "The conference will be held on **July 15, 2026**, in **Chicago, Illinois**."
- "She was born on **March 3, 1990**."

Note: When only the month and year are given, no comma is needed: "She was born in **March 1990**."

#### Rule 8: Never Use a Comma Alone to Join Two Independent Clauses

This is the **comma splice** — one of the most common errors on the SAT:

> ✗ "The team won the championship, they celebrated all night."

Two independent clauses joined by only a comma. This is ALWAYS wrong on the SAT.

**Three valid fixes:**
1. Period: "The team won the championship**.** They celebrated all night."
2. Semicolon: "The team won the championship**;** they celebrated all night."
3. Comma + FANBOYS: "The team won the championship**, and** they celebrated all night."

### 4.2 Semicolons — The Complete Rules

The semicolon is functionally equivalent to a period. It joins two **closely related independent clauses** without a coordinating conjunction.

#### Use 1: Join Two Independent Clauses

> "The experiment was a success**;** the results exceeded all expectations."

Both sides are independent clauses. The semicolon replaces a period, signaling that the two ideas are closely connected.

**Test:** Can you replace the semicolon with a period and have both parts work as separate sentences? If yes, the semicolon is correct.

#### Use 2: Separate Items in a Complex List

When list items themselves contain commas, semicolons are used as "super commas" to separate the items:

> "The conference featured speakers from **Paris, France**; **Tokyo, Japan**; and **São Paulo, Brazil**."

Without semicolons, the list would be impossibly confusing.

#### What Semicolons CANNOT Do

- **Never** use a semicolon to introduce a list after an incomplete sentence.
- **Never** use a semicolon after a subordinating conjunction.
- **Never** use a semicolon to join an independent clause and a dependent clause.

### 4.3 Colons — The Complete Rules

#### The Golden Rule of Colons

There MUST be a **complete independent clause** before the colon. What follows the colon can be anything — a list, a phrase, a single word, or another independent clause.

#### Use 1: Introduce a List

> "The recipe requires three ingredients**:** flour, sugar, and eggs."

**CRITICAL:** Do NOT use a colon after phrases like "such as," "for example," "including," "to illustrate," or "namely." These phrases already serve the function of introducing examples, and adding a colon is redundant and incorrect:

> ✗ "The recipe requires several ingredients**,** **such as:** flour, sugar, and eggs."
> ✓ "The recipe requires several ingredients**:** flour, sugar, and eggs."
> ✓ "The recipe requires several ingredients**, such as** flour, sugar, and eggs."

#### Use 2: Add Information or Explanation at the End of a Sentence

> "There was one thing she knew for certain**:** she would not give up."

The colon introduces an explanation or elaboration of what came before. You can test this by seeing if you can replace the colon with "namely" or "that is":

> "There was one thing she knew for certain**—namely,** she would not give up."

If the substitution works, the colon is correct.

#### What Colons CANNOT Do

- **Never** use a colon after an incomplete sentence:
  > ✗ "The three ingredients are**:** flour, sugar, and eggs." (No colon needed — "are" is a verb, and the list is its complement.)
  > ✓ "The recipe requires**:** flour, sugar, and eggs." (Complete clause before the colon.)

- **Never** use a colon after a preposition or verb that directly introduces the list:
  > ✗ "She enjoys**:** reading, hiking, and cooking."
  > ✓ "She enjoys reading, hiking, and cooking."
  > ✓ "She enjoys three activities**:** reading, hiking, and cooking."

### 4.4 Dashes (Em Dashes) — The Complete Rules

The em dash (—) is the longest dash and serves several functions on the SAT.

#### Use 1: Set Off a Middle Comma Clause (MCC)

Dashes can replace commas to set off a nonessential clause in the middle of a sentence. The information set off by dashes is usually more emphatic, surprising, or dramatic:

> "The discovery — **which had been hidden for centuries** — revolutionized the field."

This is grammatically equivalent to:
> "The discovery, **which had been hidden for centuries**, revolutionized the field."

But the dashes add emphasis and drama.

**CRITICAL RULE:** Dashes must always be paired when used in the middle of a sentence. You cannot open with a dash and close with a comma, or vice versa.

#### Use 2: Add Information at the End of a Sentence (Like a Colon)

A single em dash at the end of a sentence functions similarly to a colon, introducing an explanation, elaboration, or dramatic conclusion:

> "After years of research, the team made a groundbreaking discovery**—**a new species of deep-sea coral."

The clause before the dash must be a complete independent clause.

#### Use 3: Before a Concluding Summary or After a Series to Elaborate

> "She had everything she needed**—**courage, determination, and a clear plan."
> "Courage, determination, and a clear plan**—**these were the qualities that defined her."

#### Dash vs. Colon vs. Parentheses — The Emphasis Spectrum

| Punctuation | Emphasis Level | Effect |
|---|---|---|
| Parentheses | Lowest | De-emphasizes the information; treats it as an aside |
| Commas | Moderate | Standard separation; neutral emphasis |
| Dashes | Highest | Emphasizes the information; draws attention to it |
| Colon | High | Directs attention forward; introduces something important |

### 4.5 Apostrophes — The Complete Rules

#### Contractions

An apostrophe in a contraction replaces the missing letter(s):

| Contraction | Full Form |
|---|---|
| it's | it is / it has |
| they're | they are |
| who's | who is / who has |
| you're | you are |
| we're | we are |
| there's | there is / there has |
| that's | that is / that has |
| he's | he is / he has |
| she's | she is / she has |
| I'm | I am |
| don't | do not |
| can't | cannot |
| won't | will not |
| isn't | is not |
| aren't | are not |
| wasn't | was not |
| weren't | were not |
| hasn't | has not |
| haven't | have not |
| hadn't | had not |
| doesn't | does not |
| didn't | did not |
| wouldn't | would not |
| couldn't | could not |
| shouldn't | should not |

**SAT Note:** The only contraction that is generally acceptable on the SAT is "it's" (and other standard contractions). However, the SAT more commonly tests the distinction between contractions and possessives (see below).

#### Possession

**Singular nouns:** Add 's
- "The **student's** book" (the book belonging to one student)
- "The **dog's** bone" (the bone belonging to one dog)
- "**James's** car" (the car belonging to James — both "James's" and "James'" are acceptable, but the SAT prefers 's for singular nouns ending in s)

**Plural nouns ending in s:** Add only an apostrophe after the s
- "The **students'** books" (the books belonging to multiple students)
- "The **dogs'** bones" (the bones belonging to multiple dogs)

**Irregular plural nouns (not ending in s):** Add 's
- "The **children's** toys"
- "The **people's** choice"
- "The **men's** room"
- "The **women's** team"

#### The Possessive vs. Contraction Trap

This is one of the most frequently tested distinctions on the SAT:

| Possessive (NO apostrophe) | Contraction (WITH apostrophe) |
|---|---|
| **its** = belonging to it | **it's** = it is / it has |
| **theirs** = belonging to them | **they're** = they are |
| **whose** = belonging to whom | **who's** = who is / who has |
| **yours** = belonging to you | **you're** = you are |
| **ours** = belonging to us | **we're** = we are |
| **his** = belonging to him | **he's** = he is / he has |
| **hers** = belonging to her | **she's** = she is / she has |
| **my / mine** | **I'm** = I am |

**The Substitution Test:** If you can replace the word with the full form of the contraction and the sentence still makes sense, use the contraction (with apostrophe). If not, use the possessive (without apostrophe).

- "The cat licked **its** paws." → "The cat licked **it is** paws"? No. → Use "its" (possessive).
- "**It's** raining outside." → "**It is** raining outside." Yes. → Use "it's" (contraction).

#### Apostrophes with Simple Plurals — NEVER

> ✗ "I bought three apple's." → ✓ "I bought three apples."
> ✗ "The 1990's were a great decade." → ✓ "The 1990s were a great decade."
> ✗ "I have two PhD's." → ✓ "I have two PhDs."

Apostrophes are NEVER used to form simple plurals. This is a common error in everyday writing but is always wrong on the SAT.

### 4.6 Quotation Marks — The Complete Rules

#### Basic Rules

- Every opening quotation mark must have a matching closing quotation mark.
- Quotation marks enclose direct quotations (exact words spoken or written by someone).
- Quotation marks enclose titles of short works (articles, short stories, poems, songs, episodes).
- Quotation marks enclose words used in a special or ironic sense.

#### Punctuation with Quotation Marks (American English)

In American English (which the SAT follows), commas and periods go **inside** quotation marks:

> "I'll be there soon**,"** she said.
> The article was titled **"The Future of Energy."**

Colons and semicolons go **outside** quotation marks:

> She called it **"a masterpiece"**; I called it overrated.
> There was one word for the performance **"breathtaking"**: the audience gave a standing ovation.

Question marks and exclamation points go **inside** if they are part of the quotation, **outside** if they are not:

> She asked, **"Are you coming?"**
> Did he really say, **"I quit"**?

#### Quotations Introduced by a Colon

A colon can be used to introduce a quotation, especially when the quotation is being highlighted or emphasized:

> The review was unequivocal: **"This is the finest novel of the decade."**

The clause before the colon must be a complete independent clause.

---

## 5. Transitions — Expanded Theory

### 5.1 The Six Logical Relationships

Transition questions on the SAT require you to identify the logical relationship between two sentences or ideas and select the transition word or phrase that best expresses that relationship. There are six fundamental logical relationships:

#### Relationship 1: Continuation / Addition

The second idea adds to, supports, or continues the first idea in the same direction.

| Transitions | |
|---|---|
| furthermore | moreover |
| additionally | in addition |
| also | too |
| as well | similarly |
| likewise | in the same way |
| by the same token | not only...but also |
| just as | equally important |

Example:
> "The new policy reduced carbon emissions by 30%. **Furthermore**, it created thousands of jobs in the renewable energy sector."

#### Relationship 2: Contrast / Opposition

The second idea contradicts, opposes, or presents an alternative to the first idea.

| Transitions | |
|---|---|
| however | but |
| yet | nevertheless |
| on the other hand | conversely |
| whereas | while |
| in contrast | on the contrary |
| unfortunately | despite |
| although | though |
| even though | still |

Example:
> "The new policy reduced carbon emissions by 30%. **However**, it also led to higher energy costs for consumers."

#### Relationship 3: Cause / Effect / Result

The second idea is the result or consequence of the first idea.

| Transitions | |
|---|---|
| therefore | consequently |
| as a result | thus |
| hence | accordingly |
| so | for this reason |
| because (when the effect comes first) | since |

Example:
> "The new policy reduced carbon emissions by 30%. **Consequently**, air quality in the region improved significantly."

#### Relationship 4: Example / Illustration

The second idea provides a specific example or illustration of the first idea.

| Transitions | |
|---|---|
| for example | for instance |
| to illustrate | specifically |
| namely | in particular |
| such as | including |

Example:
> "Several factors contributed to the company's success. **For example**, its investment in employee training led to higher productivity."

#### Relationship 5: Emphasis / Clarification

The second idea emphasizes, restates, or clarifies the first idea.

| Transitions | |
|---|---|
| indeed | in fact |
| as a matter of fact | that is |
| in other words | to clarify |
| put differently | simply put |

Example:
> "The results were remarkable. **In fact**, they exceeded even the most optimistic projections."

#### Relationship 6: Time / Sequence

The ideas are related by chronological order or sequence.

| Transitions | |
|---|---|
| first, second, third | then |
| next | subsequently |
| previously | before |
| after | eventually |
| finally | meanwhile |
| initially | toward(s) |

Example:
> "**First**, the researchers collected soil samples. **Next**, they analyzed the samples in the lab. **Finally**, they published their findings."

### 5.2 The Transition Selection Process

When you encounter a transition question on the SAT, follow this exact process:

**Step 1:** Read the sentence BEFORE the transition carefully. Identify its main idea.

**Step 2:** Read the sentence WITH the transition carefully. Identify its main idea.

**Step 3:** Determine the logical relationship between the two ideas:
- Does the second sentence continue the same idea? → Addition
- Does the second sentence contradict or oppose the first? → Contrast
- Does the second sentence result from the first? → Cause/Effect
- Does the second sentence provide an example? → Example
- Does the second sentence emphasize or clarify the first? → Emphasis
- Are the ideas related by time or sequence? → Time

**Step 4:** Select the transition that matches the logical relationship.

**Step 5:** Read the full sentence with your chosen transition to verify that it sounds natural and logical.

### 5.3 Common Transition Traps

**Trap 1: "However" vs. "Therefore"**
Students often confuse these. "However" signals contrast; "Therefore" signals cause/effect. Read the context carefully.

**Trap 2: "For Example" vs. "Such As"**
"For example" introduces a complete sentence (often preceded by a period or semicolon). "Such as" introduces a phrase within a sentence (no comma after "such as" in most cases).

**Trap 3: "Meanwhile" vs. "However"**
"Meanwhile" indicates simultaneous events (time relationship), not contrast. "However" indicates opposition.

**Trap 4: "Indeed" vs. "Furthermore"**
"Indeed" emphasizes or confirms a point already made. "Furthermore" adds a new point in the same direction.

**Trap 5: "Although" vs. "However"**
"Although" is a subordinating conjunction that introduces a dependent clause. "However" is a conjunctive adverb that connects two independent clauses. They cannot be used interchangeably.

---

## 6. Verb Tenses — Expanded Theory

### 6.1 The Principle of Tense Consistency

The SAT tests your ability to maintain **consistent verb tense** within a sentence and across a paragraph. Unless there is a clear logical reason to shift tenses, all verbs should remain in the same tense.

Example of consistent tense:
> "The researcher **collected** the samples, **analyzed** them in the lab, and **published** her findings." (All simple past — consistent.)

Example of inconsistent tense (INCORRECT):
> "The researcher **collected** the samples, **analyzes** them in the lab, and **published** her findings." (Mixed tenses — inconsistent.)

### 6.2 The Six Tenses — Detailed Analysis

#### Simple Present
- **Form:** Base verb (add -s/-es for third-person singular)
- **Use:** General truths, habitual actions, current states, scientific facts
- **Examples:**
  - "Water **boils** at 100°C." (general truth)
  - "She **walks** to school every day." (habitual action)
  - "The data **suggest** a strong correlation." (current state)

#### Simple Past
- **Form:** Past tense verb (usually -ed for regular verbs)
- **Use:** Completed actions in the past, with no connection to the present
- **Examples:**
  - "The team **won** the championship last year."
  - "She **published** her first novel in 2015."

#### Simple Future
- **Form:** will + base verb
- **Use:** Actions that have not yet occurred
- **Examples:**
  - "The conference **will begin** tomorrow."
  - "Scientists **will continue** to study this phenomenon."

#### Present Perfect
- **Form:** has/have + past participle
- **Use:** Actions that began in the past and continue into the present, OR actions completed at an unspecified time in the past
- **Key indicators:** "since," "for," "already," "yet," "recently," "just," "ever," "never," "so far," "up to now"
- **Examples:**
  - "The team **has worked** on this project **for** three years." (still working)
  - "She **has published** five novels." (in her career, unspecified time)
  - "The researchers **have already collected** the data." (completed recently)

#### Past Perfect
- **Form:** had + past participle
- **Use:** The earlier of two past actions; an action completed before another past action
- **Key indicators:** "by the time," "before," "after," "already," "by [specific past time]"
- **Examples:**
  - "By the time the rescue team arrived, the hikers **had already found** their way back."
  - "She **had studied** French for five years before she moved to Paris."
  - "The number of cases **had declined** before the new policy was implemented."

#### Future Perfect
- **Form:** will have + past participle
- **Use:** An action that will be completed before a specific point in the future
- **Examples:**
  - "By next year, she **will have graduated** from college."
  - "By 2050, scientists **will have developed** a vaccine."

### 6.3 Progressive Tenses

Progressive (continuous) tenses indicate ongoing action:

| Tense | Form | Example |
|---|---|---|
| Present Progressive | am/is/are + -ing | "She **is studying** for the SAT." |
| Past Progressive | was/were + -ing | "She **was studying** when the phone rang." |
| Future Progressive | will be + -ing | "She **will be studying** at 8 PM tonight." |
| Present Perfect Progressive | has/have been + -ing | "She **has been studying** for three hours." |
| Past Perfect Progressive | had been + -ing | "She **had been studying** for three hours before the power went out." |

### 6.4 Tense Selection Strategy for the SAT

1. **Look for time markers** in the sentence or surrounding sentences:
   - "yesterday," "last year," "in 1999," "ago" → Simple Past
   - "since," "for," "already," "yet," "recently" → Present Perfect
   - "by the time," "before," "after" (with two past actions) → Past Perfect
   - "now," "currently," "every day" → Simple Present
   - "tomorrow," "next year," "will" → Simple Future

2. **Check for consistency** with surrounding verbs in the passage.

3. **Identify the sequence of events** when multiple actions are described. The earlier action typically requires past perfect.

4. **Do NOT overthink.** The SAT keeps tense questions clear-cut. If you're torn between two tenses, look for the time marker that makes the answer obvious.

---

## 7. Parallelism & Structure — Expanded Theory

### 7.1 The Principle of Parallelism

Parallelism is the principle that elements of a sentence that serve the same function should have the same grammatical form. The SAT tests parallelism in three main contexts: lists, comparisons, and correlative conjunctions.

### 7.2 Parallelism in Lists

All items in a list must be in the same grammatical form:

**Correct (all gerunds):**
> "She enjoys **reading**, **swimming**, and **hiking**."

**Incorrect (mixed forms):**
> ✗ "She enjoys **reading**, **to swim**, and **hiking**."

**Correct (all infinitives):**
> "She wants **to read**, **to swim**, and **to hike**."

**Incorrect (mixed forms):**
> ✗ "She wants **to read**, **swimming**, and **to hike**."

**Correct (all nouns):**
> "The job requires **discipline**, **dedication**, and **punctuality**."

**Incorrect (mixed forms):**
> ✗ "The job requires **discipline**, **being dedicated**, and **punctuality**."

**Correct (all adjectives):**
> "The presentation was **thorough**, **well-researched**, and **practically focused**."

**Incorrect (mixed forms):**
> ✗ "The presentation was **thorough**, **researched well**, and **practically focused**."

### 7.3 Parallelism with Correlative Conjunctions

Correlative conjunctions come in pairs: **either...or**, **neither...nor**, **not only...but also**, **both...and**, **whether...or**.

Whatever grammatical structure follows the FIRST word must be repeated after the SECOND word:

**Correct:**
> "She is **not only** talented **but also** hardworking."
> (adjective + adjective — parallel)

**Incorrect:**
> ✗ "She is **not only** talented **but also** works hard."
> (adjective + clause — NOT parallel)

**Correct:**
> "The program **both** provides scholarships **and** offers mentorship."
> (verb + verb — parallel)

**Incorrect:**
> ✗ "The program **both** provides scholarships **and** mentorship opportunities."
> (verb + noun — NOT parallel)

**Correct:**
> "**Either** you complete the assignment **or** you accept the penalty."
> (clause + clause — parallel)

**Incorrect:**
> ✗ "**Either** you complete the assignment **or** accepting the penalty."
> (clause + gerund — NOT parallel)

### 7.4 Parallelism in Comparisons

When making comparisons, the items being compared must be logically and grammatically parallel:

**Correct:**
> "The cost of living in New York is higher than **the cost of living in Des Moines**."
> (Or, more concisely: "...higher than **in Des Moines**.")

**Incorrect:**
> ✗ "The cost of living in New York is higher than **Des Moines**."
> (Comparing "cost of living" to a city — NOT parallel)

**Correct:**
> "She runs faster than **I** do." (or "...than **me**" in informal English, but the SAT prefers the formal version with the verb implied.)

**Incorrect:**
> ✗ "She runs faster than **him**." (Ambiguous — could mean she runs faster than she runs him, which is nonsensical.)

### 7.5 "Than" vs. "Then"

This is a common diction error tested on the SAT:

- **"Than"** = used for comparisons
  - "She is taller **than** her brother."
  - "I would rather read **than** watch television."

- **"Then"** = used for time sequence or consequence
  - "We went to the store, and **then** we went home."
  - "If you study hard, **then** you will succeed."

---

## 8. Pronouns — Expanded Theory

### 8.1 Pronoun-Antecedent Agreement — Complete Rules

A pronoun must agree with its antecedent (the noun it refers to) in **number** (singular/plural) and **gender** (masculine/feminine/neuter).

#### Singular Antecedents → Singular Pronouns

| Antecedent | Correct Pronoun |
|---|---|
| each, every, either, neither | he, she, his, her, its (or he or she / his or her) |
| everyone, everybody, someone, somebody, anyone, anybody, no one, nobody | he, she, his, her (or he or she / his or her) |
| a student (singular) | he, she, his, her |
| the company | it, its |
| the team (collective noun) | it, its |

#### Plural Antecedents → Plural Pronouns

| Antecedent | Correct Pronoun |
|---|---|
| the students | they, their, them |
| the companies | they, their, them |
| both X and Y | they, their, them |

#### The "Singular They" on the SAT

The SAT's position on singular "they" has evolved. In recent years, the test has become more accepting of singular "they" when the gender of the antecedent is unknown or when referring to a nonbinary individual. However, in formal grammar questions, the SAT still generally expects singular antecedents to take singular pronouns. When in doubt, look for answer choices that avoid the issue entirely by restructuring the sentence.

### 8.2 Pronoun Clarity — Eliminating Ambiguity

Every pronoun must have a **clear, unambiguous antecedent**. If two nouns could serve as the antecedent, the sentence is ambiguous and incorrect on the SAT.

**Ambiguous:**
> ✗ "John told Mark that **he** had won the award."

Who won the award? John or Mark? The pronoun "he" is ambiguous.

**Clear:**
> ✓ "John told Mark, '**I** have won the award.'" (Direct quotation eliminates ambiguity.)
> ✓ "John told Mark that **he himself** had won the award." (Reflexive pronoun clarifies.)
> ✓ "John, who had won the award, told Mark the news." (Restructuring eliminates the pronoun.)

**SAT Strategy:** When a pronoun is ambiguous, the correct answer often **repeats the specific noun** instead of using a pronoun.

### 8.3 Who vs. Whom — The Complete Analysis

#### The Basic Rule

- **Who** = subject pronoun (equivalent to he, she, they, I, we)
- **Whom** = object pronoun (equivalent to him, her, them, me, us)

#### The He/Him Test

To determine whether to use "who" or "whom," substitute "he" or "him":

- If **"he"** fits → use **who**
- If **"him"** fits → use **whom**

**Example 1:**
> "_____ should I trust?"

Substitute: "Should I trust **him**"? Yes → Use **whom**.
> "**Whom** should I trust?"

**Example 2:**
> "_____ is responsible for this decision?"

Substitute: "**He** is responsible"? Yes → Use **who**.
> "**Who** is responsible for this decision?"

**Example 3:**
> "The scientist _____ discovered the vaccine was awarded the Nobel Prize."

Substitute: "**She** discovered the vaccine"? Yes → Use **who**.
> "The scientist **who** discovered the vaccine was awarded the Nobel Prize."

**Example 4:**
> "The scientist _____ the committee selected was awarded the Nobel Prize."

Substitute: "The committee selected **her**"? Yes → Use **whom**.
> "The scientist **whom** the committee selected was awarded the Nobel Prize."

#### The Clause Rule

The role of who/whom is determined by its function **within its own clause**, not by the antecedent or the main clause:

> "The candidate **who** the polls predicted would win actually lost."

Within the relative clause "who the polls predicted would win," "who" is the subject of "would win" (the polls predicted that **he** would win). So "who" is correct, even though "candidate" is the object of the main clause.

### 8.4 Pronoun Case in Compound Constructions

When a pronoun appears in a compound construction (e.g., "you and I" or "you and me"), remove the other noun and test the pronoun alone:

- "between you and ___" → "between **me**" (not "between I") → "between you and **me**"
- "She and ___ went to the store" → "**I** went to the store" (not "me went") → "She and **I** went to the store"
- "The teacher gave the assignment to Jim and ___" → "The teacher gave the assignment to **me**" (not "to I") → "The teacher gave the assignment to Jim and **me**"

---

## 9. Common SAT Grammar Traps — Expanded Analysis

### 9.1 Comma Splice — The Complete Picture

A comma splice occurs when two independent clauses are joined by only a comma, with no coordinating conjunction:

> ✗ "The sun was setting, the sky turned orange."

**Why it's wrong:** A comma alone is not strong enough to join two independent clauses. You need either a period, a semicolon, or a comma + FANBOYS conjunction.

**Three valid corrections:**
1. "The sun was setting**.** The sky turned orange."
2. "The sun was setting**;** the sky turned orange."
3. "The sun was setting**, and** the sky turned orange."

### 9.2 Run-on Sentences — The Complete Picture

A run-on sentence occurs when two independent clauses are joined with **no punctuation at all**:

> ✗ "The sun was setting the sky turned orange."

**Correction:** Same three options as comma splice.

### 9.3 The "However" Run-on Trap

"However" is a **conjunctive adverb**, NOT a coordinating conjunction. It cannot join two independent clauses with just a comma:

> ✗ "The sun was setting, however, the sky turned orange."

**Corrections:**
1. "The sun was setting**.** However, the sky turned orange."
2. "The sun was setting**;** however, the sky turned orange."
3. "The sun was setting**, and** however, the sky turned orange." (awkward but grammatically valid)

**Other conjunctive adverbs that follow the same rule:**
- therefore, consequently, furthermore, moreover, nevertheless, nonetheless, meanwhile, otherwise, instead, indeed, still, also, accordingly, similarly, hence, thus

### 9.4 Wordiness and Redundancy — The SAT's Favorite Target

The SAT consistently rewards **concision**. When multiple answer choices are grammatically correct and convey the same meaning, the **shortest** option is correct.

#### Common Redundant Phrases

| Redundant | Concise |
|---|---|
| due to the fact that | because |
| in the event that | if |
| at this point in time | now / currently |
| for the reason that | because |
| in order to | to |
| on account of | because |
| the reason is because | the reason is that / because |
| a large number of | many |
| is able to | can |
| has the ability to | can |
| in spite of the fact that | although / despite |
| make a decision | decide |
| take into consideration | consider |
| give consideration to | consider |
| it is important to note that | [delete entirely] |
| it should be noted that | [delete entirely] |
| it is interesting to note that | [delete entirely] |
| the fact that | that |
| despite the fact that | despite / although |
| regardless of the fact that | although / even though |
| in light of the fact that | because / since |
| owing to the fact that | because |
| on the basis of | because / from |
| in the near future | soon |
| at the present time | now / currently |
| in this day and age | today / now |
| each and every | each / every |
| first and foremost | first |
| basic fundamentals | fundamentals |
| true fact | fact |
| added bonus | bonus |
| advance planning | planning |
| close proximity | proximity |
| collaborate together | collaborate |
| completely unanimous | unanimous |
| end result | result |
| free gift | gift |
| future plans | plans |
| past history | history |
| repeat again | repeat |
| revert back | revert |
| still remains | remains |

### 9.5 Sentence Fragments — The Complete Analysis

A sentence fragment is a group of words that is punctuated as a sentence but lacks a subject, a verb, or a complete thought.

#### Types of Fragments

**Type 1: Missing Subject**
> ✗ "Ran quickly to the store."
> ✓ "**She** ran quickly to the store."

**Type 2: Missing Verb**
> ✗ "The student with the highest grades in the class."
> ✓ "The student with the highest grades in the class **received an award**."

**Type 3: Dependent Clause Standing Alone**
> ✗ "Because the experiment failed."
> ✓ "Because the experiment failed, **the team had to start over**."

**Type 4: -ing Phrase Standing Alone**
> ✗ "Running through the forest at dawn."
> ✓ "**She was** running through the forest at dawn."

**Type 5: Appositive Phrase Standing Alone**
> ✗ "A brilliant scientist who won the Nobel Prize."
> ✓ "**She was** a brilliant scientist who won the Nobel Prize."

### 9.6 "Being" and "Having" — The SAT's Preference

**"Being"** is rarely correct on the SAT. It often creates awkward, passive constructions:

> ✗ "Being a talented musician, she was offered a scholarship."
> ✓ "**As she was** a talented musician, she was offered a scholarship."
> ✓ "A talented musician, she was offered a scholarship."

**"Having"** is preferred when showing sequence or cause:

> ✓ "**Having finished** the exam, she left the room."
> ✓ "**Having studied** for weeks, he felt confident."

### 9.7 The "FANBOYS Only" Rule for Joining Independent Clauses

Only the seven coordinating conjunctions (FANBOYS: for, and, nor, but, or, yet, so) can join two independent clauses with a comma. All other connecting words (however, therefore, moreover, furthermore, consequently, nevertheless, nonetheless, meanwhile, otherwise, instead, indeed, still, also, accordingly, similarly, hence, thus, etc.) are **conjunctive adverbs** and require a semicolon or period before them.

---

## 10. Reading Comprehension Strategies — Expanded Theory

### 10.1 The Active Reading Process — A Detailed Protocol

The SAT Reading and Writing section demands active, engaged reading. Passive reading — simply letting your eyes move over the words — is a recipe for missed questions and wasted time. Here is the complete active reading protocol:

#### Step 1: Annotate Transitions and Key Words

As you read, **highlight or circle** every transitional word and phrase. These are the signposts that reveal the author's logic:

**Contrast signals:** but, however, yet, although, though, while, whereas, on the other hand, conversely, in contrast, on the contrary, nevertheless, nonetheless, despite, unfortunately, still, even so, instead, rather

**Addition signals:** and (when coordinating), furthermore, moreover, additionally, also, too, as well, in addition, besides, similarly, likewise, not only...but also, equally, by the same token

**Cause/Effect signals:** because, since, therefore, consequently, thus, hence, as a result, accordingly, so, for this reason, due to, owing to, leads to, results in, contributes to

**Example/Illustration signals:** for example, for instance, to illustrate, specifically, namely, such as, including, in particular, as evidenced by

**Emphasis signals:** indeed, in fact, as a matter of fact, actually, importantly, significantly, crucially, above all, most importantly

**Conclusion signals:** in conclusion, in summary, to sum up, in short, ultimately, finally, all in all, on the whole, therefore, thus

**Concession signals:** granted, admittedly, certainly, of course, to be sure, naturally, although, even though, while, despite, regardless

#### Step 2: Focus on What Comes AFTER the Transition

The most important part of any sentence is often what comes **after** the transition. The transition tells you the relationship, but the content after it is the new information:

> "The initial results were promising. **However**, the follow-up study **revealed significant flaws in the methodology**."

The word "However" signals contrast. The key information is that the follow-up study revealed flaws. This is what you need to understand and remember.

#### Step 3: Write Your Foreshadow

After reading each sentence or paragraph, briefly note what you expect to happen next. This keeps you engaged and helps you track the author's argument:

> "The author has just described the benefits of renewable energy. I expect the next paragraph to discuss the drawbacks or challenges."

#### Step 4: Confirm or Deny Your Foreshadow

As you continue reading, check whether your prediction was correct:
- **If confirmed:** Continue reading with confidence.
- **If denied:** Stop, re-read the previous section, and adjust your understanding. The author has taken an unexpected turn, and you need to understand why.

### 10.2 Evidence Analysis — The Command of Evidence Skill

Many SAT questions ask you to identify the evidence that best supports a given claim. This requires precise matching between the claim and the evidence.

#### The Matching Process

**Step 1:** Read the claim carefully. Identify the **key nouns and verbs** — the specific elements that must be supported.

**Step 2:** Read each piece of evidence. Look for evidence that **directly addresses** the key elements of the claim.

**Step 3:** Eliminate evidence that:
- Mentions the topic but doesn't directly support the specific claim
- Supports a different claim or a broader/narrower point
- Contradicts the claim
- Is too vague or general

**Step 4:** Select the evidence that **most precisely and directly** supports the claim.

#### Example

**Claim:** "Dedicated bus lanes made the route faster."

**Evidence Options:**
A. "After the city added bus-only lanes, average commute times fell by 18 percent." ✓ (Directly supports — bus lanes → faster commutes)
B. "The city invested $2 million in public transportation improvements." ✗ (Doesn't mention speed or bus lanes specifically)
C. "Bus ridership increased by 12 percent after the changes." ✗ (Ridership increase doesn't prove the route is faster)
D. "Traffic congestion decreased throughout the city." ✗ (Too broad — doesn't specifically address bus lanes or route speed)

### 10.3 Inference Questions — Drawing Conclusions from Evidence

Inference questions ask you to draw a conclusion that is **not explicitly stated** but is **clearly supported** by the text.

#### The Inference Test

The correct inference must be:
1. **Logically supported** by the text (not just plausible)
2. **Conservative** in its claims (not going beyond what the text supports)
3. **Free of extreme language** (avoiding "always," "never," "all," "none" unless the text explicitly supports such absolutes)

#### Common Inference Traps

- **Too extreme:** "The author believes that ALL standardized tests are useless." (The text may criticize some tests, but "ALL" is too extreme.)
- **Too broad:** "The passage suggests that technology has ruined education." (The passage may discuss one specific technology's impact, not all of technology.)
- **Unsupported:** "The author implies that the policy will fail." (The passage may present both sides without implying failure.)
- **Contradicted:** "The author argues that the experiment was a success." (The passage may actually describe the experiment as a failure.)

### 10.4 Words in Context — The Complete Strategy

Words in context questions ask you to determine the meaning of a word as it is used in the passage.

#### The Process

**Step 1:** Read the sentence containing the target word.

**Step 2:** Read 1-2 sentences before and after for additional context.

**Step 3:** Determine the meaning of the word **as used in this specific context**. Do NOT rely on the most common dictionary definition.

**Step 4:** Write your own word or phrase that could replace the target word.

**Step 5:** Find the answer choice that matches your prediction.

#### Example

> "The politician's speech was deliberately **equivocal**, leaving both sides of the debate believing she supported their position."

Context clues: "leaving both sides believing she supported their position" → The speech was ambiguous, intentionally unclear.

Your prediction: "ambiguous" or "vague"

Answer choice: "ambiguous" ✓

#### Common Words with Multiple Meanings on the SAT

| Word | Common Meaning | SAT Contextual Meaning |
|---|---|---|
| temperate | related to temperature | moderate, restrained |
| table | furniture | to set aside (verb) |
| champion | winner | to advocate for |
| address | location | to deal with (verb) |
| arrest | to stop (legal) | to stop, halt |
| check | to verify | to restrain, curb |
| curb | edge of sidewalk | to restrain, limit |
| weather | climate | to endure, withstand |
| economy | financial system | thriftiness, efficiency |
| fabric | textile | underlying structure |
| plane | aircraft | flat surface |
| resolution | determination | solution |
| sanction | penalty | to approve |
| screen | display | to examine, filter |
| spirit | ghost, soul | enthusiasm, energy |
| store | shop | supply, reserve |
| temper | mood | to moderate, soften |
| tender | soft, gentle | to offer formally |

### 10.5 Rhetorical Synthesis — The Complete Strategy

Rhetorical synthesis questions present you with a set of bulleted research notes and ask you to synthesize the information to achieve a specific rhetorical purpose.

#### The Process

**Step 1:** Read the question header carefully. Identify the **specific claim or purpose** you need to support.

**Step 2:** Go through each bullet point and classify it as:
- **Background information:** Provides context but doesn't directly support the claim
- **Evidential information:** Directly supports the claim with data, examples, or facts

**Step 3:** Select the bullet points that are **most relevant** to the specific claim or purpose stated in the question.

**Step 4:** Combine the selected information into a coherent statement that achieves the rhetorical purpose.

#### Example

**Question:** "Which choice most effectively uses information from the given bullet points to emphasize the impact of the new policy?"

**Bullet Points:**
- The policy was implemented in January 2024. (background)
- Carbon emissions decreased by 25% in the first six months. (evidential)
- The policy was supported by 70% of voters. (background)
- Air quality improved significantly in urban areas. (evidential)

**Correct synthesis:** "Since the policy was implemented in January 2024, carbon emissions have decreased by 25% and air quality in urban areas has improved significantly."

This synthesis emphasizes the **impact** by focusing on the evidential bullet points (emissions decrease, air quality improvement) rather than the background information (implementation date, voter support).

---

## 11. ACT English Test Format — Expanded Notes

While the primary focus of this guide is the SAT, many students also prepare for the ACT English section, which shares significant overlap with SAT Writing.

### 11.1 Format Details

- **Time:** 45 minutes
- **Questions:** 75 questions
- **Passages:** 5 passages, each with underlined portions
- **Question types:** Grammar, punctuation, sentence structure, organization, word choice, concision
- **"NO CHANGE" option:** Available for every question
- **Passage-level questions:** Some questions ask about the passage as a whole (identified by numbers in a box)

### 11.2 Key Differences from the SAT

| Feature | ACT English | SAT Writing & Language |
|---|---|---|
| Time per question | 36 seconds | ~70 seconds (digital) |
| Number of passages | 5 long passages | 1 question per short passage (digital SAT) |
| Question density | 15 questions per passage | 1 question per passage |
| "NO CHANGE" frequency | ~25% of answers | ~25% of answers |
| Passage-level questions | Yes | Yes (rhetorical synthesis, etc.) |
| Math-related content | None | Some data interpretation |

### 11.3 ACT-Specific Strategies

1. **Read the entire passage first** before answering questions. This helps you understand context, tone, and organization.
2. **Read beyond the underlined portion.** Sometimes the answer depends on information in the next sentence.
3. **Don't be afraid of "NO CHANGE."** It's correct approximately 25% of the time.
4. **Trust the rules, not your ear.** What sounds natural in conversation may be grammatically incorrect.
5. **Watch for concision.** The ACT, like the SAT, rewards the shortest grammatically correct answer.

---

## 12. Key Vocabulary — Expanded Study Guide

### 12.1 High-Frequency SAT Vocabulary — Grouped by Theme

#### Words Related to Criticism and Disapproval

| Word | Definition | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **castigate** | to criticize severely | The reviewer castigated the film for its lack of originality. |
| **censure** | to express strong disapproval | The senator was censured for his unethical behavior. |
| **decry** | to publicly declare as wrong or evil | The activist decried the company's environmental practices. |
| **denounce** | to publicly declare as wrong or evil | The president denounced the terrorist attack. |
| **deride** | to ridicule, make fun of | The critics derided the author's latest novel. |
| **lambaste** | to criticize harshly | The coach lambasted the team for their poor performance. |
| **reproach** | to express disapproval or disappointment | She reproached her son for his rude behavior. |
| **reprove** | to criticize or correct | The teacher reproved the student for cheating. |
| **upbraid** | to scold sharply | The manager upbraided the employee for the error. |
| **vilify** | to speak ill of, slander | The media vilified the politician after the scandal. |

#### Words Related to Praise and Approval

| Word | Definition | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **acclaim** | to praise enthusiastically | The film was acclaimed by critics worldwide. |
| **commend** | to praise formally or officially | The officer was commended for his bravery. |
| **eulogize** | to praise highly, especially in a funeral oration | The speaker eulogized the late president. |
| **extol** | to praise highly | The author extolled the virtues of the new policy. |
| **laud** | to praise, to extol | The committee lauded the researcher's contributions. |
| **rhapsodize** | to speak about with great enthusiasm | The critic rhapsodized about the young pianist's debut. |
| **tout** | to promote or praise enthusiastically | The company touted its new product as revolutionary. |

#### Words Related to Neutrality and Objectivity

| Word | Definition | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **dispassionate** | not influenced by strong emotion | The judge provided a dispassionate analysis of the case. |
| **equanimous** | calm, even-tempered | She remained equanimous despite the criticism. |
| **impartial** | fair, unbiased | An impartial jury was selected for the trial. |
| **objective** | based on fact, not opinion | The report provided an objective assessment of the situation. |
| **unbiased** | not prejudiced | The study used an unbiased sample of participants. |

#### Words Related to Complexity and Ambiguity

| Word | Definition | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **ambiguous** | open to multiple interpretations | The contract's language was deliberately ambiguous. |
| **convoluted** | complex, difficult to follow | The plot was so convoluted that many viewers were confused. |
| **nuanced** | characterized by subtle distinctions | The professor gave a nuanced analysis of the poem. |
| **paradoxical** | seemingly contradictory | It is paradoxical that the richest country has so many poor people. |
| **perplexing** | confusing, puzzling | The disappearance of the artifact remains perplexing. |

#### Words Related to Change and Development

| Word | Definition | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **ameliorate** | to improve or make better | The new policy ameliorated the housing crisis. |
| **exacerbate** | to make worse | The drought exacerbated the food shortage. |
| **mitigate** | to make less severe | The measures mitigated the impact of the recession. |
| **precipitate** | to cause suddenly | The scandal precipitated the minister's resignation. |
| **supplant** | to replace, supersede | The new technology supplanted the old system. |

*This chapter has provided the expanded theoretical foundation for sentence structure, subject-verb agreement, modifiers, punctuation, transitions, verb tenses, parallelism, pronouns, and reading comprehension strategies as tested on the SAT. The next chapter will build on these foundations with advanced applications and edge cases.*

---


# Chapter 5: Pronouns, Modifiers, and Diction — Antecedent Clarity, Dangling-Misplaced Modifiers, and Commonly Confused Words

---

## 5.1 — THE ARCHITECTURE OF THE PRONOUN SYSTEM

Pronouns are among the most frequently tested elements on the SAT Writing and Language section. The test designers exploit a uniquely human cognitive tendency: we *think* we know what a pronoun refers to because our brains automatically fill in context. The SAT exploits this by constructing answer choices where the "obvious" reading is the *trap*, not the correct answer. Mastering pronouns requires understanding not only the mechanical rules of agreement and case but also the deeper principles of antecedent clarity, logical reference, and syntactic positioning.

### 5.1.1 — What Is a Pronoun?

A pronoun is a word that substitutes for a noun (called its **antecedent**) to avoid repetition. The word itself reveals its function: *pro-noun* means "for a noun."
