When Maya is introduced in a sentence, subsequent references to her can be replaced with *she*, *her*, *hers*, or *herself*. This substitution creates fluid, readable prose — but it also introduces the possibility of ambiguity, disagreement, and illogical reference.

On the SAT, the most commonly tested pronouns fall into several categories:

| Category | Pronouns | Function |
|---|---|---|
| **Personal (Subject)** | I, he, she, it, we, they, you | Performs the action |
| **Personal (Object)** | me, him, her, it, us, them, you | Receives the action |
| **Possessive (Determiner)** | my, his, her, its, our, their, your | Modifies a noun to show ownership |
| **Possessive (Standalone)** | mine, his, hers, its, ours, yours, theirs | Stands alone as the object of possession |
| **Reflexive/Intensive** | myself, himself, herself, itself, ourselves, themselves, yourself | Reflects back on the subject or emphasizes |
| **Relative** | who, whom, whose, which, that, where, when | Introduces dependent clauses |
| **Demonstrative** | this, that, these, those | Points to specific nouns |
| **Indefinite** | everyone, somebody, each, either, neither, anyone, nobody, everything | Refers to nonspecific entities |
| **Interrogative** | who, whom, whose, which, what | Introduces questions |
| **Reciprocal** | each other, one another | Expresses mutual relationship |

Each category operates under its own set of constraints, and the SAT tests them in overlapping, complex ways.

---

## 5.2 — PRONOUN-ANTECEDENT AGREEMENT

### 5.2.1 — The Fundamental Rule

A pronoun **must agree in number** (singular or plural) with its antecedent — the noun to which it refers. This deceptively simple rule becomes extraordinarily complex when the antecedent is hidden among prepositional phrases, embedded in a relative clause, or is an indefinite pronoun whose number is not immediately obvious.

**Basic Examples:**

- ✓ Singular: "The **student** forgot **his** notebook." (One student → his)
- ✓ Plural: "The **students** forgot **their** notebooks." (Multiple students → their)
- ✗ Error: "The **students** forgot **his** notebook." (Plural antecedent ≠ singular pronoun)

### 5.2.2 — Singular Indefinite Pronouns

This is one of the most heavily tested categories. The following indefinite pronouns are **always singular** in formal written English on the SAT:

| Singular Indefinite Pronouns |
|---|
| anybody, anyone, any |
| each, every |
| everybody, everyone |
| everything |
| nobody, no one |
| nothing |
| somebody, someone |
| something |
| either, neither |

**The rigid rule:** When these words serve as antecedents, the pronoun referring back to them must be singular:

- ✓ "Everyone must bring **his or her** own lunch."
- ✓ "Each of the girls brought **her** project."
- ✓ "Neither Tom nor Jerry admitted **his** fault."

The SAT **has not** traditionally accepted singular "their" for these indefinite pronouns in its grammar questions. While singular "they" is increasingly accepted in modern formal writing and many style guides, the SAT Writing section still tests traditional agreement rules. If "their" appears as a pronoun choice referring to a singular antecedent like "everyone" or "each," treat it as incorrect — unless no other grammatically valid option exists.

### 5.2.3 — Compound Antecedents

When two nouns are joined by **"and"**, the antecedent is **plural**, and the pronoun must be plural:

- ✓ "Sarah and Marcus finished **their** presentation." (Sarah + Marcus = plural)

When two nouns are joined by **"or"** or **"nor"**, the pronoun agrees with the **nearest antecedent** (the **proximity rule**):

- ✓ "Neither the teacher nor the students submitted **their** assignments." (nearest antecedent = "students" → plural)
- ✓ "Neither the students nor the teacher submitted **her** assignment." (nearest antecedent = "teacher" → singular)

This proximity rule is critical and frequently tested. Students often mistakenly apply the "compound = plural" rule to "or/nor" constructions, but the logic is entirely different — only *one* of the two parties is being referenced, so the pronoun agrees with the closer one.

### 5.2.4 — Collective Nouns

Collective nouns refer to a group acting as a single unit:

| Collective Nouns |
|---|
| team, committee, jury, family, group, herd, flock, class, audience, government, staff, band, orchestra, council, board |

When the group acts as a **unified whole**, these nouns are **singular** and take singular pronouns:

- ✓ "The **committee** reached **its** decision." (acting as one body)
- ✓ "The **team celebrated its** victory." (the team as a single entity)

When the members of the group act **individually**, a plural pronoun may be warranted — but the SAT almost always tests the singular usage because it aligns with the formal convention. If you see a collective noun on the SAT, **default to singular** unless the context clearly indicates members acting separately (which would be rare on the test).

### 5.2.5 — Amounts, Distances, Titles, and Units

Amounts of money, measurements of distance, expressions of time, and titles of works that refer to a **single unit** take **singular** pronouns:

- ✓ "Ten dollars **is** more than **it** seems." (one amount)
- ✓ "The Canterbury Tales **has endured because of its** wit." (one book)
- ✓ "Three miles **is** a long way to carry **its** burden." (one distance)

The key question is: Is the quantity being treated as a single unit (singular) or as individual components (plural)? The SAT almost always treats them as units.

### 5.2.6 — Plural-Form Nouns That Are Singular in Meaning

Some nouns end in "s" but are grammatically singular:

| Noun | Example |
|---|---|
| news | "The **news** reported **its** story." |
| economics | "**Economics** has **its** own terminology." |
| mathematics | "**Mathematics** has **its** challenges. |
| physics | "**Physics** earned **its** reputation. |
| measles | "**Measles** has **its** symptoms. |
| billiards | "**Billiards** requires **its** own table. |

Conversely, some nouns that look singular are **always plural**:

| Plural Nouns |
|---|
| police, people, cattle, poultry, vermin, clergy (when not specified as an individual) |

- ✓ "The **police** completed **their** investigation."
- ✓ "The **people** exercised **their** right." (but "peoples" refers to ethnic groups)

### 5.2.7 — The "Each" and "Every" Rules

**"Each"** and **"every"** are always singular when they function as the antecedent. This applies even when "each" appears after a compound antecedent:

- ✓ "Tom and Jerry each submitted **his** report." (Each = singular → his)
- ✓ "Every student and teacher must bring **his or her** ID." (Every = singular → his or her)
- ✗ "Every student and teacher must bring **their** ID." (Common error — singular antecedent demands singular pronoun)

The phrase "each of the [plural noun]" also takes a singular pronoun:

- ✓ "Each of the boys brought **his** lunch."
- ✗ "Each of the boys brought **their** lunch."

Exception: If the sentence says "They each," the pronoun "they" is the subject, and "each" is an appositive — the verb and subsequent pronouns agree with "they":

- ✓ "They each brought **their** lunch." (ambiguous; SAT prefers rephrasing to avoid this)

---

## 5.3 — PRONOUN CASE

### 5.3.1 — Subject vs. Object Pronouns

Pronouns change form depending on their grammatical function:

| Subject Pronouns | Object Pronouns |
|---|---|
| I | me |
| he | him |
| she | her |
| it | it |
| we | us |
| they | them |
| who | whom |
| whoever | whomever |

**Subject pronouns** perform the action (they are the subject of a verb):
- ✓ "**She** ran the marathon." (She runs)
- ✓ "**They** completed the project." (They completed)

**Object pronouns** receive the action or follow a preposition:
- ✓ "The teacher praised **him**." (Him receives the praise)
- ✓ "Between you and **me**, this is easy." (Object of the preposition "between")

### 5.3.2 — The Compound Construction Trap

The SAT loves to hide errors inside compound constructions. The test is simple: **remove the other subject or object and test the pronoun alone.**

**Example 1 (Compound Subject):**
- "Beth and ___ went to the store."
- Remove "Beth and": "___ went to the store." → "I" (not "me")
- ✓ "Beth and **I** went to the store."

**Example 2 (Compound Object):**
- "The award was given to Beth and ___."
- Remove "Beth and": "The award was given to ___." → "me" (not "I")
- ✓ "The award was given to Beth and **me**."

**Example 3 (After "between"):**
- "The secret is between you and ___."
- Remove "you and": "The secret is between ___." → "me"
- ✓ "The secret is between you and **me**."
- ✗ "The secret is between you and **I**." (The hypercorrection trap — people think "she and I" is always correct, but after a prepositions, object pronouns are required)

### 5.3.3 — Pronouns in Comparisons (Elliptical Clauses)

When a pronoun appears after "than" or "as" in a comparison, the sentence is often **elliptical** — meaning words are implied but not spoken. To determine whether to use a subject or object pronoun, mentally complete the comparison:

- "She is taller than ___." → Expand: "She is taller than **I am**." → Use subject pronoun "I"
- "She is taller than **I**." (formal, correct on SAT)
- "He likes pizza more than ___." → Expand: "He likes pizza more than **he likes me**." → Use object pronoun "me"
- "He likes pizza more than **me**."

The key: Does the expanded clause imply "she is" (subject pronoun) or "she kissed" (object pronoun)?

### 5.3.4 — Who vs. Whom

This is one of the most classic grammar distinctions tested on the SAT.

**Method 1: The He/Him Substitution Test**

Replace who/whom with he/him. If "he" fits, use "who" (subject). If "him" fits, use "whom" (object).

- "___ did you call?" → "Did you call **him**?" → "him" fits → **whom**
- ✓ "**Whom** did you call?"

- "___ called you?" → "**He** called you?" → "he" fits → **who**
- ✓ "**Who** called you?"

**Method 2: Function Within the Clause**

The critical insight — and the trap — is that the role of who/whom is determined **within its own clause**, not by the main sentence.

Example: "The author ___ the committee praised received an award."

Identify the dependent clause: "___ the committee praised."
- In this clause: "the committee praised ___" (the committee is the subject; the blank is the object being praised)
- Object → whom
- ✓ "The author **whom** the committee praised received an award."

Example 2: "The scientist ___ discovered the vaccine was celebrated."

Identify the dependent clause: "___ discovered the vaccine."
- In this clause: "___ discovered the vaccine" (no subject — the blank is the subject)
- Subject → who
- ✓ "The scientist **who** discovered the vaccine was celebrated."

**Common Traps:**

1. **Extra words inserted between subject and verb** can obscure the role of who/whom:
   - "The CEO, ___ many believed ___ would resign, stayed on." → "many believed (he) would resign" → "he" → **who**. The interrupting phrase "many believed" misleads students into choosing "whom."

2. **Indirect questions:**
   - "She asked ___ was responsible." → Embedded question subject → **who**
   - "She asked ___ I wanted to speak with." → Embedded question object → **whom**

### 5.3.5 — Whoever vs. Whomever

Apply the same logic as who/whom, but within the **noun clause** that whoever/whomever introduces.

- "Give the prize to ___ deserves it." → The clause is "___ deserves it" → subject of "deserves" → **whoever**
- ✓ "Give the prize to **whoever** deserves it."

- "Give the prize to ___ you choose." → The clause is "you choose ___" → object of "choose" → **whomever**
- ✓ "Give the prize to **whomever** you choose."

---

## 5.4 — PRONOUN CLARITY AND REFERENCE

### 5.4.1 — The Fundamental Requirement: Clear Antecedent

Every pronoun must point to **exactly one** specific noun. If a pronoun could refer to more than one antecedent, the sentence is **ambiguous** and grammatically incorrect on the SAT.

**Example of Ambiguity:**
- "Tom told Jerry that **he** was wrong."
  - Who is "he"? Tom? Jerry?
  - This is a classic ambiguity error.

**Corrections:**
- Repetition: "Tom told Jerry, 'You are wrong.'" OR "Tom told Jerry, 'I am wrong.'"
- Restructuring: "Tom admitted his own error to Jerry." OR "Tom pointed out Jerry's error."

**The SAT Rule:** When faced with an ambiguous pronoun, the correct answer almost always **replaces the pronoun with the specific noun** or **restructures the sentence to eliminate ambiguity**. Merely moving the pronoun closer to its intended antecedent is generally insufficient if either noun could logically serve as the antecedent.

### 5.4.2 — Implied Antecedents

A pronoun cannot refer to a noun that is merely *implied* but not explicitly stated. The antecedent must be a **clearly stated noun** in the sentence or in a closely connected previous sentence.

- ✗ "In **the instructions**, it says to wait 24 hours." (What is "it"? There's no noun "it" can refer to. "Instructions" is inside a prepositional phrase.)
- ✓ "**The instructions** say to wait 24 hours." (Eliminate the pronoun; make the noun the subject.)

- ✗ "They have good food at that restaurant." ("They" has no antecedent — this is the "floating they" error common in speech but incorrect in formal writing.)
- ✓ "That restaurant has good food."

The SAT tests this with **"it"** used without a clear noun antecedent, especially after prepositional phrases. The fix is almost always to replace the dangling pronoun with the actual noun.

### 5.4.3 — This, That, These, Those as Pronouns

When demonstrative pronouns (*this, that, these, those*) are used **alone** (without a noun immediately following), they must also have a clear, unambiguous reference. The SAT frequently tests this by using "this" alone where it could refer to an entire idea or multiple nouns:

- ✗ "The company increased prices and reduced quality. **This** led to customer complaints." (Vague — "this" could refer to either or both actions.)
- ✓ "The company increased prices and reduced quality. **The combination of these changes** led to customer complaints."
- ✓ "The company's price increases **and** quality reductions led to customer complaints."

The fix is to add a **summarizing noun** after the demonstrative: "this decision," "this policy," "these factors," "this combination." The noun **disambiguates and specifies** what "this" refers to.

### 5.4.4 — Pronouns with Multiple Possible Antecedents (The Proximity Principle)

While a clear antecedent is required, when a pronoun *could* grammatically refer to multiple nouns, the SAT tests whether the intended antecedent is sufficiently clear. The closer a pronoun is to a potential antecedent, the more "natural" the connection seems — but proximity alone does not guarantee clarity.

**Example:**
- "The report that the manager submitted to the director was revised **before she signed it**."
  - Who is "she"? The manager or the director? Ambiguous.

On the SAT, if a pronoun has **two grammatically possible antecedents** within the same sentence, it is considered incorrect. The answer choice will resolve the ambiguity by:
1. Replacing the pronoun with a specific noun
2. Restructuring the sentence

### 5.4.5 — "It" as a Vague Pronoun

When "it" appears as a **subject** pronoun, the SAT is especially vigilant. It tests two distinct problems with "it":

**Problem 1: Ambiguous "it"** (multiple possible antecedents):
- ✗ "The report and the letter were on **it** before the meeting." (What is "it"? A desk? Ambiguous.)

**Problem 2: Expletive "it" used where specificity is required:**
- "It is raining." (Fine — expletive "it" has no antecedent needed)
- "It says in the book that..." (Wrong — "it" has no antecedent. The real subject should be stated.)

**Problem 3: "It" referring to different things in the same sentence:**
- ✗ "I read the book and enjoyed **it**, but when I returned **it** to the library, they said **it** was overdue." (Three uses of "it" — managing all three correctly is fine, but the SAT will sometimes test whether any one of them creates ambiguity.)

---

## 5.5 — REFLEXIVE AND INTENSIVE PRONOUNS

### 5.5.1 — The Rule: Reflexive Pronouns Require the Antecedent in the Sentence

Reflexive pronouns end in *-self* or *-selves*:

| Singular | Plural |
|---|---|
| myself | ourselves |
| yourself | yourself (yourselves) |
| himself, herself, itself | themselves |

A reflexive pronoun **must** refer back to a clearly stated subject in the **same clause**. It cannot appear if no antecedent exists to "reflect" back to.

- ✓ "She **herself** completed the project." (Intensive — emphasizes "she")
- ✓ "He cut **himself**." (Reflexive — "he" and "himself" refer to the same person)
- ✗ "The package was delivered to **myself**." (No subject "I" to reflect back)
- ✓ "The package was delivered to **me**."

**Critical Rule:** A reflexive pronoun **cannot be used as a substitute for a regular subject or object pronoun.** People sometimes use "myself" to avoid choosing between "I" and "me," but this is always incorrect on the SAT.

- ✗ "Beth and **myself** went to the store." (Should be "Beth and I")
- ✗ "The manager spoke to Beth and **myself**." (Should be "Beth and me")

The test: Ask yourself — is there an "I" or "we" in the sentence that the reflexive could refer to? If not, the reflexive is wrong.

### 5.5.2 — Proper Uses of Reflexive Pronouns

1. **True reflexive:** The subject and object are the same entity.
   - "She blamed **herself**." (She blamed her own self.)

2. **Intensive/emphatic:** The reflexive renames the subject for emphasis.
   - "The president **himself** attended the ceremony." (Emphasizes: not a substitute, but the actual president)

3. **Idiomatic usage:** Some verbs are conventionally used with reflexive pronouns:
   - "They found **themselves** in a difficult position."
   - "She applied **herself** to the task."
   - "He behaves **himself**."

---

## 5.6 — RELATIVE PRONOUNS AND THEIR ROLE IN SENTENCE COMPLEXITY

### 5.6.1 — The Relative Pronouns

| Relative Pronoun | Used For | Type of Clause |
|---|---|---|
| **who** | People (subject in clause) | Restrictive or Nonrestrictive |
| **whom** | People (object in clause) | Restrictive or Nonrestrictive |
| **whose** | People or things (possession) | Restrictive or Nonrestrictive |
| **which** | Things, animals, concepts | Nonrestrictive (preferred by SAT) |
| **that** | People or things | Restrictive ONLY |
| **where** | Places | Restrictive or Nonrestrictive |
| **when** | Times | Restrictive or Nonrestrictive |

### 5.6.2 — "That" vs. "Which"

This distinction is heavily tested on the SAT and is one of the most important rules in the entire grammar section.

**"THAT"** — Used exclusively for **restrictive** (essential) clauses. These clauses define or identify which thing is being discussed. No commas.

> "The car **that has a sunroof** is mine."
> (Which car? The one with a sunroof. The clause is essential to identify the car.)

**"WHICH"** — Used for **nonrestrictive** (nonessential) clauses. These clauses add extra, removable information. Must be preceded by a comma (or commas, if in the middle of the sentence).

> "The car, **which has a sunroof**, is mine."
> (The car is already identified — it's mine. The sunroof information is an extra detail set off by commas.)

**The SAT Test:**
- If there's a comma before the relative pronoun, use **which** (for things).
- If there's NO comma, use **that**.
- Mixing these (comma before "that" or no comma before "which") is wrong.

> ✓ "The novel **that won prizes** became a bestseller."
> ✓ "The novel, **which won prizes**, became a bestseller."
> ✗ "The novel, **that won prizes**, became a bestseller." (comma + that = wrong)
> ✗ "The novel **which won prizes** became a bestseller." (no comma + which for restrictive = less preferred; "that" is better)

### 5.6.3 — "Who" vs. "Whom" When Used as Relative Pronouns

When who/whom introduces a relative clause, the same he/him test applies *within the clause*:

- "The woman **who** lives next door is a doctor." (She lives next door → who)
- "The woman **whom** I interviewed is a doctor." (I interviewed her → whom)

### 5.6.4 — "Whose" for Possession (Including Objects)

"Whose" shows possession and can refer to **both people and things**:

- ✓ "The company **whose CEO resigned** faced losses."
- ✓ "The house **whose roof collapsed** was condemned."

Do not confuse **"whose"** (possession) with **"who's"** (contraction of "who is" or "who has"):

- ✓ "**Whose** jacket is this?" (possession)
- ✓ "**Who's** coming to the party?" (who is)
- ✗ "**Who's** jacket is this?" (does not mean possession)

---

## 5.7 — MODIFIERS: THE COMPLETE SYSTEM

Modifiers are words, phrases, or clauses that describe, clarify, or give more information about another word or group of words. The SAT tests modifiers primarily through **placement** (where the modifier sits relative to what it modifies) and **logic** (whether what the modifier is describing actually makes sense).

Mastery of modifier rules accounts for a significant percentage of SAT Writing questions. This system includes:

1. **Dangling Modifiers** — The grammatical subject doesn't match the logical subject.
2. **Misplaced Modifiers** — A modifier is too far from the word it describes.
3. **Squinting/Amphibious Modifiers** — A modifier could be read as modifying more than one thing.
4. **Adjective vs. Adverb Errors** — The wrong form is used to modify the target.

---

## 5.8 — DANGLING MODIFIERS

### 5.8.1 — The Anatomy of a Dangling Modifier

A dangling modifier occurs when a sentence begins with an **introductory phrase** (typically a participial phrase or a prepositional phrase acting as one) that implies a **logical subject**, but the subject of the main clause **cannot perform the action** described by the phrase.

The modifier is "dangling" because it has **nothing logical to attach to** at the start of the sentence.

**Structure:**
[Introductory modifying phrase/phrase with implied subject], [main clause with grammatical subject].

**The Rule:**
The **grammatical subject** of the main clause (the noun immediately after the comma) **must** be the entity that is logically performing the action in the introductory modifier.

### 5.8.2 — Classic Examples and Diagnosis

**Example 1:**
> "Walking through the forest, the trees were beautiful."

*Diagnosis:* Who is walking? The sentence says "the trees" are the subject, but trees cannot walk. The modifier "walking through the forest" dangles — no logical subject is present.

*Correction:*
> "**Walking through the forest, I** saw beautiful trees."
> OR
> "**As I walked through the forest, the** trees were beautiful."

**Example 2:**
> "After studying for three hours, the exam seemed easier."

*Diagnosis:* Who was studying? The sentence says "the exam" was the subject, but exams don't study. The modifier dangles.

*Correction:*
> "**After studying for three hours, I** found the exam easier."

**Example 3:**
> "Having finished the assignment, the TV was turned on."

*Diagnosis:* Who finished the assignment? The TV cannot finish assignments. Dangling.

*Correction:*
> "**Having finished the assignment, Jen** turned on the TV."

### 5.8.3 — The "Comma Rule" for Identifying Dangling Modifiers

If a sentence follows this structure:

**[Participial/introductory phrase] + [COMMA] + [subject + verb]**

then ask: **"Is the subject after the comma the thing performing the action of the introductory phrase?"**

- If NO → dangling modifier error
- If YES → correct

This diagnostic is **mechanical and reliable**. Apply it every time.

### 5.8.4 — Types of Dangling Modifiers

| Type | Example | Problem |
|---|---|---|
| **Participial (-ing phrase)** | "Running down the street, the bus was missed." | Bus wasn't running |
| **Past participial** | "Exhausted from work, the couch called." | Couch wasn't exhausted |
| **Prepositional acting as modifier** | "After years of practice, the piano was mastered." | Piano didn't practice |
| **Infinitive phrase** | "To succeed in business, hard work is required." | Hard work doesn't succeed in business |
| **Appositive-like** | "A talented musician, the audience applauded wildly." | Audience isn't the musician |

### 5.8.5 — How to Fix Dangling Modifiers

There are **two** valid approaches:

**Method 1: Change the subject of the main clause** to the logical performer of the action.
- ✗ "After reading the book, the movie was disappointing."
- ✓ "After reading the book, **I** found the movie disappointing."

**Method 2: Expand the modifier into a full dependent clause** with its own subject and verb.
- ✗ "After reading the book, the movie was disappointing."
- ✓ "**After I read the book**, the movie was disappointing."

**Method 3 (rare): Add the logical subject to the modifier itself.**
- ✗ "While driving to work, the accident was witnessed."
- ✓ "While **I was** driving to work, the accident was witnessed."

### 5.8.6 — The "Dangling Modifier" vs. "Correct Modifier" Distinction

Not every introductory phrase creates a dangling modifier. The key is whether the **subject of the main clause** can logically perform the action.

**Correct Examples:**
- "Walking to school, **I** saw a cardinal." (I was walking — correct)
- "Exhausted from the race, **she** collapsed on the bench." (She was exhausted — correct)
- "Having completed the project, **the team** celebrated." (The team completed — correct)

**Incorrect Examples:**
- "Walking to school, **a** cardinal was seen." (Cardinal wasn't walking to school — dangling)
- "Exhausted from the race, **the** bench was a welcome sight." (Bench wasn't exhausted — dangling)
- "Having completed the project, **the** celebration began." (Celebration didn't complete the project — dangling)

### 5.8.7 — Special Case: "Upon" and "On" + Gerund

Phrases beginning with "upon" or "on" followed by a -ing word create a similar structure:

- ✗ "**Upon entering the room**, the lights were blinding." (Lights didn't enter the room.)
- ✓ "**Upon entering the room, I** was blinded by the lights."

- ✗ "**On hearing the news**, the decision was made." (Decision didn't hear news.)
- ✓ "**On hearing the news, she** made the decision."

### 5.8.8 — Special Case: "When" + Past Participle

Reduced adverb clauses with "when" + past participle can also dangle:

- ✗ "**When heated to 100°C**, the chemist observed the reaction." (The chemist wasn't heated.)
- ✓ "**When heated to 100°C**, the **substance** reacted." (The substance was heated — correct.)
- ✓ "**When the substance was heated to 100°C**, the chemist observed the reaction." (Full clause — correct.)

---

## 5.9 — MISPLACED MODIFIERS

### 5.9.1 — The Fundamental Rule

A modifier must be placed **as close as possible** to the word or phrase it modifies. When a modifier is placed too far from its target, or when it sits between two possible targets, the meaning becomes unclear or absurd.

### 5.9.2 — Classic Misplaced Modifier Examples

**Example 1:**
> "The dog was adopted by the family with the brown spots."

*Problem:* "With the brown spots" is next to "the family," implying the family has brown spots. It should modify "the dog."

*Correction:*
> "The dog **with the brown spots** was adopted by the family."

**Example 2:**
> "She served sandwiches to the children **on paper plates**."

*Problem:* "On paper plates" is next to "the children," implying the children were on paper plates.

*Correction:*
> "She served the children sandwiches **on paper plates**."

**Example 3:**
> "The professor explained the theory **using a diagram** to the students."

*Problem:* "Using a diagram" is awkwardly placed. Who used the diagram? The professor or the theory?

*Correction:*
> "**Using a diagram**, the professor explained the theory to the students."
> OR
> "The professor **used a diagram** to explain the theory to the students."

### 5.9.3 — Limiting Modifiers: The Most Critical Subcategory

Certain modifiers are called **limiting modifiers** because they restrict or limit the meaning of the word they modify. These must be placed **immediately before** the word they modify:

| Limiting Modifiers |
|---|
| only, almost, nearly, just, even, hardly, scarcely, merely, simply, exactly, precisely, especially, particularly, chiefly, mainly, solely, solely |

**The position of "only" changes the entire meaning of a sentence:**

- "**Only** I ate the cake." (No one else ate it; I was the sole eater.)
- "I **only** ate the cake." (I only ate it — I didn't bake it, buy it, or serve it.)
- "I ate **only** the cake." (I ate nothing but the cake.)
- "I ate the **only** cake." (There was one cake, and I ate it.)

**SAT Test:**
> "The company **only** manufactures parts for automobiles."

This means the company does nothing *except* manufacture parts — it doesn't design, sell, or distribute. If the intended meaning is that the company makes parts *exclusively for automobiles* (not for trucks or motorcycles), the correct version is:

> "The company manufactures parts **only for automobiles**."

**The Rule:** Place "only" (and its siblings) **immediately before the word or phrase it modifies.**

### 5.9.4 — Misplaced Participial Phrases

Participial phrases (-ing or -ed phrases) that modify a noun must be placed next to that noun:

- ✗ "Covered in frosting, **I** ate the cake." (I am not covered in frosting.)
- ✓ "I ate the cake, **covered in frosting**." (The cake is covered in frosting.)

- ✗ "Running late, **the** bus was missed." (The bus wasn't running late.)
- ✓ "**Running late, I** missed the bus." (I was running late.)

### 5.9.5 — Misplaced Adverbs

Adverbs like *almost, nearly, just, hardly* must be carefully positioned:

- ✗ "I **almost** ate the whole pizza." (Ambiguous — did you almost start eating it?)
- ✓ "I ate **almost** the whole pizza." (Clear — you ate nearly all of it.)

- ✗ "She **nearly** drove her kids to school every day." (Did she almost drive them?)
- ✓ "She drove her kids to school **nearly** every day." (Clear — almost every day.)

### 5.9.6 — The "Squinting Modifier" (Ambiguous Modifier)

A squinting modifier is one that could modify **either** the word before it **or** the word after it, creating genuine ambiguity:

> "Students who study **frequently** get good grades."

Does "frequently" modify "study" (students who study often) or "get" (students who often get good grades)?

*Corrections:*
- "Students who **frequently** study get good grades." (frequently modifies study)
- "Students who study get good grades **frequently**." (frequently modifies get)

The SAT tests squinting modifiers less often than dangling and misplaced ones, but the principle is the same: **a modifier must have one clear target.**

---

## 5.10 — ADJECTIVE VS. ADVERB ERRORS

### 5.10.1 — The Basic Distinction

| Part of Speech | Modifies | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Adjective** | Nouns and pronouns | "a **quick** decision," "She is **tall**." |
| **Adverb** | Verbs, adjectives, other adverbs | "She ran **quickly**," "She is **extremely** tall." |

Most adverbs are formed by adding **-ly** to an adjective: quick → quickly, careful → carefully, happy → happily.

### 5.10.2 — The Linking Verb Trap

After **linking verbs** (be, seem, appear, become, remain, feel, look, sound, smell, taste, grow, stay, turn, prove), use an **adjective** (not an adverb) because the word describes the **subject**, not the action:

- ✓ "She **feels bad**." (Describes her state — adjective)
- ✗ "She **feels badly**." (Implies her sense of touch is impaired — adverb)

- ✓ "The soup **tastes delicious**." (Describes the soup — adjective)
- ✗ "The soup **tastes deliciously**." (Implies the soup is tasting something — adverb)

- ✓ "He **looks happy**." (Describes him — adjective)
- ✗ "He **looks happily**." (Implies he is looking at something in a happy manner — adverb)

**Exception:** If the verb is being used as an **action verb** (not a linking verb), use an adverb:

- "She **looked** at him **sadly**." (Action: she performed the act of looking in a sad manner — adverb)
- "She **looked sad**." (Linking: her appearance was sad — adjective)

### 5.10.3 — Flat Adverbs (Adverbs Without -ly)

Some adverbs have the same form as their adjective counterparts. The SAT occasionally tests these:

| Flat Adverb | Example |
|---|---|
| fast | "He runs **fast**." (not "fastly") |
| hard | "She works **hard**." (not "hardly" — which means "barely") |
| late | "He arrived **late**." (not "lately" — which means "recently") |
| early | "She woke up **early**." |
| daily | "The newspaper is delivered **daily**." |
| well | "She sings **well**." (adverb of "good") |

**Critical Distinction:**
- **Hard** (adverb) = with great effort: "She works hard."
- **Hardly** (adverb) = barely, scarcely: "She hardly works." (almost doesn't work at all)
- **Late** (adverb) = after the expected time: "He arrived late."
- **Lately** (adverb) = recently: "He has been tired lately."

### 5.10.4 — Good vs. Well

- **Good** is an adjective: "She is a **good** singer." (modifies "singer")
- **Well** is an adverb: "She sings **well**." (modifies "sings")
- **Well** can also be an adjective when referring to health: "I feel **well**." (healthy)

The SAT tests this distinction:
- ✗ "She sings **good**." (wrong — "good" is an adjective, cannot modify a verb)
- ✓ "She sings **well**." (correct — adverb modifying verb)

---

## 5.11 — COMPARATIVE AND SUPERLATIVE FORMS

### 5.11.1 — The Rules

| Form | Used For | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Positive** | No comparison | tall, beautiful, quickly |
| **Comparative** | Comparing **two** items | taller, more beautiful, more quickly |
| **Superlative** | Comparing **three or more** items | tallest, most beautiful, most quickly |

**Formation:**
- One-syllable adjectives: add -er / -est (tall → taller → tallest)
- Two-syllable adjectives ending in -y: change y to i, add -er / -est (happy → happier → happiest)
- Most other two-syllable adjectives and all three-syllable adjectives: use more / most (beautiful → more beautiful → most beautiful)
- Adverbs of two or more syllables: use more / most (quickly → more quickly → most quickly)

### 5.11.2 — Common Errors

**Double comparatives/superlatives:**
- ✗ "She is **more taller** than her sister."
- ✓ "She is **taller** than her sister."
- ✗ "This is the **most best** solution."
- ✓ "This is the **best** solution."

**Using superlative for two items:**
- ✗ "She is the **taller** of the two sisters." (Should be comparative: "taller")
- ✓ "She is the **tallest** of the three sisters." (Superlative for three or more)

### 5.11.3 — Irregular Forms

| Positive | Comparative | Superlative |
|---|---|---|
| good | better | best |
| well | better | best |
| bad | worse | worst |
| badly | worse | worst |
| little | less | least |
| much | more | most |
| many | more | most |
| far | farther/further | farthest/furthest |

---

## 5.12 — PARALLEL STRUCTURE WITH MODIFIERS AND PRONOUNS

### 5.12.1 — Parallelism in Lists

Items in a list must share the same grammatical form:

- ✗ "She likes **swimming**, **to run**, and **biking**." (mixed forms)
- ✓ "She likes **swimming**, **running**, and **biking**." (all gerunds)
- ✓ "She likes **to swim**, **to run**, and **to bike**." (all infinitives)

### 5.12.2 — Parallelism with Correlative Conjunctions

The structures following each part of a correlative conjunction pair must be parallel:

| Pair | Example |
|---|---|
| either...or | "She will **either call** or **text**." |
| neither...nor | "He **neither called** nor **texted**." |
| not only...but also | "She is **not only smart** but also **hardworking**." |
| both...and | "He is **both intelligent** and **kind**." |
| whether...or | "I don't know **whether to stay** or **to leave**." |

**The Test:** Whatever grammatical form follows the first word must also follow the second word.

- ✗ "She is **not only talented** but also **works hard**." (adjective + verb phrase — not parallel)
- ✓ "She is **not only talented** but also **diligent**." (adjective + adjective — parallel)
- ✓ "She **not only sings** but also **dances**." (verb + verb — parallel)

### 5.12.3 — Parallelism in Comparisons

When comparing two things, the items being compared must be grammatically parallel:

- ✗ "Running a marathon is harder than **to swim** a mile." (gerund vs. infinitive)
- ✓ "Running a marathon is harder than **swimming** a mile." (gerund vs. gerund)
- ✓ "**To run** a marathon is harder than **to swim** a mile." (infinitive vs. infinitive)

- ✗ "Her salary is higher than **a teacher**." (salary compared to a person — illogical)
- ✓ "Her salary is higher than **a teacher's**." (salary compared to salary — logical and parallel)

---

## 5.13 — COMMONLY CONFUSED WORDS (DICTION)

The SAT tests word choice extensively. The following pairs and groups are among the most frequently tested. Each distinction must be understood at a deep level, not merely memorized.

### 5.13.1 — Affect vs. Effect

| Word | Part of Speech | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Affect** | Verb (most common) | To influence or produce a change in | "The weather **affects** my mood." |
| **Effect** | Noun (most common) | A result or outcome | "The **effect** of the storm was devastating." |

**Less Common Uses:**
- **Effect** as a verb: "to bring about" or "to cause to happen" — "The new policy will **effect** change." (This is formal and less common but correct.)
- **Affect** as a noun: In psychology, pronounced with stress on the first syllable, meaning "emotional expression" — "The patient displayed flat **affect**." (Rare on the SAT.)

**The SAT Test:** 95% of the time, **affect = verb** and **effect = noun**. If you need a verb, choose "affect." If you need a noun, choose "effect."

### 5.13.2 — Than vs. Then

| Word | Function | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Than** | Conjunction used in comparisons | "She is taller **than** I am." |
| **Then** | Adverb indicating time or sequence | "We ate, and **then** we left." |

**The SAT Test:** If the sentence involves a comparison (taller, more, less, better, etc.), use **than**. If the sentence involves time or sequence (next, after that, subsequently), use **then**.

- ✗ "She is taller **then** her brother."
- ✓ "She is taller **than** her brother."

### 5.13.3 — Allusion vs. Illusion

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Allusion** | An indirect reference to something | "The poem contains an **allusion** to Shakespeare." |
| **Illusion** | A false perception or deceptive appearance | "The magician created an **illusion** of a floating ball." |

### 5.13.4 — Elicit vs. Illicit

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Elicit** (verb) | To draw out or evoke a response | "The question **elicited** a strong reaction." |
| **Illicit** (adjective) | Illegal or forbidden | "The police discovered an **illicit** operation." |

### 5.13.5 — Accept vs. Except

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Accept** (verb) | To receive or agree to | "She **accepted** the award." |
| **Except** (preposition/conjunction) | Excluding, other than | "Everyone came **except** Tom." |

### 5.13.6 — Advice vs. Advise

| Word | Part of Speech | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Advice** | Noun | "She gave me good **advice**." |
| **Advise** | Verb | "I **advise** you to study." |

### 5.13.7 — Allude vs. Elude

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Allude** | To refer to indirectly | "He **alluded** to the scandal without naming names." |
| **Elude** | To escape or avoid | "The suspect **eluded** the police." |

### 5.13.8 — Altar vs. Alter

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Altar** | A sacred table or platform | "They were married at the **altar**." |
| **Alter** | To change or modify | "She **altered** her dress." |

### 5.13.9 — Capital vs. Capitol

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Capital** | A city serving as a seat of government; also wealth or uppercase letter | "Washington, D.C. is the **capital**." |
| **Capitol** | The building where a legislature meets | "The **Capitol** building has a dome." |

### 5.13.10 — Cite vs. Site vs. Sight

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Cite** | To quote or reference as evidence | "Please **cite** your sources." |
| **Site** | A location or place | "The construction **site** was busy." |
| **Sight** | Vision, something seen | "The view was a beautiful **sight**." |

### 5.13.11 — Complement vs. Compliment

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Complement** | Something that completes or goes well with | "The wine **complemented** the meal." |
| **Compliment** | An expression of praise | "She paid me a **compliment**." |

**Memory Trick:** An "e" in compl**e**ment = compl**e**te (both have "e"). An "i" in compl**i**ment = I like getting compl**i**ments (both have "i").

### 5.13.12 — Council vs. Counsel

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Council** | A group or assembly | "The city **council** voted on the issue." |
| **Counsel** | Advice; a lawyer; to advise | "She sought legal **counsel**." |

### 5.13.13 — Discrete vs. Discreet

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Discrete** | Separate, distinct | "The report is divided into **discrete** sections." |
| **Discreet** | Careful, prudent, tactful | "Please be **discreet** about this matter." |

### 5.13.14 — Eminent vs. Imminent

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Eminent** | Famous, distinguished, prominent | "An **eminent** scholar gave the lecture." |
| **Imminent** | About to happen, impending | "A storm is **imminent**." |

### 5.13.15 — Explicit vs. Implicit

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Explicit** | Stated directly, clearly expressed | "The instructions were **explicit**." |
| **Implicit** | Implied, understood though not directly stated | "Her silence carried an **implicit** agreement." |

### 5.13.16 — Farther vs. Further

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Farther** | Physical distance | "She ran **farther** than I did." |
| **Further** | Figurative degree or extent | "Let's discuss this **further**." |

### 5.13.17 — Fewer vs. Less

| Word | Modifies | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Fewer** | Countable (plural) nouns | "There are **fewer** students this year." |
| **Less** | Uncountable (singular) nouns | "There is **less** water in the pool." |

**The SAT Test:** This is one of the most commonly tested distinctions. Countable items (books, people, dollars, miles) take **fewer**. Uncountable quantities (money, time, water, effort) take **less**.

- ✗ "I have **less** books than you."
- ✓ "I have **fewer** books than you."
- ✓ "I have **less** money than you." (money is uncountable)

### 5.13.18 — Imply vs. Infer

| Word | Meaning | Who Does It | Example |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Imply** | To suggest indirectly | The speaker/writer | "She **implied** that she was unhappy." |
| **Infer** | To conclude based on evidence | The listener/reader | "I **inferred** from her tone that she was upset." |

**Memory Trick:** The speaker **impl**ies; the listener **infer**s. Both have the same vowel: **impl-y, infer**.

### 5.13.19 — Its vs. It's

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Its** | Possessive of "it" | "The dog wagged **its** tail." |
| **It's** | Contraction of "it is" or "it has" | "**It's** raining." / "**It's** been a long day." |

**The Substitution Test:** Replace with "it is." If the sentence still makes sense, use **it's**. If not, use **its**.

### 5.13.20 — Lie vs. Lay

| Word | Meaning | Present | Past | Past Participle |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| **Lie** | To recline or rest | lie | lay | lain |
| **Lay** | To put or place something | lay | laid | laid |

- "I **lie** down every afternoon." (recline)
- "Yesterday I **lay** down." (past of lie)
- "I have **lain** down." (past participle of lie)
- "I **lay** the book on the table." (put something down)
- "Yesterday I **laid** the book on the table." (past of lay)
- "I have **laid** the book on the table." (past participle of lay)

**The Key:** "Lay" requires a direct object (you lay *something* down). "Lie" does not (you just lie down).

### 5.13.21 — Principal vs. Principle

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Principal** | Main, most important; the head of a school | "The **principal** reason was cost." / "The **principal** addressed the students." |
| **Principle** | A rule, law, or standard | "She lives by strong moral **principles**." |

**Memory Trick:** The princip**al** is your p**al**. A princip**le** is a ru**le** (both end in -le).

### 5.13.22 — Stationary vs. Stationery

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Stationary** | Not moving, fixed in place | "The car remained **stationary**." |
| **Stationery** | Writing materials (paper, envelopes) | "She wrote the letter on elegant **stationery**." |

**Memory Trick:** st**a**tion**a**ry = st**a**nding still (both have "a"). st**a**tion**e**ry = l**e**tt**e**rs (both have "e").

### 5.13.23 — Their vs. There vs. They're

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Their** | Possessive of "they" | "**Their** house is on the corner." |
| **There** | A place; also an expletive | "Over **there**." / "**There** are three options." |
| **They're** | Contraction of "they are" | "**They're** coming to dinner." |

### 5.13.24 — Who's vs. Whose

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Who's** | Contraction of "who is" or "who has" | "**Who's** at the door?" |
| **Whose** | Possessive of "who" | "**Whose** jacket is this?" |

### 5.13.25 — Your vs. You're

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Your** | Possessive of "you" | "**Your** car is ready." |
| **You're** | Contraction of "you are" | "**You're** going to do well." |

### 5.13.26 — Ensure vs. Insure

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Ensure** | To make certain or guarantee | "Please **ensure** that the door is locked." |
| **Insure** | To provide insurance | "I need to **insure** my car." |

### 5.13.27 — Precede vs. Proceed

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Precede** | To come before | "Thunder **precedes** lightning." |
| **Proceed** | To continue or move forward | "Please **proceed** with the presentation." |

### 5.13.28 — Than vs. Then (Revisited with Nuance)

Beyond the basic distinction, the SAT sometimes tests "then" in the sense of "in that case" or "therefore":

- "If you're tired, **then** you should rest." (then = in that case)
- "She studied hard, **then** took a break." (then = next, subsequently)

### 5.13.29 — Beside vs. Besides

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Beside** | Next to, at the side of | "She sat **beside** me." |
| **Besides** | In addition to, moreover | "**Besides** math, she studies science." |

### 5.13.30 — Uninterested vs. Disinterested

| Word | Meaning | Example |
|---|---|---|
| **Uninterested** | Bored, not interested | "He was **uninterested** in the lecture." |
| **Disinterested** | Impartial, unbiased | "A **disinterested** judge presided over the case." |

This is a subtle but tested distinction. Many people use "disinterested" to mean "uninterested," but they are not synonyms. A disinterested party is one with no stake in the outcome — an impartial observer.

---

## 5.14 — THE SAT'S APPROACH TO PRONOUN AND MODIFIER QUESTIONS

### 5.14.1 — How the SAT Constructs Pronoun Questions

The SAT typically tests pronouns in the following ways:

1. **Agreement errors:** The pronoun doesn't match its antecedent in number.
   - Trap: A plural noun near the verb tricks you into using a plural pronoun when the true antecedent is singular.

2. **Ambiguity errors:** The pronoun could refer to more than one antecedent.
   - Trap: The "obvious" reading is the wrong one; the correct answer replaces the pronoun with a specific noun.

3. **Case errors:** Subject pronouns used as objects or vice versa.
   - Trap: Compound constructions ("between you and I") or comparisons ("than me" vs. "than I").

4. **Missing antecedent:** The pronoun has no clear noun to refer to.
   - Trap: "It" used vaguely ("It says in the book...").

5. **Reflexive misuse:** "Myself" used instead of "I" or "me."
   - Trap: People use "myself" to sound formal or avoid choosing between "I" and "me."

### 5.14.2 — How the SAT Constructs Modifier Questions

1. **Dangling modifiers:** The introductory phrase doesn't match the subject.
   - Trap: The sentence "sounds fine" because your brain supplies the missing logical subject.

2. **Misplaced modifiers:** A phrase is too far from what it modifies.
   - Trap: The sentence seems to make sense on first reading, but the literal meaning is absurd.

3. **Limiting modifier errors:** "Only," "almost," "nearly" are in the wrong position.
   - Trap: The sentence is grammatically correct but changes meaning based on modifier placement.

4. **Adjective/adverb confusion:** An adjective is used where an adverb is needed (or vice versa).
   - Trap: Linking verbs (feel, seem, look, taste) take adjectives, not adverbs.

### 5.14.3 — The Process for Solving Pronoun Questions

**Step 1:** Identify the pronoun in question.
**Step 2:** Find its antecedent (the noun it replaces).
**Step 3:** Check agreement (singular/plural).
**Step 4:** Check clarity (is the antecedent unambiguous?).
**Step 5:** Check case (subject vs. object).
**Step 6:** If the pronoun is "who/whom," apply the he/him test within the clause.
**Step 7:** Eliminate choices that violate any of these rules.

### 5.14.4 — The Process for Solving Modifier Questions

**Step 1:** Identify the modifier (introductory phrase, participial phrase, limiting word, etc.).
**Step 2:** Determine what the modifier is supposed to describe.
**Step 3:** Check placement — is the modifier next to its target?
**Step 4:** If the modifier is introductory, check whether the subject after the comma can logically perform the action.
**Step 5:** If the modifier is a limiting word (only, almost, nearly), check that it's immediately before the word it modifies.
**Step 6:** Eliminate choices that violate any of these rules.

---

## 5.15 — ADVANCED PRONOUN CONCEPTS

### 5.15.1 — The "One" Pronoun

When "one" is used as a pronoun (meaning "a person" or "any person"), subsequent references to it should also use "one":

- ✓ "If **one** studies hard, **one** will succeed."
- ✗ "If **one** studies hard, **you** will succeed." (inconsistent pronoun shift)
- ✗ "If **one** studies hard, **they** will succeed." (number disagreement)

In British English, "one...one...one" is standard. In American English, the SAT accepts this pattern but also allows "you" if used consistently. The key is **consistency** — don't shift from "one" to "you" to "they" in the same sentence.

### 5.15.2 — The "You" Shift

Starting a sentence with "you" and then shifting to "one" or "people" is an error:

- ✗ "**You** should always be prepared, because **one** never knows what might happen."
- ✓ "**You** should always be prepared, because **you** never know what might happen."
- ✓ "**One** should always be prepared, because **one** never knows what might happen."

### 5.15.3 — The "We" vs. "Us" Before a Noun

When a pronoun appears before a noun that it's associated with, the pronoun should be in the same case it would be in if the noun were removed:

- ✗ "**Us** students need more time." (Would you say "Us need more time"?)
- ✓ "**We** students need more time." (We need more time — correct.)

- ✗ "The prize was given to **we** winners." (Would you say "given to we"?)
- ✓ "The prize was given to **us** winners." (Given to us — correct.)

### 5.15.4 — Pronouns After "Let"

"Let" takes an object pronoun:

- ✓ "Let **him** try." (not "Let he try.")
- ✓ "Let **us** go." (not "Let we go.")
- ✓ "Let **them** eat cake." (not "Let they eat cake.")

The structure is: **Let + object pronoun + base verb.**

### 5.15.5 — The "It" Expletive vs. Referential "It"

**Expletive "it"** has no antecedent — it's a placeholder subject:
- "**It** is raining."
- "**It** is important to study."
- "**It** seems that she is correct."

**Referential "it"** refers to a specific noun:
- "I bought a new car, and **it** is fast." (it = the car)

The SAT tests whether "it" is being used as a referential pronoun without a clear antecedent. If "it" doesn't point to a specific noun, the sentence may be incorrect.

---

## 5.16 — ADVANCED MODIFIER CONCEPTS

### 5.16.1 — Absolute Phrases

An absolute phrase modifies an entire sentence rather than a single noun. It consists of a noun + participle (and sometimes additional modifiers), and it is set off by a comma:

- "**The game having ended**, the crowd dispersed." (The entire situation — the game ending — is the context for the crowd dispersing.)
- "**Her work finished**, she went home."

Absolute phrases are **not** dangling modifiers because they don't modify a single noun — they modify the whole clause. The SAT occasionally tests whether students can distinguish a correct absolute phrase from a dangling modifier.

**Key Distinction:**
- Dangling modifier: The introductory phrase implies a subject that doesn't match the main clause subject.
- Absolute phrase: The introductory phrase has its own subject and modifies the entire situation.

### 5.16.2 — Participial Phrases at the End of a Sentence

When a participial phrase appears at the end of a sentence, it is usually separated by a comma and modifies the subject of the main clause:

- "She walked across the stage, **smiling at the audience**." (She was smiling — correct.)
- "The dog ran through the yard, **chasing the ball**." (The dog was chasing — correct.)

These are generally correct because the subject of the main clause is the logical performer of the participial action. The SAT tests this by placing the participial phrase near a different noun:

- ✗ "She crossed the finish line, **exhausted from the race**, the crowd cheering." (This is actually fine — "exhausted" modifies "she," and "cheering" is part of an absolute phrase.)

### 5.16.3 — Adjective Clauses vs. Adverb Clauses

**Adjective clauses** modify nouns and begin with relative pronouns (who, whom, whose, which, that, where, when):

- "The book **that I borrowed** was fascinating." (modifies "book")

**Adverb clauses** modify verbs, adjectives, or other adverbs and begin with subordinating conjunctions (because, although, when, while, if, since, after, before, unless, until, whereas):

- "**Because she studied hard**, she passed the test." (modifies the entire main clause — explains why)

The SAT tests whether the correct connector is used for the intended relationship. Using "which" where "where" is needed (or vice versa) is a common error:

- ✗ "This is the city **which** I was born." (Should be "where" — modifies a place)
- ✓ "This is the city **where** I was born."
- ✓ "This is the city **in which** I was born." (formal but correct)

### 5.16.4 — The "That" vs. "Where" Distinction

- **"That"** introduces a relative clause modifying a noun: "The house **that I bought** is old."
- **"Where"** introduces a relative clause modifying a place: "The house **where I grew up** is old."

Using "that" for a place (without a preposition) is incorrect:
- ✗ "The city **that** I was born is beautiful."
- ✓ "The city **where** I was born is beautiful."
- ✓ "The city **in which** I was born is beautiful."

### 5.16.5 — The "When" vs. "Where" Distinction

- **"When"** modifies a time: "I remember the day **when we met**."
- **"Where"** modifies a place: "I remember the place **where we met**."

Using "when" for a place or "where" for a time is incorrect:
- ✗ "The year **where** the war ended was 1945."
- ✓ "The year **when** the war ended was 1945."

---

## 5.17 — THE INTERSECTION OF PRONOUNS AND MODIFIERS

### 5.17.1 — Relative Pronouns as Modifier Connectors

Relative pronouns (who, whom, which, that, where, when) serve a dual function: they are pronouns (standing in for nouns) AND they are subordinating connectors (introducing dependent clauses that modify nouns). This dual role makes them a frequent testing ground for both pronoun rules and modifier rules simultaneously.

**Example:**
> "The scientist ___ discovered the vaccine was awarded a Nobel Prize."

- The blank introduces a dependent clause that modifies "scientist."
- Within that clause, the blank is the subject of "discovered."
- Subject → who
- ✓ "The scientist **who** discovered the vaccine was awarded a Nobel Prize."

### 5.17.2 — Pronoun Reference in Modified Sentences

When a sentence contains multiple modifiers, the pronoun's antecedent can become obscured. The SAT tests whether the pronoun still has a clear reference despite intervening clauses:

- ✗ "The report, **which was submitted to the committee and the board**, was praised for **its** clarity, but **they** requested revisions." (Who is "they"? The committee? The board? Both? Ambiguous.)
- ✓ "The report, **which was submitted to the committee and the board**, was praised for **its** clarity, but **the committee** requested revisions."

### 5.17.3 — Dangling Modifiers with Pronouns

Sometimes a dangling modifier involves a pronoun that has no antecedent:

- ✗ "**After finishing the report**, **it** was submitted immediately." (Who finished the report? "It" has no antecedent — the person who finished it is never mentioned.)
- ✓ "**After finishing the report, I** submitted it immediately."

This combines the dangling modifier error (no logical subject) with the missing antecedent error (no noun for "it" to refer to).

---

## 5.18 — COMPREHENSIVE DICTION REFERENCE

### 5.18.1 — Words That Sound Alike but Have Different Meanings (Homophones and Near-Homophones)

| Pair/Group | Distinction |
|---|---|
| **accept/except** | accept = receive; except = excluding |
| **affect/effect** | affect = verb (influence); effect = noun (result) |
| **allude/elude** | allude = refer to; elude = escape |
| **allusion/illusion** | allusion = reference; illusion = false perception |
| **altar/alter** | altar = sacred platform; alter = change |
| **ascent/assent** | ascent = climb; assent = agreement |
| **beside/besides** | beside = next to; besides = in addition to |
| **brake/break** | brake = stop; break = shatter |
| **breath/breathe** | breath = noun; breathe = verb |
| **canvas/canvass** | canvas = fabric; canvass = survey |
| **capital/capitol** | capital = city/wealth; capitol = building |
| **cite/site/sight** | cite = quote; site = location; sight = vision |
| **climactic/climatic** | climactic = peak/tension; climatic = weather-related |
| **complement/compliment** | complement = complete; compliment = praise |
| **conscience/conscious** | conscience = moral sense; conscious = aware |
| **continual/continuous** | continual = repeated; continuous = unbroken |
| **council/counsel** | council = group; counsel = advice |
| **desert/dessert** | desert = arid land; dessert = sweet course |
| **discreet/discrete** | discreet = tactful; discrete = separate |
| **emigrate/immigrate** | emigrate = leave a country; immigrate = enter a country |
| **eminent/imminent** | eminent = famous; imminent = about to happen |
| **ensure/insure** | ensure = guarantee; insure = provide insurance |
| **everyday/every day** | everyday = adjective (ordinary); every day = each day |
| **farther/further** | farther = physical distance; further = figurative extent |
| **fewer/less** | fewer = countable; less = uncountable |
| **imply/infer** | imply = suggest; infer = conclude |
| **its/it's** | its = possessive; it's = it is |
| **later/latter** | later = after; latter = second of two |
| **lay/lie** | lay = put down; lie = recline |
| **lead/led** | lead = present tense (to guide); led = past tense |
| **loose/lose** | loose = not tight; lose = misplace |
| **passed/past** | passed = went by; past = previous time |
| **peace/piece** | peace = calm; piece = part |
| **personal/personnel** | personal = private; personnel = staff |
| **precede/proceed** | precede = come before; proceed = continue |
| **principal/principle** | principal = main; principle = rule |
| **stationary/stationery** | stationary = still; stationery = paper |
| **than/then** | than = comparison; then = time |
| **their/there/they're** | their = possessive; there = place; they're = they are |
| **to/too/two** | to = preposition; too = also/excessive; two = number |
| **weather/whether** | weather = climate; whether = if |
| **who's/whose** | who's = who is; whose = possessive |
| **your/you're** | your = possessive; you're = you are |

### 5.18.2 — Words with Similar Meanings but Different Usage

| Pair | Distinction |
|---|---|
| **amount/number** | amount = uncountable; number = countable |
| **among/between** | among = three or more; between = two |
| **bring/take** | bring = toward the speaker; take = away from the speaker |
| **can/may** | can = ability; may = permission |
| **compare to/compare with** | compare to = liken; compare with = examine similarities/differences |
| **comprise/compose** | comprise = the whole includes the parts; compose = the parts make up the whole |
| **famous/infamous** | famous = well-known (positive); infamous = well-known (negative) |
| **farther/further** | farther = distance; further = degree |
| **fewer/less** | fewer = countable; less = uncountable |
| **hanged/hung** | hanged = executed; hung = suspended |
| **historic/historical** | historic = important; historical = related to history |
| **imply/infer** | imply = suggest; infer = conclude |
| **learn/teach** | learn = acquire knowledge; teach = impart knowledge |
| **lend/borrow** | lend = give temporarily; borrow = take temporarily |
| **lie/lay** | lie = recline; lay = put down |
| **may be/maybe** | may be = verb phrase; maybe = adverb (perhaps) |
| **precede/proceed** | precede = come before; proceed = continue |
| **raise/rise** | raise = lift something (transitive); rise = go up (intransitive) |
| **sit/set** | sit = be seated (transitive); set = place something (intransitive) |
| **than/then** | than = comparison; then = time |

### 5.18.3 — The "Among" vs. "Between" Rule

- **Between** is used for **two** items: "The choice is **between** tea and coffee."
- **Among** is used for **three or more** items: "The choice is **among** tea, coffee, juice, and water."

The SAT occasionally tests this, though it's less common than other distinctions.

### 5.18.4 — "Comprise" vs. "Compose"

- **Comprise** means "to include" or "to consist of." The whole **comprises** the parts.
  - ✓ "The United States **comprises** 50 states."
  - ✗ "The United States **is comprised of** 50 states." (This usage is common but traditionally considered incorrect on the SAT.)

- **Compose** means "to make up." The parts **compose** the whole.
  - ✓ "Fifty states **compose** the United States."
  - ✓ "The United States **is composed of** 50 states."

**Memory Trick:** The whole **comprises**; the parts **compose**. "Com**prise**" = "includes" (both start with vowels in the second syllable).

### 5.18.5 — "Raise" vs. "Rise"

- **Raise** is transitive (requires a direct object): "Please **raise** the flag." (You raise *something*.)
- **Rise** is intransitive (no direct object): "The sun **rises**." (The sun rises by itself.)

### 5.18.6 — "Sit" vs. "Set"

- **Sit** is intransitive: "Please **sit** down." (No object.)
- **Set** is transitive: "Please **set** the book down." (You set *something* down.)

### 5.18.7 — "Learn" vs. "Teach"

- **Learn** = to acquire knowledge: "I **learned** calculus."
- **Teach** = to impart knowledge: "She **taught** me calculus."

- ✗ "She **learned** me calculus."
- ✓ "She **taught** me calculus."

### 5.18.8 — "Lend" vs. "Borrow"

- **Lend** = to give temporarily: "Please **lend** me your pen."
- **Borrow** = to take temporarily: "May I **borrow** your pen?"

- ✗ "Please **borrow** me your pen."
- ✓ "Please **lend** me your pen."

### 5.18.9 — "Historic" vs. "Historical"

- **Historic** = important or famous in history: "A **historic** event."
- **Historical** = related to or concerned with history: "A **historical** novel." (A novel set in the past, not necessarily important.)

### 5.18.10 — "Famous" vs. "Infamous"

- **Famous** = widely known (positive or neutral): "A **famous** singer."
- **Infamous** = widely known for something bad: "An **infamous** criminal."

---

## 5.19 — SPECIAL PRONOUN AND MODIFIER CONSTRUCTIONS ON THE SAT

### 5.19.1 — The "Being" Problem

The word **"being"** is rarely correct on the SAT. It often appears in answer choices as a trap:

- ✗ "**Being** a good student, the test was easy." (Dangling + "being" is awkward.)
- ✓ "**As I was** a good student, the test was easy."
- ✓ "**Because I was** a good student, the test was easy."

When "being" appears as a participial connector, it almost always creates a dangling modifier or an unnecessarily wordy construction. The correct answer will typically use "because," "since," "as," or will restructure the sentence entirely.

### 5.19.2 — The "Having" Construction

"Having + past participle" is used to show that one action was completed before another:

- ✓ "**Having finished** the assignment, she went to bed." (She finished first, then went to bed.)
- ✓ "**Having studied** all night, he was exhausted." (He studied, then was exhausted.)

This construction is correct and commonly tested. It is the preferred way to show sequence when the subject of both actions is the same.

### 5.19.3 — The "If I Were" Subjunctive

For hypothetical or contrary-to-fact statements, use **"were"** (not "was"):

- ✓ "If I **were** rich, I would travel the world." (I am not rich — hypothetical.)
- ✓ "I wish I **were** taller." (I am not taller — contrary to fact.)
- ✗ "If I **was** rich, I would travel the world." (Incorrect in formal English.)

The subjunctive mood is tested on the SAT, and "If I were" is the most common form.

### 5.19.4 — The "That" After Certain Verbs

After verbs of demand, recommendation, request, or suggestion, the following clause uses the **base form of the verb** (subjunctive):

- "The teacher demanded that he **be** quiet." (not "was")
- "I suggest that she **study** more." (not "studies")
- "They requested that the report **be** revised." (not "was")

Common verbs triggering this pattern: demand, suggest, recommend, request, require, insist, ask, order, propose, advise, mandate, stipulate.

---

## 5.20 — SUMMARY OF KEY RULES AND STRATEGIES

### Pronoun Rules Summary

1. **Agreement:** Pronouns must agree in number with their antecedents.
2. **Clarity:** Every pronoun must have one clear, unambiguous antecedent.
3. **Case:** Subject pronouns for subjects; object pronouns for objects.
4. **Who/Whom:** Use the he/him test within the clause.
5. **Reflexive:** Only use -self pronouns when the antecedent is in the same clause.
6. **That/Which:** "That" for restrictive (no comma); "which" for nonrestrictive (with comma).
7. **Singular antecedents:** Everyone, each, either, neither, etc. take singular pronouns.
8. **Proximity rule:** With "or/nor," the pronoun agrees with the nearest antecedent.
9. **Demonstrative pronouns:** "This," "that," "these," "those" alone need clear antecedents; add a noun if needed.
10. **Avoid "being":** It's almost always wrong on the SAT.

### Modifier Rules Summary

1. **Dangling modifiers:** The subject after the comma must logically perform the action of the introductory phrase.
2. **Misplaced modifiers:** Place modifiers next to the words they modify.
3. **Limiting modifiers:** "Only," "almost," "nearly," "just" must be immediately before the word they modify.
4. **Adjective vs. adverb:** Adjectives modify nouns; adverbs modify verbs, adjectives, and other adverbs.
5. **Linking verbs:** Use adjectives (not adverbs) after linking verbs (be, seem, feel, look, taste, etc.).
6. **Absolute phrases:** Noun + participle modifying the entire sentence — not dangling.
7. **Participial phrases at the end:** Must modify the subject of the main clause.

### Diction Rules Summary

1. **Affect/Effect:** Affect = verb; effect = noun.
2. **Than/Then:** Than = comparison; then = time.
3. **Its/It's:** Its = possessive; it's = it is.
4. **Fewer/Less:** Fewer = countable; less = uncountable.
5. **Who's/Whose:** Who's = who is; whose = possessive.
6. **Imply/Infer:** Imply = suggest; infer = conclude.
7. **Lie/Lay:** Lie = recline (no object); lay = put down (needs object).
8. **Comprise/Compose:** The whole comprises; the parts compose.
9. **Ensure/Insure:** Ensure = guarantee; insure = provide insurance.
10. **Eminent/Imminent:** Eminent = famous; imminent = about to happen.

---

## 5.21 — THE COGNITIVE FRAMEWORK FOR MASTERY

To truly master pronouns, modifiers, and diction on the SAT, you must develop a **systematic internal checklist** that you apply automatically to every question. This checklist should become so ingrained that you apply it without conscious effort, freeing your mental resources for the harder questions.

**For every pronoun question, ask:**
- What is the antecedent?
- Is it singular or plural?
- Is it clear and unambiguous?
- Is the pronoun in the correct case?
- Is "who/whom" correct within its clause?

**For every modifier question, ask:**
- What does this modifier describe?
- Is it placed next to that word?
- If it's introductory, can the subject after the comma perform the action?
- Is "only/almost/nearly" in the right position?
- Is an adjective being used where an adverb is needed (or vice versa)?

**For every diction question, ask:**
- What is the meaning of each word choice in context?
- Does the word match the intended meaning?
- Is the word the correct part of speech for its role in the sentence?
- Is the word formal enough for standard written English?

This framework, applied consistently and rigorously, will transform pronoun, modifier, and diction questions from sources of uncertainty into reliable points on test day.

---

*This chapter has provided an exhaustive treatment of pronouns, modifiers, and diction as tested on the SAT. The rules, distinctions, and strategies outlined here represent the complete body of knowledge required to answer every question in this domain correctly. Mastery comes not from reading this material once but from applying it repeatedly to practice questions until the patterns become instinctive.*

---

---


# Chapter 6: Punctuation Master Reference — Commas, Semicolons, Colons, Dashes, and Apostrophes with Rules and Edge Cases

---

## Introduction to Punctuation on the SAT

Punctuation constitutes one of the most heavily tested categories within the Standard English Conventions domain of the SAT Writing and Language section. Understanding the precise, formal rules governing every mark of punctuation — commas, semicolons, colons, dashes, apostrophes, and even the nuances of quotation marks and end punctuation — is non-negotiable for achieving a top score. This chapter provides an exhaustive, microscopic dissection of every punctuation rule that appears on the SAT, including all edge cases, exceptions, and trap scenarios the test makers use to challenge students.

One of the most critical truths about SAT punctuation is this: **the SAT tests formal, rule-based writing conventions, not conversational or informal usage.** A sentence may "sound right" out loud but be fundamentally incorrect according to the formal rules the SAT enforces. Conversely, a construction that feels stiff or overly formal may in fact be the only grammatically correct option. Every rule in this chapter reflects the formal standard written English that the College Board expects.

---

## Part I: The Comma — The Most Tested and Most Misunderstood Punctuation Mark

The comma is the single most frequently tested punctuation mark on the SAT. It is also the mark most commonly misused by students. The reason is simple: commas serve multiple distinct grammatical functions, and each function has its own precise rule. Students who rely on intuition — "I put a comma where I would pause when speaking" — will consistently fall into trap answers. The SAT does not care where you would pause. The SAT cares about grammatical structure.

### 1.1 The Eight Core Comma Rules

The SAT tests eight primary comma rules. Mastery of these eight rules alone will resolve the vast majority of comma questions on the test.

---

#### Rule 1: The FANBOYS Comma — Joining Two Independent Clauses

**FANBOYS** is the acronym for the seven coordinating conjunctions: **For, And, Nor, But, Or, Yet, So**.

When a FANBOYS conjunction connects two independent clauses (complete sentences that could stand alone), a comma must precede the conjunction.

**Structure:** Independent Clause + **,** + FANBOYS + Independent Clause

**Examples:**
- "Ashish enjoys pickleball**,** **and** he plans to compete in a tournament next month."
- "The experiment yielded unexpected results**,** **so** the researchers revised their hypothesis."
- "Vishesh could not locate his water bottle**,** **nor** could he find his backpack."

**Critical Edge Case — The Compound Predicate Trap:**
When a single subject performs two verbs (a compound predicate), there are NOT two independent clauses. Therefore, NO comma is used before the FANBOYS.

- ✗ "Ashish enjoys pickleball**,** and tennis." (Incorrect — "Ashish" is the single subject; "enjoys pickleball and tennis" is a compound predicate, not two independent clauses.)
- ✓ "Ashish enjoys pickleball and tennis." (Correct — no comma needed.)

**How to Test:** Ask yourself whether there is a second subject after the FANBOYS conjunction. If there is a new subject (and a new verb), you have two independent clauses and need a comma. If the FANBOYS simply connects two verbs, two nouns, or two adjectives under a single subject, no comma is needed.

- "Tom wrote a song and performed it for his friends." (Single subject "Tom" with compound predicate — no comma.)
- "Tom wrote a song**,** and he performed it for his friends." (Two independent clauses with two subjects — comma required.)

---

#### Rule 2: The Introductory Comma Clause (ICC) Comma

When a sentence begins with a dependent clause, a participial phrase, a prepositional phrase functioning as an introductory element, or a transitional expression, a comma must follow the introductory element and precede the main clause.

**Structure:** Introductory Element + **,** + Main Clause

**Subtypes of Introductory Elements:**

**a) Subordinate (Dependent) Clauses:**
These begin with subordinating conjunctions such as *because, although, though, even though, while, when, whenever, where, wherever, if, unless, until, before, after, since, as, once, provided that, whereas, in order that, so that.*

- "**Because the storm intensified**, the outdoor concert was postponed."
- "**Although the data seemed contradictory**, the researchers identified a clear pattern."
- "**When the committee reconvenes next week**, they will vote on the proposal."

**b) Participial Phrases (often beginning with -ing or -ed words):**
- "**Walking through the museum**, the visitors admired the Renaissance paintings."
- "**Exhausted from the marathon**, the athlete collapsed at the finish line."
- "**Having completed the assignment**, the student submitted it early."

**c) Prepositional Phrases (when they function as true introductions to the sentence):**
- "**In the early morning hours**, the city was remarkably quiet."
- "**After a lengthy deliberation**, the jury reached a verdict."

**d) Transitional Expressions:**
- "**However**, the results were inconclusive."
- "**Furthermore**, the study revealed additional correlations."
- "**In fact**, the phenomenon had been documented decades earlier."

**Critical Edge Case — The Dangling Modifier Connection:**
The comma after an introductory element is not merely a punctuation formality — it is structurally essential. The noun or pronoun that immediately follows the comma is understood to be the logical subject performing the action described in the introductory phrase. If the wrong noun follows the comma, a dangling modifier results (covered in detail in Chapter 5 on Modifiers).

- ✗ "Walking through the museum**, the Renaissance paintings were admired." (The paintings cannot walk — dangling modifier.)
- ✓ "Walking through the museum**, the visitors admired the Renaissance paintings." (The visitors are walking — correct.)

---

#### Rule 3: The Non-Essential (Nonrestrictive) Clause Comma — Setting Off Extra Information

When a clause, phrase, or word provides additional, non-essential information — information that could be removed without changing the core meaning of the sentence — it must be set off by commas.

**Test for Essential vs. Non-Essential:** Remove the clause in question. If the sentence still identifies the same person, thing, or idea and retains its core meaning, the clause is non-essential and requires commas. If removing the clause changes the meaning or makes identification impossible, the clause is essential and should NOT have commas.

**Examples:**
- "Tom**,** who is usually very uncreative**,** wrote a beautiful song." (The clause "who is usually very uncreative" is extra information about Tom. We already know who Tom is. The clause can be removed: "Tom wrote a beautiful song." Core meaning unchanged → non-essential → commas required.)
- "The student **who scored the highest on the exam** received a scholarship." (Here, "who scored the highest on the exam" is essential — it tells us WHICH student. Without it: "The student received a scholarship" — which student? We no longer know. → essential → NO commas.)

**The "That" vs. "Which" Distinction:**
On the SAT, **"that" introduces essential clauses (no comma)** and **"which" introduces non-essential clauses (comma required)**. This is one of the most consistent rules on the test.

- "The car **that** I bought last week is already in the shop." (Essential — specifies which car.)
- "The car**, which** I bought last week**,** is already in the shop." (Non-essential — we already know which car; the clause adds extra information.)

**Note:** In British English and some informal American writing, "which" is used for both essential and non-essential clauses. On the SAT, this distinction is strictly enforced.

---

#### Rule 4: The Oxford Comma — Separating Items in a Series

When listing three or more items, a comma should separate each item. The comma before the final "and" or "or" (before the last item) is called the **Oxford comma** (also known as the serial comma).

**Structure:** Item 1 + **,** + Item 2 + **,** + and/or + Item 3

**Example:**
- "Ashish enjoys pickleball**,** tennis**,** football**,** and baseball."

**SAT Treatment of the Oxford Comma:**
The SAT consistently uses the Oxford comma in its correct answer choices. The test will never penalize you for including it, and in certain cases, the Oxford comma is necessary to avoid ambiguity.

**Ambiguity Case:**
- "I admire my parents, Batman and Wonder Woman." (Without the Oxford comma, this reads as if your parents ARE Batman and Wonder Woman.)
- "I admire my parents**,** Batman**,** and Wonder Woman." (With the Oxford comma, it is clear these are four separate entities.)

---

#### Rule 5: The Coordinate Adjective Comma

When two or more adjectives independently modify the same noun (i.e., each adjective separately describes the noun), a comma separates them. These are called **coordinate adjectives**.

**The Two-Part Test for Coordinate Adjectives:**
1. Can you insert "and" between the adjectives and the sentence still makes sense?
2. Can you reverse the order of the adjectives and the sentence still makes sense?

If the answer to BOTH questions is yes, the adjectives are coordinate, and a comma is required.

**Examples:**
